Why This Section on the Path Comes After Inquiry
Raises and answers an objection about delaying this section.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the methodological reasoning behind placing the discussion of the 'path' after the discussion of 'inquiry' in kalam works.

Core Idea
In theological method, order is determined by dependency, not by natural sequence.

In classical kalam texts, the ordering of topics is not arbitrary. Here, the author anticipates a possible objection: why discuss the path (the means) after the inquiry itself? The answer reveals a deeper principle about conceptual dependency and focus. Let's explore this exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice the argument from 'natural order' (al-nazm al-tabi'i): since this section deals with the matter of inquiry, it should come before the discussion of inquiry itself.
The objector assumes a progression from matter to form.
2
Understand the first answer
The first answer invokes dependency (tawaquf): the concept of the connecting path (al-tariq al-muwassil) includes the concept of inquiry, so it is epistemologically posterior.
This shows that conceptual priority can override temporal order.
3
Understand the second answer
The second answer distinguishes matter (madda) from form (sura): the earlier discussions focused on the form of inquiry, while this section focuses on its matter, so it is placed later.
This reflects a different organizational principle: focus on form first, then matter.
4
Reflect on the methodological lesson
Consider how this passage demonstrates the author's awareness of potential criticisms and his use of logical principles (dependency, form/matter) to justify the structure.
This is a common feature of Islamic theological writing – anticipating objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( المرصد السادس في الطريق الذي يقع فيه النظر قيل : لم أخر هذا المرصد عن مباحث النظر وضعاً؟ مع أن النظم الطبيعي يقتضي تقديمه لأن البحث فيه عن المعلومات التي يقع النظر فيها، فهو كالبحث عن المادة بالنسبة إلى ما سبق في مباحث النظر، وأجيب بأن مفهوم النظر مأخوذ في مفهوم الطريق الموصل، فقد توقف مفهوم الطريق الموصل على مفهوم النظر فلذا أخر مباحثه عنه وقيل : وجه الترتيب المذكور أن المعتمد بحث الصورة.
His statement: 'The sixth observation post: on the path in which inquiry occurs.' It is said: Why was this observation post delayed from the discussions of inquiry in terms of placement? While the natural order requires its precedence, because research in it concerns the informations in which inquiry occurs, so it is like research on matter in relation to what preceded in the discussions of inquiry. And it was answered: The concept of inquiry is taken in the concept of the connecting path, for the concept of the connecting path is dependent on the concept of inquiry, therefore its discussions were delayed after it. And it was said: The reason for the mentioned order is that the focus is on the discussion of form.
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage raises an objection: Why is this section on the path (al-tariq) placed after the discussions of inquiry (nazr), even though natural order would require it to come first, since it deals with the material (matter) of inquiry? Two answers are given: First, the concept of the path already includes the concept of inquiry (tawaquf), so the path is dependent on inquiry and thus placed after. Second, the main focus in the earlier sections was on the form (sura) of inquiry, not its matter, so the matter is discussed later.

Key Terms
المرصد
النظر
الطريق
المادة
الصورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the natural order of topics (moving from matter to form) must always be followed in theological writing.
Correction: The passage shows that conceptual dependency and authorial focus can justify a different order. Here, the path is discussed after inquiry because it is conceptually dependent on it, and the focus in earlier sections was on form, not matter.

Checkpoint
What is one of the answers given for why the section on the path is placed after the discussions of inquiry?
A) Because the path is more important than inquiry
B) Because the concept of the path depends on the concept of inquiry
C) Because the author made a mistake in ordering
D) Because the path does not involve inquiry

Key Takeaway
The order of topics in kalam can override natural sequence based on conceptual dependency and the priority of form over matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study Islamic theology, do you ever question the order of topics or accept it without reflection? How does this passage challenge you to understand the methodological reasoning behind the arrangement, and what does that reveal about your own approach to learning?
?
In your own pursuit of knowledge, do you tend to focus on form (structure and definitions) or matter (content and evidence) first? How might the priority given to form over matter in this passage influence your study habits?
?
If the path to inquiry depends on the concept of inquiry itself, then your method of learning is shaped by your understanding of what learning is. Are you truly engaging in inquiry, or are you just following a path without thought? What concrete step will you take today to ensure your method leads to genuine understanding?

Priority of Knowing the Identifier over the Identified
States that the identifier must be known before the identified, with two types of priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the identifier must be known before the identified, and the distinction between temporal and essential priority.

Core Idea
Knowing the identifier is both a path and a cause.

This lesson introduces a key methodological principle: in order to know something (the identified), we must first know that which identifies it (the identifier). The author explains that this priority is not merely temporal but also causal. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping the structure of knowledge in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage carefully. Notice the terms المعرف (identifier) and المعرف (identified). The passage states a rule: the identifier must be known before the identified.
The Arabic uses the same root for both terms, distinguished by context.
2
Understand the reason
The reason given is that knowledge of the identifier is a path (طريق) to knowledge of the identified. This establishes temporal priority.
Path implies sequence: first the path, then the destination.
3
Distinguish the two priorities
The commentary explains that there are two types of priority: temporal (زمانية) and essential (ذاتية). The path gives temporal priority; the causal relationship gives essential priority.
Essential priority means the identifier is the cause of knowledge.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Some might think priority is only temporal, but the passage shows it is also causal. Why would that matter for theology?
This distinction affects how we understand the role of definitions and proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثاني : المعرف تجب معرفته من المعرف تجب معرفته قبل] معرفة (المعرف) لأن معرفته طريق إلى معرفته قوله : ( قبل معرفة المعرف) قبلية زمانية وذاتية، وكونه طريقاً إليها يثبت القبلية الزمانية، وكونه سبباً لها يثبت القبلية الذاتية.
The second intention: The identifier, its knowledge is obligatory. From the identifier, its knowledge is obligatory before knowledge of the identified, because its knowledge is a path to knowledge of it. His statement: 'before knowledge of the identified' is a priority of time and essence. Its being a path to it establishes temporal priority, and its being a cause for it establishes essential priority.
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage introduces the second intention (maqsad thani) which states that the identifier (al-mu'arif) must be known before the identified (al-mu'arraf). The reason is that knowing the identifier is the path to knowing the identified. The commentator clarifies that this priority is twofold: temporal priority (al-qabliyya al-zamaniyya) because the identifier is the path, and essential priority (al-dhatiyya) because it is the cause of knowledge.

Key Terms
المعرف
المعرف
القبلية الزمانية
القبلية الذاتية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the priority of the identifier over the identified is only temporal (i.e., it is simply learned first in chronological order).
Correction: The passage teaches that there is also an essential priority: the identifier is the cause of knowledge of the identified. This causal relationship means the identifier is more fundamental, not just earlier.

Checkpoint
Why does the identifier have temporal priority over the identified?
A) Because it is more important
B) Because it is a path to the identified
C) Because it is created before
D) Because it is simpler

Key Takeaway
The identifier must be known before the identified in two ways: as a path (temporal priority) and as a cause (essential priority).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn a new concept in 'aqeedah, do you first master the key defining terms (the identifier), or do you jump straight to conclusions? This lesson teaches that the identifier is both the path and the cause. How has neglecting this order made your understanding shaky in the past? What specific term will you commit to defining clearly before your next study session?
?
The identifier is not just a tool but a cause—it actively produces knowledge of the identified. Have you treated foundational definitions as a mere formality rather than the very engine of your understanding? If you were to truly honour the causal role of the identifier starting today, what would you do differently when you encounter a new theological topic?
?
You have just learned that priority is both temporal and essential. Which type of priority do you tend to overlook in your learning: the order of time or the order of causation? How has that oversight led you to misunderstand or misapply a truth in your faith? Write down one change you will make to your study habits to align with both priorities.

5 min
The Definition Must Be Different and Clearer
Explains the condition that a definition must be different and clearer, with circularity examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a definition must be different from and clearer than the thing defined, and how circular definitions (dawr) violate this condition.

Core Idea
A definition must be clearer than the defined, not equal or more obscure.

Before we can define anything, we need to know what counts as a valid definition. The classical logicians insisted that a definition must be both different from (ghayr) and clearer than (ajla) the thing being defined. This lesson unpacks why these two conditions are necessary and introduces the two types of circular definitions that break them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation. Notice how the author presents the condition and then gives examples.
The passage uses technical terms like dawr ṣarīḥ and dawr muḍmar.
2
Understand the two conditions
Identify the two requirements: the definition must be other than the defined (ghayruhu) and clearer (ajla). Ask why each is necessary.
If it were identical, you'd have a thing known before it is known, which is absurd.
3
Examine the circularity examples
The passage gives two types of circular definitions: explicit and implicit. See how each example makes a circle in reasoning.
Explicit circularity is direct, implicit is indirect through multiple steps.
4
Reflect on why clarity matters
Think about why a definition must be clearer. The passage says if it were equal or more obscure, it couldn't serve as prior knowledge.
This prevents pseudo-explanations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسبب لها، فلا بد أن تتقدمها ( فيكون غيره) إذ لو كان عينه لزم كون الشيء معلوماً قبل أن يكون معلوماً (و) يكون أيضاً ( أجلى منه ) إذ لو ساواه في الجلاء، أو كان أخفى منه لم يكن معلوماً قبله فلا يعرف هذا تفريع على كونه أجلى، أي لا يعرف الشيء (بما لا يعرف إلا (به فإنه لا يكون أجلى منه سواء توقف معرفته على معرفته (بمرتبة ) واحدة، ويسمى دوراً صريحاً كقولك : الشمس كوكب نهاري، والنهار زمان كون الشمس طالعة ( أو أكثر) ويسمى دوراً مضمراً، كقولك: الحركة خروج الشيء من القوة إلى الفعل بالتدريج والتدريج وقوع الشيء في زمان والزمان مقدار الحركة )
And it is a cause for it, so it must precede it (so it is other than it) because if it were identical, it would necessitate that a thing is known before it is known (and) it is also (clearer than it) because if it equaled it in clarity, or was more obscure, it would not be known before it, so it is not known. This is a corollary of it being clearer, i.e., a thing is not known (by that which is not known except through it) for then it would not be clearer than it, whether its knowledge depends on its knowledge by one degree, which is called explicit circularity (dawr ṣarīḥ) like your saying: the sun is a daytime star, and daytime is the time when the sun is rising (or more) and is called implicit circularity (dawr muḍmar) like your saying: motion is the gradual emergence of a thing from potentiality to actuality, and gradualness is the occurrence of a thing in time, and time is the measure of motion)
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage presents the first condition for a definition (muʿarraf): it must be other than (ghayr) the defined thing and clearer (ajla) than it. The author argues that if the definition were identical, it would lead to a thing being known before it is known, which is absurd. If it were equal in clarity or more obscure, it could not serve as prior knowledge. This condition is illustrated by two types of circular definitions (dawr): explicit circularity (dawr ṣarīḥ), where the mutual dependency is direct, as in defining the sun as a daytime star and daytime as the time of sunrise; and implicit circularity (dawr muḍmar), where the dependency is indirect, as in defining motion as gradual emergence and gradualness in terms of time, and time in terms of motion. The key point is that the definition must be clearer to avoid circular reasoning.

Key Terms
معرف
دور صريح
دور مضمر
جلاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A definition can be equal in clarity to the defined and still be useful.
Correction: The passage states that if it equaled it in clarity, it would not be known before it, so it cannot serve as a definition. It must be clearer.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two types of circular definitions (dawr) mentioned?
A) Explicit (ṣarīḥ) and implicit (muḍmar)
B) Formal and material
C) Direct and indirect
D) Simple and complex

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must be different from and clearer than the defined; otherwise it leads to circular reasoning (dawr).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a definition of a core Islamic concept (like tawḥīd or īmān) that, when you really examine it, goes in a circle? What would it take for you to replace it with a definition that is truly clearer?
?
In your own learning, do you prefer definitions that are comfortable (familiar terms) or those that force you to think more clearly? When have you settled for an unclear definition because it was easier?

Later vs Early Scholars on Equality in Definitions
Presents the conflicting views on equality in definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the contrasting views of the later scholars (muta'akhkhirun) and early scholars (mutaqaddimun) regarding whether a definition must be equal in scope to the defined.

Core Idea
Equality in definition is required by later scholars but only for perfect distinction, not by early scholars.

This passage presents the core disagreement in Islamic logic about the condition of equality (musawat) in definitions. The later scholars insist on equality to achieve complete distinction, while the early scholars permit definitions that are more general or more specific, considering equality only a condition for a good definition. Read on to see the exact positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on later scholars' view
Start with the first sentence which states that later scholars require equality for complete distinction. Notice the term 'tamayyuz tamm' (complete distinction).
This is the strictest position.
2
Read the early scholars' response
Then read the second part where early scholars distinguish between perfect (tamm) and imperfect (naqis) definitions, and allow more general or specific definitions.
They consider equality a condition for quality, not necessity.
3
Compare the two positions
Identify the key difference: later scholars require equality for all definitions to avoid confusion, while early scholars accept partial distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن اشتراط المساواة في الصدق مما ذهب إليه المتأخرون إذ حينئذ يحصل التميز التام بحيث يمتاز جميع أفراد المعرف ما عداها ولا يلتبس شيء منها بغيرها، وأما المتقدمون فقد قالوا : الرسم تام يميز المرسوم عن كل ما يغايره ومنه ناقص يميزه عن بعض ما يغايره، بأن المساواة شرط لجودة الرسم كيلا يتناول ما ليس من المرسوم، ولا يخلو عما هو منه وجوزوا الرسم بالأعم والأخص، وأيد ذلك بأن المعرف لا بد أن يفيد التميز عن بعض الأغيار، فإن ما لا يفيد تميز الشيء عن غيره أصلاً لم يكن سبباً لتصوره، وأما التميز عن جميعها فليس شرطاً له، لأن التصورات المكتسبة كما قد تكون بوجه خاص بالشيء، إما ذاتي أو عرضي، كذلك قد تكون بوجه عام ذاتي أو عرضي، فيجب أن يكون كاسب كل منهما معرفاً، فالمساواة شرط للمعرف التام دون غيره حداً كان أو
And know that the requirement of equality in application (muṣāwāt fī al-ṣidq) is what the later scholars (mutaʾakhkhirūn) held, for then complete distinction (tamayyuz tāmm) is achieved such that all individuals of the defined are distinguished from what is other than them and nothing of them is confused with others. As for the early scholars (mutaqaddimūn), they said: the definition (rasm) is perfect (tāmm) distinguishing the defined from everything that differs from it, and among it is imperfect (nāqiṣ) distinguishing it from some of what differs from it, [and they held] that equality is a condition for the quality of the definition so that it does not include what is not from the defined, nor exclude what is from it, and they allowed definition by the more general and more specific. This is supported by the fact that the definition must provide distinction from some others, for what does not provide distinction of a thing from others at all is not a cause for its conceptualization. As for distinction from all of them, it is not a condition for it, because acquired concepts (taṣawwurāt muktasaba) may be by a specific aspect of the thing, either essential or accidental, and likewise may be by a general aspect, essential or accidental. So the acquirer of each must be a definition. Thus equality is a condition for the perfect definition (muʿarrif tāmm) as opposed to others, whether a limit (ḥadd) or not
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage presents the differing opinions of the early (mutaqaddimūn) and later (mutaʾakhkhirūn) scholars regarding the condition that a definition must be equal in scope to the defined. The later scholars insist on equality to achieve complete distinction (tamayyuz tāmm) of all individuals of the defined from everything else. The early scholars, however, distinguish between a perfect definition (rasm tāmm) that distinguishes from everything different, and an imperfect definition (rasm nāqiṣ) that distinguishes only from some. They consider equality a condition for a good (jīda) definition, not an absolute necessity, and permit definitions that are more general or more specific. Their justification is that a definition only needs to provide distinction from at least some others; complete distinction is not required because acquired concepts can be specific or general. Therefore, equality is only required for the perfect definition (muʿarrif tāmm), not for all definitions.

Key Terms
متأخرون
متقدمون
رسم تام
رسم ناقص
تمييز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all scholars agree that definitions must be equal in scope to the defined.
Correction: Early scholars allow definitions that are broader or narrower than the defined, because they only require partial distinction.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the view of the early scholars (mutaqaddimun) regarding definitions that are broader or narrower than the defined?
A) They reject such definitions completely
B) They allow them as imperfect definitions
C) They require them to be equal
D) They consider them invalid

Key Takeaway
Later scholars insist on equality for complete distinction, while early scholars permit definitions that are more general or specific, considering equality a condition for perfection, not validity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn a new concept, do you demand absolute precision in its definition, or are you willing to accept an imperfect description and clarify later? Which scholarly group's approach resonates more with your current practice, and what does that reveal about your learning style?
?
The later scholars insisted on equality for complete distinction. In your own studies, do you ever reject a teaching because it doesn't perfectly distinguish the concept from others, missing the benefit of an imperfect but useful definition? How might adopting the early scholars' flexibility improve your understanding?
?
Consider a time when you were confused by a definition because it was too broad or too narrow. Did you dismiss the concept entirely, or did you try to work with the imperfect definition? What does this lesson teach you about the value of both approaches?

The Early Scholars' Justification for Non-Equal Definitions
Provides the early scholars' logical justification for allowing non-equal definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical justification provided by the early scholars for allowing definitions that are not equal in scope, based on the role of distinction in conceptualization.

Core Idea
Definitions need only partial distinction because acquired concepts can be general or specific.

After presenting the early scholars' permission of non-equal definitions, the author now explains their reasoning. Early scholars argue that a definition only needs to provide distinction from some others, not all, because concepts can be acquired through either specific or general aspects. This lesson explores that justification.

Guided Path
1
Read the justification passage
Read the Arabic passage that begins with 'وأيد ذلك بأن المعرف لا بد أن يفيد التميز عن بعض الأغيار'. It presents the core argument that definition only requires partial distinction.
Focus on the phrase 'لا بد أن يفيد التميز عن بعض الأغيار'.
2
Understand the analogy with acquired concepts
Then read the explanation that acquired concepts can be specific or general, so the definition must match the level of the concept.
This shows why equality is only required for perfect (complete) definitions.
3
See the conclusion
The passage concludes that equality is a condition for the perfect definition (mu'arif tamm) but not for others.
The text ends mid-sentence, but the conclusion is clear from context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن اشتراط المساواة في الصدق مما ذهب إليه المتأخرون إذ حينئذ يحصل التميز التام بحيث يمتاز جميع أفراد المعرف ما عداها ولا يلتبس شيء منها بغيرها، وأما المتقدمون فقد قالوا : الرسم تام يميز المرسوم عن كل ما يغايره ومنه ناقص يميزه عن بعض ما يغايره، بأن المساواة شرط لجودة الرسم كيلا يتناول ما ليس من المرسوم، ولا يخلو عما هو منه وجوزوا الرسم بالأعم والأخص، وأيد ذلك بأن المعرف لا بد أن يفيد التميز عن بعض الأغيار، فإن ما لا يفيد تميز الشيء عن غيره أصلاً لم يكن سبباً لتصوره، وأما التميز عن جميعها فليس شرطاً له، لأن التصورات المكتسبة كما قد تكون بوجه خاص بالشيء، إما ذاتي أو عرضي، كذلك قد تكون بوجه عام ذاتي أو عرضي، فيجب أن يكون كاسب كل منهما معرفاً، فالمساواة شرط للمعرف التام دون غيره حداً كان أو
And know that the requirement of equality in application (muṣāwāt fī al-ṣidq) is what the later scholars (mutaʾakhkhirūn) held, for then complete distinction (tamayyuz tāmm) is achieved such that all individuals of the defined are distinguished from what is other than them and nothing of them is confused with others. As for distinction from all of them, it is not a condition for it, because acquired concepts (taṣawwurāt muktasaba) may be by a specific aspect of the thing, either essential or accidental, and likewise may be by a general aspect, essential or accidental. So the acquirer of each must be a definition. Thus equality is a condition for the perfect definition (muʿarrif tāmm) as opposed to others, whether a limit (ḥadd) or not
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage presents the differing opinions of the early (mutaqaddimūn) and later (mutaʾakhkhirūn) scholars regarding the condition that a definition must be equal in scope to the defined. The later scholars insist on equality to achieve complete distinction (tamayyuz tāmm) of all individuals of the defined from everything else. Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the early scholars' justification, what is the primary purpose of a definition?
A) To distinguish the defined from everything else
B) To distinguish the defined from at least some others
C) To provide an essential description
D) To be equal in scope to the defined

Key Takeaway
Early scholars justify non-equal definitions by arguing that a definition only needs partial distinction, as acquired concepts can be general or specific.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you hesitated to act on a religious understanding because you felt you didn't have a complete definition. How does this lesson's principle of partial distinction challenge you to move forward with what you know?
?
In your personal study of Islamic creed, do you tend to dismiss definitions that only give partial distinction? How might accepting partial definitions change your learning process?
?
Consider a concept of Allah's attributes that you struggle to understand fully. Can you hold a valid but partial understanding of it? What would it take for you to accept that partial understanding as sufficient for your faith?

Universal to Particular Inference
Sanusi's observatory on universal and particular inference

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical principle that a state affirmed or negated of a universal as such applies to every particular instance, and why this is essential in kalam reasoning.

Core Idea
What holds for the universal holds for every particular.

In this passage, the text explains one of the fundamental inference patterns in kalam: reasoning from a universal to its particulars. Understanding this logical move is crucial for evaluating theological arguments that rely on general principles. The commentary clarifies the rationale and the conditions under which such inference is valid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice the concise style and the example of animal and human.
The passage is part of a larger observatory structure.
2
Identify the two inference directions
The passage states that inference can be from universal to particular or particular to universal. Focus on the first direction, which is explained in detail.
The commentary notes this classification is inductive.
3
Understand the condition for validity
The key condition is that the state must be known of the universal 'insofar as it is universal' — i.e., as a universal, not accidentally. Otherwise, the inference fails.
This preserves the logical connection between universal and particular.
4
Reflect on the underlying principle
If the state applies to the universal as such, it must apply to every particular because the universal's nature includes its particulars. The passage warns against the impossibility of a thing being separated from itself.
This principle is foundational in kalam and Islamic logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الثالث : الاستدلال الكلي والجزئي الاستدلال إما بالكلي ( كالحيوان مثلاً ( على الجزئي) كالإنسان فإنه يستدل بحال الأول على حال الثاني (وهو) أي ما يستدل فيه بحال الكلي حال الجزئي قوله : (الاستدلال إلخ) وهذا الحصر استقرائي على رأي من يجعل المفرد دليلاً، وحاصل الكلام أنه إن كان المعلوم ثبوت حال الكلي، أو انتفاؤه عنه من حيث إنه كلي، مع قطع النظر عن على كل وجه يتحققان وتكون الماهية معلومة بالكنه وإلا لتخلف الشيء عن نفسه ولازمه عنه انتهى كلامه.
The Sixth Observatory - Third Purpose: Universal and Particular Inference. Inference is either from the universal (e.g., animal) to the particular (e.g., human), for one infers from the state of the first to the state of the second; (and it is) i.e., that in which one infers from the state of the universal to the state of the particular. His statement: 'Inference, etc.' And this enumeration is inductive, according to the opinion of one who considers the singular as evidence. And the summary of the speech is that if what is known is the affirmation of a state of the universal, or its negation from it insofar as it is universal, with abstraction from... in every way they are realized and the essence is known in its true nature, otherwise the thing would be separated from itself and its necessary consequence. End of his speech.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the third purpose of the observatory: inference (istidlāl) in kalam. It states that inference is either from the universal (kullī) to the particular (juz'ī), e.g., from animal to human, inferring the state of one from the state of the other. The commentary clarifies that this classification is inductive, based on the view that the singular term (mufrad) can serve as evidence. The key point is that if we know that a state applies to the universal as such (either affirmation or negation), then it applies to every particular instance, because the essence (māhiyya) is known in its true nature (bi-l-kunh); otherwise, the thing would be separated from itself, which is impossible. This reflects the logical principle that what is true of the universal is true of its particulars.

Key Terms
استدلال
كلي
جزئي
معلوم
ماهية
كنه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Inference from universal to particular is always valid without conditions.
Correction: The inference is only valid if the state is known of the universal in its universal aspect, not accidentally. Otherwise, the inference may not hold.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for the inference from universal to particular?
A) The singular term is considered evidence.
B) The universal is known through the particular.
C) The essence is known only through the differentia.
D) It is based on analogical reasoning.

Key Takeaway
A state known of a universal as such necessarily applies to every particular instance, because the universal's essence is fully realized in each particular.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim that God's attribute of mercy is universal—but do you truly live as if it applies to every failure, every sin, every dark moment of your life? When you feel unworthy, do you still trust that His mercy reaches you? If you doubt, what does that say about your belief in the universal nature of His attributes? Commit today to affirm His mercy over every specific regret.
?
The passage warns that denying the transfer of a essential state from universal to particular would 'separate the thing from itself.' In your own faith, are there areas where you act as if some Islamic truth applies in general but not to your particular situation? For example, do you believe prayer is obligatory for all Muslims but make exceptions for yourself when busy? Reflect on one such contradiction and resolve to align your practice with your belief.

Avoiding Unclear Words in Definitions
The author enumerates three types of unclear words to avoid in definitions and discusses their relative severity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three types of unclear words that must be avoided in definitions and how their severity depends on the presence of clarifying context.

Core Idea
Definitions must be crystal clear; strange, ambiguous, and metaphorical terms only create confusion.

In Islamic logic, definitions are meant to clarify, not obscure. The author explains three types of words that defeat this purpose: strange words the listener doesn't understand, ambiguous words with multiple meanings, and metaphorical words that mislead. He explains why each is problematic and how they compare in severity. This lesson unpacks that crucial methodological principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Notice the author's direct style and the commentator's expansions.
2
Identify the three types
Pinpoint the three categories of unclear words: strange (ghariba), common/musharak, and metaphorical/majaz. Understand the specific problem each creates.
The problem: strange words require explanation, mushtarak causes hesitation, majaz misleads.
3
Examine the ranking discussion
The passage presents two differing rankings from commentators. Note the resolution based on the presence or absence of a context (qarina) diverting from the literal meaning.
Without context: majaz is worst. With context but ambiguous: mushtarak is worst.
4
Apply the principle
Reflect on why the author insists on 'clear indication' (zuhur al-dalala). Consider how this principle applies to writing definitions in any field.
The goal of a definition is explanation (izhar wa tawdih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الثالث : الاستدلال الكلي والجزئي
المنحصرة في الجنس والفصل ( ويحترز) فيه ( عن الألفاظ الغريبة الوحشية التي لا يفهم السامع معناها فيحتاج إلى تفسيرها فتطول المسافة، وذلك مما يختلف بالقياس إلى السامعين، فإن اصطلاحات كل قوم مشهورة عند أربابها غريبة عند غيرهم (وعن المشترك والمجاز بلا (قرينة ظاهرة فيتردد السامع حينئذ في المشترك بين المقصود ،وغيره ويتبادر ذهنه في المجاز إلى غيره ) وبالجملة فعن كل لفظ غير ظاهر الدلالة على المقصود ) وذلك لأنه بصدد الإظهار والتوضيح، فلا بد من ظهور الدلالة.
قوله : ( فتطول المسافة ) فيه إشارة إلى أنه لا خلل في إفادة المراد .
قوله : ( بلا قرينة (ظاهرة بأن لا تكون قرينة أو لا تكون ظاهرة .
قوله : ( ويتبادر ذهنه في المجاز إلى غيره فيه إشارة إلى أن المجاز أردأ من المشترك، وبه صرح في حواشي المطالع من أن المشترك أردأ من المجاز ، فلعله بالنظر إلى الاستعمال، فإن استعمال المشترك والمجاز بلا قرينة غير جائز ، ومع ذلك استعمال المشترك أقل من المجاز .
قوله : ( ويحترز عن الألفاظ الغريبة الوحشية وعن المشترك والمجاز بلا قرينة ذكر الشارح في حواشي شرح المختصر إِن هذه الثلاثة مرتبة في الرداءة فإن الألفاظ المشتركة أردأ من الغريبة، إذ لا يفهم من الألفاظ الغريبة شيء، فيحتاج إلى تفسيرها فتطول المسافة، وأيضاً الغرابة تختلف بحسب قوم وقوم، وفي الألفاظ المشتركة بلا قرينة معينة لأحد معانيها يتردد السامع بين المقصود، وغيره فلا يفهم المقصود بل ربما يفهم غيره والألفاظ المجازية أردأ من المشتركة إذ المجازية بلا قرينة صارفة عن المعنى الحقيقي ظاهرة في غير المقصود، فلا يفهم المقصود بل يتبادر الفهم إلى غير المقصود، ويقع الجهل وذكر في حواشي المطالع أن الألفاظ المشتركة أردأ من المجازية والمجازية أردأ من الغريبة الوحشية وبين كلاميه مخالفة ظاهرة لا يقال : عند عدم صرف القرينة عن الحقيقة، فالمجاز أرداً من المشترك كما ذكر في حواشي شرح المختصر، وعند الصرف وعدم تعيين المراد فالمشترك أرداً منه إذ فيه مزاحمة غير المقصود للمقصود،
The sixth observatory - the third objective: Universal and particular inference. Confined to genus and differentia. He (the author) guards against strange, wild words whose meaning the listener does not grasp, so he needs their explanation and the distance becomes long; and that differs relative to listeners, for the terminologies of each group are well-known among their practitioners but strange to others. And against the common (mushtarak) and metaphorical (majaz) without a clear context (qarina), so the listener then hesitates in the common between the intended and other, and his mind in the metaphorical jumps to other. In summary, against every word whose indication of the intended is not apparent, because it is in the position of making apparent and clarifying, so clarity of indication is necessary. His saying: 'so the distance becomes long' indicates that there is no defect in conveying the intended. His saying: 'without a clear context' means either there is no context or it is not clear. His saying: 'and his mind in the metaphorical jumps to other' indicates that the metaphor is worse than the common; and it is stated in the glosses of al-Matali' that the common is worse than the metaphor; perhaps it is with respect to usage, for using the common and metaphor without context is not permissible, yet use of the common is less than the metaphor. His saying: 'And he guards against strange, wild words and against the common and metaphor without context.' The commentator mentioned in the glosses of Sharh al-Mukhtasar that these three are ranked in undesirability: common words are worse than strange ones, because from strange words nothing is understood, so they need interpretation and the distance becomes long; also strangeness differs according to different groups. In common words without a context specifying one of their meanings, the listener hesitates between the intended and other, so he does not understand the intended, rather he might understand something else. Metaphorical words are worse than common ones, because metaphorical without a context diverting from the literal meaning appear as other than the intended, so he does not understand the intended, rather understanding jumps to the unintended, and ignorance occurs. And it was mentioned in the glosses of al-Matali' that common words are worse than metaphorical, and metaphorical are worse than strange wild words. There is an apparent discrepancy between his two statements. It is not said: when there is no diversion of context from the real meaning, then metaphor is worse than common, as mentioned in the glosses of Sharh al-Mukhtasar; and when there is diversion and no determination of the intended, then common is worse because it involves competition of the unintended with the intended.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the requirement for clear wording in definitions. The author states that when defining something by genus and differentia, one must avoid three types of problematic words: (1) strange and barbaric terms that the listener does not understand and need explanation, which lengthens the discourse; (2) ambiguous (mushtarak) words without a clear context that leave the listener uncertain between possible meanings; and (3) metaphorical (majaz) terms without a clear context that cause the listener to jump to a wrong meaning. The reason is that the definition aims to clarify, so its wording must be unambiguous. The commentator then clarifies each point: the phrase 'the distance becomes long' indicates no flaw in conveying intent; 'without a clear context' means either no context exists or it is not plain; and the statement about metaphor jumping to other shows metaphor is worse than common (contradicting another mention where common is worse). The commentator reconciles by noting context: without any context diverting from the literal, metaphor is worse; but when context diverts but still ambiguous, common is worse due to competition of meanings. This ranking is context-dependent.

Key Terms
لفظ غريب
لفظ مشترك
لفظ مجاز
قرينة
ظهور الدلالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Metaphorical words are always a problem, even when a clear context is present.
Correction: The passage specifies that metaphorical words are only problematic when used without a clear context (قرينة ظاهرة). With proper context, they can be acceptable.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of unclear word is considered the worst when there is no context diverting from the literal meaning?
A) Strange (ghariba)
B) Common (mushtarak)
C) Metaphorical (majaz)
D) All are equally bad

Key Takeaway
A good definition must use clear, unambiguous words to effectively communicate the intended meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about matters of faith, do you ever use words that could be misunderstood? Think of a specific example where your listener might have hesitated or jumped to a wrong meaning. What will you do differently tomorrow to ensure clarity?
?
The author teaches that ambiguous (mushtarak) and metaphorical (majaz) words can cause serious misunderstanding. Is there a belief you hold about Allah that you learned from a teacher or book that might have been unclear? How can you verify its correctness using clear, unambiguous sources?
?
You are responsible for the words you use when teaching or advising others. Will you now take the time to examine your vocabulary and remove any terms that are 'strange, ambiguous, or metaphorical without context'? If not, what is holding you back?

The Syllogism as the Mainstay and Its First Form
Al-Sharif al-Jurjani defines qiyas as the mainstay and gives its first form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why qiyas is considered the mainstay of reasoning and how its first form operates.

Core Idea
Qiyas yields certainty; induction and analogy only yield certainty if they reduce to qiyas.

In this passage, al-Sharif al-Jurjani establishes qiyas (syllogism) as the cornerstone of logical reasoning for its ability to yield certainty. He contrasts it with induction and analogy, which fall short on their own, and then delineates the first of its five forms. The commentary clarifies the logical ordering of premises, emphasizing that the word 'thumma' (then) denotes rank, not time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the English translation. Notice how the author defines qiyas and contrasts it with other methods.
2
Identify why qiyas is the mainstay
Look for the phrase 'because it yields certainty' and note why induction and analogy are insufficient without reverting to qiyas.
The commentator specifies that this applies when premises are certain.
3
Analyze the first form
Read the description of the first form: knowing a judgment for every individual of the middle term, then applying it to the minor term. Understand the logical ordering indicated by 'thumma'.
The commentary explains that 'thumma' indicates logical rank, not temporal sequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الرابع: القياس وهو العمدة. القياس وهو العمدة لإفادته اليقين فإن الاستقراء لا يفيد يقينا إلا إذا كان قياسا مقسما وكذا التمثيل لا يفيده إلا إذا كانت العلة فيه قطعية وحينئذ يرجع إلى القياس هكذا النبيذ مسكر وكل مسكر حرام صوره خمس: الأولى أن يعلم حكم إيجابي أو سلبي لكل أفراد شيء هو الأوسط ثم يعلم ثبوته أي ثبوت ذلك الشيء الذي هو الأوسط لآخر هو الأصغر كله أو بعضه فيعلم ثبوت ذلك الحكم. قوله: (لإفادته اليقين) إذا كانت مقدماته يقينية بخلاف الاستقراء والتمثيل، فإنهما من حيث ذاتيهما لا يفيد أنه أصلاً . قوله: (ثم يعلم إلخ) كلمة ثم للتراخي في الرتبة لا في الزمان إذ لا يجب أن يتقدم على الصغرى بعد الكبرى زمان .
The fourth purpose: The syllogism (qiyas), and it is the mainstay. The syllogism is the mainstay because it yields certainty; for induction (istiqra') does not yield certainty unless it is a divided syllogism, and likewise analogy (tamthil) does not yield it unless the cause (illa) in it is decisive, and then it reverts to syllogism. For example: 'Wine is intoxicating, and every intoxicant is forbidden.' Its forms are five: The first is that one knows a positive or negative judgment for every individual of something (the middle term), then one knows its affirmation, i.e., the affirmation of that thing which is the middle term, for another (the minor term) wholly or partially, and then one knows the affirmation of that judgment. His statement: (because it yields certainty) — when its premises are certain, unlike induction and analogy, for they by their essence do not yield certainty at all. His statement: (then one knows, etc.) — the word 'thumma' (then) indicates delay in rank, not in time, since it is not necessary that the minor premise temporally follow the major premise.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage defines qiyas (syllogism) as the mainstay (al-umda) of reasoning because it yields certainty (yaqin). It contrasts qiyas with induction (istiqra') and analogy (tamthil): induction only yields certainty if it is a 'divided syllogism' (qiyas muqassam), and analogy only if its cause (illa) is decisive—in such cases they revert to qiyas. The author gives an example: 'Wine is intoxicating, and every intoxicant is forbidden.' He then outlines the first of five forms of qiyas: (1) One knows a positive or negative judgment for every individual of a thing (the middle term), then one knows that thing (the middle) applies to another thing (the minor) wholly or partially, resulting in the judgment for that minor. The commentary clarifies that 'for the sake of certainty' means when the premises are certain, unlike induction and analogy which by themselves do not yield certainty. The word 'thumma' (then) in the description denotes a logical ordering, not temporal sequence; it is not necessary that the minor premise be known after the major premise in time.

Key Terms
قياس
عمدة
يقين
استقراء
تمثيل
علة
أوسط
أصغر
حكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'thumma' (then) in the description implies that the minor premise must be known after the major premise in time.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that 'thumma' indicates a logical ordering (rank), not temporal sequence; it is not necessary that the minor premise temporally follow the major premise.

Checkpoint
Why is qiyas considered the mainstay of reasoning?
A) It is the most common form of reasoning
B) It yields certainty when its premises are certain
C) It is the simplest form of reasoning
D) It is the only form accepted by the early scholars

Key Takeaway
Qiyas is the mainstay of reasoning because it yields certainty when its premises are certain; its first form requires knowing a judgment for the middle term, then applying it to the minor term via a logical inference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a decision about a religious ruling, do you rely on certain premises (yaqini) or only on inductive generalizations? Have you ever mistaken a weak analogy for a certain syllogism? How will you change your method of deriving rulings after this lesson?
?
The passage says qiyas is the mainstay because it yields certainty. In your own life, where do you seek certainty? Are you willing to build your faith on syllogisms that are only as strong as their premises? Is there any area where you have accepted uncertainty in matters of aqeedah? What must you verify?
?
Consider the phrase 'every individual of something (the middle term)'. Do you know all individuals of a category when you make a claim? For example, do you assume something about all Muslims based on a few? This lesson challenges you to ensure your premises are universally certain. Can you honestly say your daily judgments about others are based on certain premises or only on incomplete induction?

Understanding Implication (Talāzum) and Its One-Way Rule
Al-Iji defines talazum and states its condition and the absence of reversal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Iji defines talazum (implication) and why it is valid only in one direction.

Core Idea
Implication runs one way: existence of the necessitating requires existence of the necessitated, but not the reverse.

Al-Iji introduces the fourth strong method for establishing a link between two things: implication. He lays down the condition for such an implication and emphasizes that it does not work backwards. Understanding this one-way relationship is essential for constructing valid theological proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the original Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a feel for the concise style. Notice the key terms: ملازمة, لازم, ملزوم, عكس.
The Arabic is dense; read carefully.
2
Identify the two conditions for implication
Find where al-Iji states that implication holds only if (a) the necessitating's existence implies the necessitated's existence, and (b) the necessitated's non-existence implies the necessitating's non-existence.
If either fails, there is no implication.
3
Understand the 'without reversal' clause
Realize that implication is not symmetric. The text explicitly says that from the non-existence of the necessitating, the non-existence of the necessitated does not follow, nor from existence of the necessitated does existence of the necessitating follow.
The reason given is that the necessitated may be more general.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابعة أن تثبت ملازمة بين شيئين فيلزم من وجود الملزوم وجود اللازم ومن عدم اللازم عدم الملزوم وإلا فلا لزوم بينهما إذ قد وجد الملزوم حينئذ بدون اللازم من غير عكس أي ليس يلزم من عدم الملزوم عدم اللازم ولا من وجود اللازم وجود الملزوم لجواز أن يكون اللازم أعم
The fourth [strong method] is to establish an implication between two things, so from the existence of the necessary (malzum) the existence of the necessary (lazim) follows, and from the non-existence of the necessary the non-existence of the necessary follows. Otherwise there is no implication between them, since the necessary would then have existed without the necessary, without reversal, i.e., it is not necessary from the non-existence of the necessary the non-existence of the necessary, nor from the existence of the necessary the existence of the necessary, because it is possible that the necessary is more general.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth strong method: establishing implication (talazum) between two things. The rule: if the necessary exists, the necessary must exist; if the necessary does not exist, the necessary must not exist. If either of these conditions fails, there is no implication. Moreover, implication is not reciprocal: from the non-existence of the necessary, the non-existence of the necessary does not follow, nor from the existence of the necessary does the existence of the necessary follow, because the necessary may be more general than the necessary (i.e., it could exist without the necessary).

Key Terms
ملازمة
لازم
ملزوم
لزوم
عكس
أعم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Implication always works in both directions (if A then B, then if B then A).
Correction: Al-Iji clarifies that implication is one-way: from the existence of the necessitating to the existence of the necessitated, and from the non-existence of the necessitated to the non-existence of the necessitating. The reverse does not hold because the necessitated may be more general.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'without reversal' (من غير عكس) mean?
A) Implication holds in both directions
B) Implication is only from the necessary to the necessary
C) Implication is only from the necessary to the necessary, not the reverse
D) Implication is transitive

Key Takeaway
Implication (talazum) requires that the existence of the necessitating necessitates the existence of the necessitated, and the non-existence of the necessitated necessitates the non-existence of the necessitating, but not vice versa.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a core belief you hold about Allah (your malzum). What specific actions or attitudes (lazim) must necessarily follow from that belief? Now examine your life honestly: are those actions consistently present? If not, what does that reveal about the depth of your belief today?Â
?
Think of a time you assumed that because another Muslim’s practice seemed lacking (absence of a lazim), their faith (the malzum) must also be weak. In light of al-Iji’s rule that absence of the malzum does not necessarily follow from absence of the lazim, how might you have been mistaken? Will you commit to judging others’ hearts less harshly from now on?

4 min
Establishing Mutual Exclusivity (Munafah) and Its Types
Al-Iji defines munafah and its two types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of munafah (mutual exclusivity), its two types (contradiction in truth and contradiction in falsehood), and the number of logical conclusions each type yields.

Core Idea
Mutual exclusivity gives us powerful logical rules: if one is true, the other is false, and vice versa.

Al-Iji now introduces the fifth strong method of proof: establishing mutual exclusivity between two propositions. This method provides us with clear logical implications based on the relationship of contradiction. The passage defines two kinds of munafah — one that concerns truth, and another that concerns falsehood — each yielding specific conclusions. Understanding these will sharpen your ability to construct valid arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand the plain meaning.
Notice that the passage begins by stating that this is the fifth method.
2
Identify the two types of munafah
Find where the author distinguishes between exclusivity in truth only and exclusivity in falsehood only.
These two types are the core classification in this passage.
3
Note the conclusions for each type
For each type, determine what follows from affirming the truth or falsehood of one matter.
Each type yields two conclusions.
4
Combine both types
Understand that when both types apply simultaneously, there are four possible conclusions.
The passage explicitly states this combination.
5
Reflect on the method's strength
Consider why this method is called a 'strong method' — it provides definite logical implications.
This method is foundational in kalam arguments.

الخامسة أن تثبت المنافاة بين أمرين فيلزم من ثبوت أيهما عدم الآخر قطعاً فإن تنافيا صدقاً فقط لزم من ثبوت صدق أيهما كان عدم صدق الآخر أي كذبه وإن تنافيا كذباً فقط لزم من ثبوت كذب أيهما كان عدم كذب الآخر أعني صدقه ففي كل واحدة من هاتين المنافاتين نتيجتان وإذا اجتمعا معاً كان هناك أربع نتائج
The fifth [strong method] is to establish mutual exclusivity between two matters, so from the affirmation of either of them the non-existence of the other necessarily follows. For if they are mutually exclusive in truth only, then from the affirmation of the truth of either of them, the non-truth of the other, i.e., its falsehood, follows. And if they are mutually exclusive in falsehood only, then from the affirmation of the falsehood of either of them, the non-falsehood of the other, i.e., its truth, follows. So in each of these two exclusivities there are two conclusions, and when both are combined together, there are four conclusions.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage defines the fifth strong method: establishing mutual exclusivity (munafah) between two matters. The basic rule: if one is affirmed, the other is necessarily negated. The author then identifies two types of exclusivity: exclusivity in truth only — if one is true, the other is false; and exclusivity in falsehood only — if one is false, the other is true. Each type yields two conclusions (e.g., true → false, false → true). When both types apply together, there are four possible conclusions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mutual exclusivity only applies to truth values, meaning if one is true the other must be false.
Correction: Al-Iji shows there is also exclusivity in falsehood: if one is false, the other must be true. This treats both truth and falsehood symmetrically.

Checkpoint
When two matters are mutually exclusive in both truth and falsehood, how many conclusions are possible?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Six

Key Takeaway
Mutual exclusivity, whether in truth or falsehood, yields logical implications that are the foundation of many arguments in kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Iji teaches that from the truth of one, the falsehood of the other follows necessarily. In your own faith, do you treat the implications of your beliefs as necessary? For example, if you truly believe that Allah is the only Creator, do you necessarily reject all false deities, or do you sometimes hesitate in practice? Will you commit today to following the logical consequences of your iman without exception?
?
The passage identifies two types of exclusivity. Perhaps you have been neglecting exclusivity in falsehood: if you know a false belief leads to another being true, have you actively pursued that truth? Are you willing to use this method to examine your own assumptions and accept where logic leads, even if it means admitting a cherished idea is false? What step will you take today to apply this in your search for knowledge?

The Two Weak Methods and Their Rationale
Al-Jurjani identifies two weak methods and explains their nature and rationale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the two weak methods used by some mutakallimun are, why they are considered weak in matter, and why they are specifically mentioned despite the abundance of weak methods.

Core Idea
These methods are weak in matter, not form, yet frequently cited.

In this passage, al-Jurjani discusses two methods that some theologians rely on for rational proofs, even though they are considered weak. These methods are not outside the standard logical forms but are weak due to the specific premises used. He explains why they are singled out for mention, addressing a potential objection. As you read, notice how the commentator distinguishes between formal strength and material weakness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Get a sense of the author's flow.
2
Identify the two weak methods

The first is a form of qiyas, the second is tamthil.

المقصد الخامس : ماهي الطرق القوية ] وهنا طريقان ضعيفان يسلكهما بعض المتكلمين في إثبات مطالبهم العقلية ( الأول ( أنهم إذا حاولوا نفي شيء غير معلوم الثبوت بالضرورة . قوله : وهاهنا طريقان إلخ) ليس هذان الطريقان خارجين عن الطرق المذكورة لأن الأول شكل أول مخصوص والثاني مخصوص فقوله : وهاهنا طريقان بمنزلة الاستثناء من الطرق السابقة فإن قيل : ضعفهما إنما هو من حيث المادة أما الأول فلضعف صغراه وكبراه وأما الثاني فلعدم الجامع والطرق الضعيفة من حيث المادة كثيرة فلم خصهما بالذكر قلت : لتمسك البعض بهما وجريانهما في صور كثيرة وإليه أشار الشارح بقوله يسلكهما بعض المتكلمين . قوله : ( وهاهنا طريقان ضعيفان ) لايذهب عليك أن هذين الطريقين لا يخرجان عما مر من الطرق لأن الطريق الأول قياس بل شكل أول والطريق الثاني قياس فقهي أي تمثيل لكن لما كان هذان الطريقان باعتبار خصوص مقدمات مخصوصة أمراً ممتازاً عما عداها عدا طريقين آخرين
The fifth aim: What are the strong methods? And here are two weak methods that some mutakallimun use in proving their rational claims. The first: that when they attempt to negate something not known by necessity... His saying: 'and here are two methods etc.' — these two methods are not outside the methods mentioned, because the first is a specific first figure and the second is specific. So his saying 'and here are two methods' is like an exception from the previous methods. If it is said: their weakness is only in terms of matter; as for the first, due to the weakness of its minor and major, and as for the second, due to the lack of a middle term; and weak methods in terms of matter are many, so why single them out? I say: because some adhere to them and they occur in many cases, and the commentator pointed to this by his saying 'some mutakallimun use them.' His saying: 'and here are two weak methods' — do not overlook that these two methods do not exit from the methods that have passed, because the first method is a syllogism, indeed a first figure, and the second is a juristic syllogism, i.e., analogy. However, because these two methods, in consideration of their specific premises, are distinct from others, they are considered two other methods.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage introduces the fifth aim of the chapter: 'What are the strong methods?' but immediately mentions two weak methods used by some mutakallimun. The commentary clarifies that these weak methods are not entirely new; the first is a specific form of the first figure syllogism, and the second is a specific form (likely juristic analogy). The commentator explains they are singled out because some scholars adhere to them and they occur frequently. He also addresses an objection: weakness in matter is common, so why mention these two? The answer is that people rely on them in many cases. The commentary further emphasizes that these two methods do not fall outside the previously discussed strong methods but are distinguished by their specific premises.

The Two Weak Methods in Kalam
Al-Iji enumerates and critiques two weak logical methods used in kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two weak methods that some mutakallimun use in their reasoning and why they are considered weak in kalam methodology.

Core Idea
Two formally valid but materially weak methods: negating by lack of evidence and requiring negation of the un-evidenced.

In this passage, al-Iji identifies two methods used by some theologians that appear logically valid but are ultimately weak in establishing rational truths. These methods rely on the absence of evidence and the principle that unproven claims must be false. Let's explore how they work and why they are criticized.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get an overview of the two weak methods. Note that the passage includes both the methods and a commentary explaining them.
The passage is from al-Mawaqif; the commentary is by al-Jurjani.
2
Identify the two weak methods
Find where al-Iji introduces the first method (negation by claiming no evidence) and the second method (obligation to negate what has no evidence). Notice how each is defined.
3
Understand the first method's proof
The first method proves 'no evidence' either by refuting all proofs for the opposite or by enumerating all possible proofs and finding none. Note the criticism that enumeration adds unnecessary effort.
This method is weak because absence of evidence does not prove non-existence.
4
Understand the second method's justification
The second method argues that without the obligation to negate the unproven, necessary truths would be negated. This is a reductio ad absurdum. Note the qualification 'not known by necessity' to protect necessary truths.
5
Reflect on why these methods are weak
The commentary explains that these methods are formally valid but materially unsound; they are weak in content, not form. Think about why reliance on absence of evidence is problematic in theological reasoning.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الخامس : ماهي الطرق القوية وهنا طريقان ضعيفان يسلكهما بعض المتكلمين في إثبات مطالبهم العقلية ( الأول ( أنهم إذا حاولوا نفي شيء غير معلوم الثبوت بالضرورة قالوا : لا دليل عليه فيجب نفيه أما الأول ) وهو أنه لا دليل عليه ( فيثبت تارة بنقل أدلة المثبتين لذلك الشيء ) وبيان ضعفها وفسادها مع عدم وجدان دليل سواها ( وأخرى بحصر وجوه الأدلة ثم نفيها أي نفي الوجوه كلها (بالاستقراء) أي تتبعناها فلم نجد هاهنا شيئاً منها ( وهو عائد إلى الأول ) إذ ماله إلى عدم الوجدان ( مع مزيد مؤنة ) هو بيان حصر وجوه الأدلة فالتمسك بالأول أولى لتسقط هذه المؤنة ( وأما الثاني) وهو أن كل ما لا دليل عليه يجب نفيه فيثبتونه بوجهين أشار إلى الأول بقوله : ( فإذ (لولاه أي لولا وجوب نفي ما لا دليل عليه ( انتفت الضروريات ثم قال الشارح: قوله : ( في إثبات مطالبهم (العقلية أي التي يطلب فيها اليقين كالمسائل الاعتقادية بخلاف المطالب التي يكتفى فيها بالظن كالمسائل العملية فإنهما ليسا بضعيفين فيها، أما الثاني فلأنه إحدى الأدلة الشرعية، وأما الأول فلأنه لو جوز ثبوت حكم شرعي لا دليل عليه شرعاً لزم جواز إثبات للشرع بالرأي. قوله : ( غير معلوم الثبوت (بالضرورة المراد بها ما يقابل النظر أي إذا حاولوا نفي شيء نظري الثبوت ولولا التقييد بذلك لانتقض الدليل المذكور بالضروريات لأنه يصدق عليها أنه لا دليل على الضروري وإلا لكان نظرياً، وما لا دليل عليه يجب نفيه، فيجب نفي الضروريات وهو باطل قوله : ( إذ مآله إلى عدم الوجدان أي مآل الأول إلى عدم الوجدان وإبطال أدلة المثبتين إنما هو لتوقف عدم الوجدان عليه إذ مع صحتهما يشعر عدم الوجدان فالدليل في الحقيقة هو عدم الوجدان بخلاف حصر وجوه الأدلة، إذ لا تعلق ولا توقف لعدم الوجدان عليه، فهو مؤنة زائدة فالأولى تركه والاكتفاء بعدم الوجدان . قوله : (انتفت الضروريات لأنه لا دليل على خلافها، وإلا لم يكن الضروري علماً فضلاً عن أنه ضروري فلو جوز ثبوت ما لا دليل عليه جوز ثبوت خلاف كل ضروري فانتفت الضروريات بأسرها
The fifth objective: What are the strong methods? And here there are two weak methods which some mutakallimun use in proving their intellectual demands. The first: that when they attempt to negate something whose existence is not known by necessity (ḍarūra), they say: there is no evidence for it, so its negation is obligatory. As for the first (i.e., that there is no evidence for it), it is established sometimes by transmitting the proofs of those who affirm that thing and showing their weakness and corruption, along with not finding any evidence besides them; and sometimes by enumerating the types of evidence and then negating them, i.e., negating all the types, by induction (istiqrāʼ), meaning we have examined them and found none of them here. And this reverts to the first (i.e., not finding evidence), since its outcome is not finding, with additional effort. The explanation of the enumeration of the types of evidence—so adhering to the first is more appropriate to avoid this extra effort. As for the second: that everything for which there is no evidence must be negated. They prove it by two aspects; he indicated the first by his saying: for if not for it (i.e., if not for the obligation to negate what has no evidence), the necessary truths (ḍarūriyyāt) would be negated. Then the commentator said: His statement: 'in proving their intellectual demands' means those in which certainty is sought, like creedal issues, unlike issues where probability suffices, like practical issues—for these two methods are not weak in them. As for the second, because it is one of the sharʻī proofs; as for the first, because if one allowed the existence of a sharʻī ruling for which there is no sharʻī proof, it would necessitate allowing the establishment of the Law by opinion. His statement: 'whose existence is not known by necessity'—what is meant by necessity is that which is opposed to theoretical reasoning (naẓar); i.e., when they attempt to negate a theoretically established thing. Were it not for this qualification, the mentioned proof would be refuted by necessary truths, because it is true of them that there is no proof for the necessary (otherwise it would be theoretical), and what has no proof must be negated; thus necessary truths would have to be negated, which is false. His statement: 'since its outcome is not finding'—meaning the outcome of the first is not finding; and the refutation of the proofs of the affirmers is only to make not finding dependent on it, because if those proofs are valid, not finding is not sensed; so the proof in reality is not finding, unlike the enumeration of types of evidence, for not finding has no relation or dependence on it; so it is extra effort, so it is better to abandon it and suffice with not finding. His statement: 'the necessary truths would be negated'—because there is no proof for their opposites; otherwise it would not be necessary knowledge (ʻilm), let alone necessary. So if one allows the existence of what has no evidence, one allows the existence of the opposite of every necessary truth, resulting in the negation of all necessary truths.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the two weak methods (الطريقان الضعيفان) used by some mutakallimun in proving their intellectual demands. It first states that there are two weak methods, then describes each. The first method: when attempting to negate something not known by necessity, they say 'there is no evidence for it, so its negation is obligatory.' The proof of no evidence is established either by refuting all alleged proofs for the opposite, or by enumerating all possible types of evidence and finding none. The latter, while reverting to the first approach, adds unnecessary effort. The second method: the principle that everything without evidence must be negated; they argue that otherwise necessary truths would be negated, because necessary truths have no proof for their opposites. The commentary then clarifies key points: these methods are weak only in matter (content) not in form, and they are used specifically for certain intellectual (creedal) demands, not practical ones. The qualification 'not known by necessity' prevents the argument from destroying necessary truths. The first method's reliance on 'not finding evidence' is contrasted with the more burdensome enumeration method. Finally, the commentary defends the second method by showing that if undemonstrated things were allowed to exist, then the opposites of necessary truths could exist, leading to the negation of all necessary knowledge—a reductio ad absurdum.

Key Terms
طريق ضعيف
مطلب عقلي
ضرورة
دليل
نفي
استقراء
ضروريات
نظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These methods are weak in logical form (i.e., invalid syllogisms).
Correction: The methods are formally valid; their weakness lies in the material content (the premises are not reliably established). They are weak because absence of evidence does not prove non-existence.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentary say the first method's use of enumeration ('حصْر وجوه الأدلة') is inferior to simply not finding evidence?
A) Because enumeration is impossible in practice.
B) Because enumeration is logically invalid.
C) Because enumeration adds unnecessary effort while its outcome reverts to not finding evidence.
D) Because enumeration only works for necessary truths.

Key Takeaway
These two methods are logically valid but unsound because they rely on the absence of evidence, which does not necessarily indicate non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a Muslim viewpoint simply because you 'hadn't seen evidence' for it? This lesson shows that is a weak method. Which specific belief do you need to investigate more deeply rather than assume false?
?
The second method claims that without the principle 'no evidence implies negation', all necessary truths would collapse. Consider your own foundation: are there core Islamic truths you hold only because you assume absence of counter-evidence? What would happen if you applied the weak method to those themselves?
?
When you argue against someone's position, do you often rely on 'there is no evidence for that' as a conclusive defeat? This lesson calls that weak. Next time you are in a debate, will you commit to seeking positive evidence rather than resting on absence?
