The Necessity of Active Refutation
Commentator explains the contradiction and active refutation condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the ignorant must actively observe and invalidate proofs for existence, and how failure to do so leads to logical contradictions.

Core Idea
Mere unawareness of evidence does not make belief in non-existence knowledge.

Sanusi's commentator opens by showing that if the ignorant's belief were considered knowledge, then the knowledgeable's belief would be ignorance, creating a contradiction of opposites. He then clarifies that this contradiction only arises when the ignorant has actively refuted the proof—mere lack of awareness is insufficient.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to get the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the shift from the initial contradiction to the condition of active refutation.
2
Identify the two possible contradictions
The passage mentions two logical consequences if the ignorant's belief were knowledge: truth of two opposites or conjunction of two opposites. Note how each is derived.
This is the core logical structure.
3
Understand the condition for the ignorant's belief to be knowledge
The commentator states that the ignorant must 'observe the proof of existence and invalidate it in reality.' Mere lack of awareness is not enough. Why is this important?
This condition prevents the collapse of knowledge into ignorance.
4
Reflect on the 'sanad' response
The passage briefly mentions an answer involving the chain (sanad). Consider what this adds to the argument.
The chain refers to the transmission of proofs in disputation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( كان اعتقاد العالم بثبوته إلخ لأنه فرض أن ما لا دليل عليه عند شخص يجب نفيه، فلو لم يكن اعتقاد العالم جهلاً، يلزم حقية النقيضين ولو نظر إلى أن اعتقاد العالم علم في نفس الأمر، يلزم اجتماع النقيضين . قوله : ( وفي نهاية العقول إلخ إشارة إلى أنه يمكن حمل عبارة المتن على إبطال شقي 

قوله : ( فإن اعتقاد الجاهل بانتفائه لعدم الدليل عنده لما كان علماً إلخ) فيه بحث لأنه علم مما ذكره في صدر هذا الطريق أنه لا بد فيه من ملاحظة أدلة الثبوت بأحد الطريقين، ثم نفيها كما قررناه آنفاً ولا يكفي عدم الشعور بالدلائل بالمرة، ففي هذه الصور أعني فيما علم العالم دليلاً على ثبوت شيء لم يتحقق انتفاء الدليل عند الجاهل، ليكون اعتقاده بنفي ذلك الشيء علماً، وإنما يتحقق إذا لاحظ دليل العالم المثبت، وأبطله في نفس الأمر وهذا الإبطال لا يتأتى في نفس الأمر وإلا لما كان المثبت عالماً وقد يجاب بأنه كلام على السند لأن قوله وإلا لزم في قوة السند، فالمنع بحاله ولك أن تقول : المراد بعلم العالم بالثبوت اعتقاده المطابق للواقع، وهذا الاعتقاد قد يكون ناشئاً عن دليل ضعيف كأدلة أهل الحق ،الضعيفة فإذا كان إبطال الجاهل هذا الدليل الضعيف يفيد العلم له بمعنى الاعتقاد المطابق كان اعتقاد العالم جهلاً غير مطابق للواقع، فيتم الكلام ثم إن القول بلزوم كون اعتقاد العالم جهلاً من كون اعتقاد الجاهل علماً كلام تحقيقي، إذ لا احتمال لعلمية الاعتقادين المذكورين بمعنى مطابقتهما للواقع، فلا يرد أن هذا إنما يصح أن لو كان المتكلم المستدل بالطريق المذكور منكراً لكون ما يستند إلى العلم بالدليل علماً، وهو ظاهر البطلان للزوم نفي الصانع ووحدته إلى غير ذلك، وذلك لأن المراد لزوم هذا المحذور في الواقع لا التزامه، ثم إن اللازم في التحقيق وإن كان جهلية أحد الاعتقادين لا على التعيين بناء على أن المستدل المذكور لا ينكر علمية الحاصل عقيب العلم بالدليل، بل يقول بأنينية الطريق الموصل إليه لكن يمكن في طريق المناظرة إلزام جهلية كل منهما بخصوصه قوله : ( وفي نهاية العقول إلخ) فإن قلت : عبارة المصنف صالحة لأن تحمل على ما يفهم من عبارة نهاية العقول بأن يرجع ضمير أنه إلى الدليل فلم أرجعه إلى العلم بالدليل، قلت : لأن
His statement: 'the knowledgeable's belief in its existence, etc.' because it was assumed that whatever has no evidence for a person must be denied. If the knowledgeable's belief were not ignorance, then the truth of two opposites would follow, and if one considers that the knowledgeable's belief is knowledge in reality, then the conjunction of two opposites would follow. His statement: 'and in Nihayat al-Uqul, etc.' is an indication that the expression of the text can be interpreted as invalidating both sides.

His statement: 'For if the ignorant's belief in its non-existence due to lack of evidence with him is knowledge, etc.' There is investigation in it, because it is known from what he mentioned at the beginning of this method that it is necessary to observe the proofs of existence by one of the two ways, then deny them as we established earlier. And mere lack of awareness of proofs at all is not sufficient. In these cases, i.e., where the knowledgeable has evidence for the existence of something, the absence of evidence for the ignorant is not verified so that his belief in the non-existence of that thing would be knowledge. It is only verified if he observes the knowledgeable's affirmative proof and invalidates it in reality. This invalidation cannot occur in reality, for otherwise the affirmer would not be knowledgeable. And it may be answered that it is speech about the chain (sanad), because his statement 'otherwise it would follow' is in the strength of the chain, so the denial remains. However, you may say: What is meant by the knowledgeable's knowledge of existence is his belief that conforms to reality, and this belief may arise from a weak proof, like the weak proofs of the people of truth. If the ignorant's invalidation of this weak proof yields knowledge for him in the sense of a belief conforming to reality, then the knowledgeable's belief would be ignorance not conforming to reality, so the speech is complete. Then the statement that the knowledgeable's belief is ignorance necessarily follows from the ignorant's belief being knowledge is a precise statement, because there is no possibility of both said beliefs being knowledge in the sense of conforming to reality. So it is not objected that this is only valid if the speaker using the mentioned method denies that what is based on knowledge of the proof is knowledge, which is clearly false because it necessitates denying the Maker, His oneness, etc. That is because what is meant is the necessary occurrence of this absurdity in reality, not his commitment to it. Then the necessary consequence in investigation, although it is the ignorance of one of the two beliefs not determined, based on the fact that the mentioned user of the method does not deny the knowledge that results from knowledge of the proof, but rather says that the method leading to it is invalid; however, in the way of disputation it is possible to make each of them specifically ignorant. His statement: 'and in Nihayat al-Uqul, etc.' If you say: The author's expression is suitable to be interpreted as understood from the expression of Nihayat al-Uqul by making the pronoun 'it' refer to the evidence; then why did you make it refer to knowledge of the evidence? I say: Because
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the earlier matn. The commentator first explains that if the ignorant's belief in non-existence were considered knowledge, then the knowledgeable's belief in existence would be ignorance, leading to logical contradictions (truth of two opposites or conjunction of opposites). He then investigates the requirement that the ignorant must actively observe and refute the proofs of existence; mere unawareness is insufficient. The discussion considers the possibility that the knowledgeable's belief is based on a weak proof; if the ignorant refutes that weak proof, then his belief becomes conformable to reality and the knowledgeable's belief becomes ignorance. The commentator then addresses an objection: the argument only works if one denies that knowledge resulting from evidence is valid, which is false. He clarifies that the absurdity is in reality, not in the disputant's commitment. Finally, there is a question about why the pronoun in the passage is taken to refer to knowledge of the evidence rather than evidence itself, with the answer implied (but cut off).

Correction: The commentary shows that one must actively observe and invalidate the proof for existence.

What is required for the ignorant's belief in non-existence to be considered knowledge?

B) He must observe and invalidate the proof for existence
C) He must simply ignore the scholar's claim

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Weak Proofs and the Objection to the Argument
Commentator discusses weak proofs, answers objection, and clarifies pronoun reference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator addresses the objection that the argument only works if one denies knowledge from evidence, and how weak proofs play a role.

Core Idea
Even if the knowledgeable relies on a weak proof, the ignorant must refute that specific proof to gain knowledge.

The commentator now considers a scenario where the knowledgeable's belief is based on a weak proof. If the ignorant successfully refutes that weak proof, then the ignorant's belief becomes knowledge and the knowledgeable's belief becomes ignorance. He then answers an objection: this argument does not require denying that knowledge from evidence is valid; it merely shows what would necessarily happen in reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the shift to the weak proof scenario and the objection.
The passage is denser; take your time.
2
Understand the weak proof scenario
The commentator says that the knowledgeable's belief may come from a weak proof. If the ignorant refutes that weak proof, then the ignorant's belief is true and the knowledgeable's is false.
This shows that the argument does not require the knowledgeable's proof to be strong.
3
Analyze the objection and response
The objection is that the argument only works if the speaker denies that knowledge from evidence is valid. The commentator responds that it's about what would happen in reality, not the speaker's commitment.
The absurdity (ignorance of one of the two) is a necessary consequence in reality.
4
Examine the pronoun reference question
The passage ends with a question about why the pronoun refers to 'knowledge of the evidence' rather than 'evidence' itself. Consider the implications for the argument.
This highlights the precision needed in theological disputation.

Pronoun Reference and the Refutation Structure
The commentator resolves pronoun reference and explains refutation by absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator resolves the pronoun reference and structures the refutation by absurdity.

Core Idea
The pronoun's referent changes the logical force of the argument.

The commentary begins by clarifying a subtle point of pronoun reference in the original argument. This seemingly minor decision affects how the refutation is understood. The commentator then shows how the conjunction and refutation work together to uphold the infinity argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage focusing on the pronoun reference and the subsequent refutation.
Notice that the commentator is replying to a matn.
2
Identify the two possible referents
The pronoun 'it' could refer to the evidence itself or to knowledge of the evidence. Find why the commentator prefers the latter.
3
Understand the conjunction and refutation
The phrase 'and also if it is correct' is conjoined to the earlier answer 'prevention', forming a refutation by absurdity.
4
See how the objection is parried
The commentator counters an objection by showing that absence of evidence for existence is itself evidence for non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الترديد، بأن يرجع ضمير أنه إلى الدليل لا إلى العلم كما وقع في النهاية، وإنما اختار أولاً إرجاعه إلى العلم بالدليل لأن تعلق العلاوة بالشق الثاني أظهر لأنه أقرب . قوله: (وأيضاً إن صح إلخ) عطف على قوله والجواب وهو منع، وهذا نقض باستلزامه المحال . قوله : ( يستلزم إلخ ) إذ لا فرق بينهما، فإن كلاً منهما عدم دليل على أحد النقيضين، فلو استلزم أحدهما العلم بالانتفاء استلزم الآخر العلم بالثبوت بقرينة أن كونه دليل الانتفاء متنازع فيه، والمجيب بصدد إبطاله فلا يرد أنه إذا كان أحد أدلة النفي عدم دليل الثبوت لايمكن عدم دليل الطرفين لأنه إذا لم يتحقق دليل الثبوت تحقق دليل الانتفاء وهو عدم دليل الثبوت . قوله : ( لايقال إلخ ) إبداء للفارق بينهما بطريق الآن .، رد الشق الثاني من شقي الترديد والملائم له أن يجعل الضمير عبارة عن العلم بالدليل الكلام في لا عن نفس الدليل كما لا يخفى . قوله : (لا) يتوقف على هذه المقدمة، وإلا لكان نظرياً فيه بحث لجواز أن لا يكون التوقف بطريق النظر كما في الفطريات والتجربيات والحدسيات ونحوها على ما سيجيء. قوله : فيلزم من عدم دليل الطرفين إلخ) فيه بحث إذ لا يعقل عدم دليل الطرفين على تقدير صحة ما ذكر من أن عدم الدليل على الثبوت يستلزم العلم بالعدم، حتى يرد المحذور في ذلك، لأن كل أمر إما أن يتحقق دليل ثبوته أم لا، وعلى كل تقدير يتحقق دليل أحد الطرفين، أما على الأول فظاهر وأما على الثاني فلان انتفاء دليل الثبوت دليل العدم.
The disjunction, by making the pronoun 'it' refer to the evidence, not to knowledge, as occurred in al-Nihaya. He initially chose to refer it to knowledge of the evidence because the attachment of the additional remark to the second side is more apparent since it is closer. His statement: 'And also if it is correct, etc.' is conjoined to his statement 'and the answer is prevention', and this is a refutation by implying absurdity. His statement: 'necessitates, etc.' because there is no difference between the two, for each is absence of evidence for one of the two opposites. So if one necessitates knowledge of non-existence, the other necessitates knowledge of existence, by the indication that its being evidence for non-existence is disputed, and the respondent is in the process of invalidating it. So it is not objected that if one of the proofs of negation is absence of evidence for existence, then absence of evidence for both sides is impossible, because if evidence for existence is not realized, then evidence for non-existence is realized, which is absence of evidence for existence. His statement: 'It cannot be said, etc.' is a demonstration of the distinction between them by way of the now (al-ān). He repudiates the second side of the disjunction, and the appropriate thing is to make the pronoun refer to knowledge of the evidence, speaking about it, not about the evidence itself, as is not hidden. His statement: 'does not depend on this premise, otherwise it would be theoretical' has investigation in it, because it is possible that the dependence is not by way of theoretical reasoning, as in innate (fiṭriyyāt), experiential (tajribiyyāt), and intuitive (ḥadsiyyāt) and similar, as will come. His statement: 'so from absence of evidence for both sides, etc.' has investigation in it, because absence of evidence for both sides is inconceivable given the truth of what was mentioned that absence of evidence for existence necessitates knowledge of non-existence, such that the absurdity would occur. Because every matter either has evidence for its existence or not; in either case, evidence for one of the two sides is realized. As for the first, it is apparent; as for the second, because the non-existence of evidence for existence is evidence of non-existence.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the preceding matn. It begins by discussing the pronoun reference: the earlier passage in Nihayat al-Uqul had the pronoun refer to evidence itself, but the commentator chooses to refer it to knowledge of the evidence for clarity. He then explains the conjunction: 'And also if it is correct' is connected to the earlier 'answer is prevention', and this is a refutation by absurdity. The commentator argues that there is no difference between the two forms of absence of evidence, so if one entails knowledge of non-existence, the other entails knowledge of existence. He addresses a potential objection: that absence of evidence for both sides is impossible because absence of evidence for existence is itself evidence for non-existence. Therefore, the mutual implication argument does not lead to affirming both opposites. The objection about prophecy is then addressed as a demonstration of the distinction between the two cases. Finally, the commentator discusses the statement about the premise being unnecessary: it does not mean that the knowledge is theoretical, as it could be innate, experiential, or intuitive. The impossibility of absence of evidence for both sides is reaffirmed.

Key Terms
ترديد
ضمير
عطف
نقض
دليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The pronoun reference is a trivial grammatical detail with no impact on the argument.
Correction: The commentator shows that the referent determines whether the argument is about evidence or knowledge of evidence, affecting the refutation structure.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why does the commentator choose to refer the pronoun to knowledge of the evidence rather than the evidence itself?
A) Because it appears in al-Nihaya
B) Because it makes the additional remark clearer
C) Because it avoids a contradiction
D) Because it is more common

Key Takeaway
The pronoun's referent determines the logical structure of the refutation, making the choice between evidence and knowledge of evidence crucial.

?

?

?
If you were to apply this level of precision to your own reasoning about Allah's attributes, would your arguments hold up? What is one area where you rely on vague evidence rather than clear proof?

Absence of Evidence and Types of Knowledge
The commentator discusses the non-theoretical nature of knowledge and proves impossibility of double absence of evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the absence of evidence for both sides is impossible and how knowledge can be non-theoretical.

Core Idea
Absence of evidence for existence is itself evidence for non-existence.

The commentator continues by addressing a potential objection: that the argument requires a premise that would make the knowledge theoretical. He clarifies that the knowledge can be innate, experiential, or intuitive, not necessarily theoretical. He then reaffirms that the case of absence of evidence for both sides is impossible because absence of evidence for existence is itself evidence for non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Focus on the discussion of the premise and the impossibility of double absence.
2
Understand the objection about the premise
The commentator addresses the claim that the knowledge depends on a premise, which would make it theoretical.
3
See how the commentator broadens the types of knowledge
He mentions innate (fiṭriyyāt), experiential (tajribiyyāt), and intuitive (ḥadsiyyāt) knowledge as non-theoretical.
4
Grasp the impossibility of double absence
The commentator argues that for any matter, either evidence for its existence exists or not; in either case, evidence for one side is realized.

الترديد، بأن يرجع ضمير أنه إلى الدليل لا إلى العلم كما وقع في النهاية، وإنما اختار أولاً إرجاعه إلى العلم بالدليل لأن تعلق العلاوة بالشق الثاني أظهر لأنه أقرب . قوله: (وأيضاً إن صح إلخ) عطف على قوله والجواب وهو منع، وهذا نقض باستلزامه المحال . قوله : ( يستلزم إلخ ) إذ لا فرق بينهما، فإن كلاً منهما عدم دليل على أحد النقيضين، فلو استلزم أحدهما العلم بالانتفاء استلزم الآخر العلم بالثبوت بقرينة أن كونه دليل الانتفاء متنازع فيه، والمجيب بصدد إبطاله فلا يرد أنه إذا كان أحد أدلة النفي عدم دليل الثبوت لايمكن عدم دليل الطرفين لأنه إذا لم يتحقق دليل الثبوت تحقق دليل الانتفاء وهو عدم دليل الثبوت . قوله : ( لايقال إلخ ) إبداء للفارق بينهما بطريق الآن .، رد الشق الثاني من شقي الترديد والملائم له أن يجعل الضمير عبارة عن العلم بالدليل الكلام في لا عن نفس الدليل كما لا يخفى . قوله : (لا) يتوقف على هذه المقدمة، وإلا لكان نظرياً فيه بحث لجواز أن لا يكون التوقف بطريق النظر كما في الفطريات والتجربيات والحدسيات ونحوها على ما سيجيء. قوله : فيلزم من عدم دليل الطرفين إلخ) فيه بحث إذ لا يعقل عدم دليل الطرفين على تقدير صحة ما ذكر من أن عدم الدليل على الثبوت يستلزم العلم بالعدم، حتى يرد المحذور في ذلك، لأن كل أمر إما أن يتحقق دليل ثبوته أم لا، وعلى كل تقدير يتحقق دليل أحد الطرفين، أما على الأول فظاهر وأما على الثاني فلان انتفاء دليل الثبوت دليل العدم.
The disjunction, by making the pronoun 'it' refer to the evidence, not to knowledge, as occurred in al-Nihaya. He initially chose to refer it to knowledge of the evidence because the attachment of the additional remark to the second side is more apparent since it is closer. His statement: 'And also if it is correct, etc.' is conjoined to his statement 'and the answer is prevention', and this is a refutation by implying absurdity. His statement: 'necessitates, etc.' because there is no difference between the two, for each is absence of evidence for one of the two opposites. So if one necessitates knowledge of non-existence, the other necessitates knowledge of existence, by the indication that its being evidence for non-existence is disputed, and the respondent is in the process of invalidating it. So it is not objected that if one of the proofs of negation is absence of evidence for existence, then absence of evidence for both sides is impossible, because if evidence for existence is not realized, then evidence for non-existence is realized, which is absence of evidence for existence. His statement: 'It cannot be said, etc.' is a demonstration of the distinction between them by way of the now (al-ān). He repudiates the second side of the disjunction, and the appropriate thing is to make the pronoun refer to knowledge of the evidence, speaking about it, not about the evidence itself, as is not hidden. His statement: 'does not depend on this premise, otherwise it would be theoretical' has investigation in it, because it is possible that the dependence is not by way of theoretical reasoning, as in innate (fiṭriyyāt), experiential (tajribiyyāt), and intuitive (ḥadsiyyāt) and similar, as will come. His statement: 'so from absence of evidence for both sides, etc.' has investigation in it, because absence of evidence for both sides is inconceivable given the truth of what was mentioned that absence of evidence for existence necessitates knowledge of non-existence, such that the absurdity would occur. Because every matter either has evidence for its existence or not; in either case, evidence for one of the two sides is realized. As for the first, it is apparent; as for the second, because the non-existence of evidence for existence is evidence of non-existence.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the preceding matn. It begins by discussing the pronoun reference: the earlier passage in Nihayat al-Uqul had the pronoun refer to evidence itself, but the commentator chooses to refer it to knowledge of the evidence for clarity. He then explains the conjunction: 'And also if it is correct' is connected to the earlier 'answer is prevention', and this is a refutation by absurdity. The commentator argues that there is no difference between the two forms of absence of evidence, so if one entails knowledge of non-existence, the other entails knowledge of existence. He addresses a potential objection: that absence of evidence for both sides is impossible because absence of evidence for existence is itself evidence for non-existence. Therefore, the mutual implication argument does not lead to affirming both opposites. The objection about prophecy is then addressed as a demonstration of the distinction between the two cases. Finally, the commentator discusses the statement about the premise being unnecessary: it does not mean that the knowledge is theoretical, as it could be innate, experiential, or intuitive. The impossibility of absence of evidence for both sides is reaffirmed.

Key Terms
فطريات
تجربيات
حدسيات
دليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mutual implication argument requires theoretical reasoning, and absence of evidence for both sides is a possible scenario.
Correction: The commentator shows that the knowledge can be non-theoretical, and absence of evidence for both sides is impossible because absence of evidence for existence is evidence for non-existence.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what is the proof that there cannot be absence of evidence for both sides?
A) Because every matter has evidence for its existence
B) Because absence of evidence for existence is evidence for non-existence
C) Because we have innate knowledge of everything
D) Because the argument is only about infinite regresses

Key Takeaway
Every proposition either has evidence for its truth or its falsity, because absence of evidence for truth is itself evidence for falsity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of something you claim to know with certainty. Is your knowledge based on theoretical reasoning or on innate/experiential/intuitive grounds? How does this affect your level of certainty? Will you now rely more on your innate knowledge?
?
Consider a negative belief you hold about yourself (e.g., 'I am not good enough') that you have no evidence for. According to this lesson, absence of evidence for existence is evidence of non-existence. What does that imply about that belief? Are you willing to reject it?
?
The lesson teaches that for every matter, evidence for one side always exists. Where in your life have you been stuck in indecision, claiming 'there is no evidence for either side'? What does this passage demand you do now regarding that decision?

The Rule of Analogy for the Infinite
The author states the rule for analogy when a definitive proof affirms impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand when analogy from finite things to infinite things is prohibited and when existence of the infinite is permitted.

Core Idea
Analogy breaks down when a definitive proof already settles the matter.

This passage from al-Mawaqif establishes a key methodological principle: the use of analogy (qiyas) depends on whether a definitive proof (qati') exists. When a definitive proof affirms the impossibility of an infinite regress, analogy from finite cases is invalid. But without such proof, you cannot simply rule out existence—it remains possible. This distinction is crucial for avoiding flawed reasoning in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the conditional structure: 'if... then...' and 'otherwise...'.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two cases
The passage presents two scenarios: (1) when a definitive proof shows impossibility, and (2) when it does not. Understand the different outcomes for each.
3
Understand the rationale
Why is analogy prohibited in the first case? Because the distinction (fariq) is clear: a definitive proof exists. In the second case, without proof, existence must be considered possible.
This prevents overgeneralization.
4
Apply to the concept of infinite regress
Consider how this rule affects arguments about infinite regress. It warns against using finite analogies when a definitive theological proof has already established impossibility.
Think of examples like arguments about the temporal beginning of the universe.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووجود ما لا نهاية له إن امتنع لقاطع دل على امتناعه امتنع القياس عليه أعني قياس ما لا دليل عليه من الأمور المتناهية التي لم يدل قاطع على امتناعها لظهور الفارق حينئذ وإلا أي وإن لم يمتنع القاطع منع الحكم الذي هو وجوب الانتفاء فيه أي فيما لا يتناهى وجوز ثبوته في نفس الأمر كسائر الأمور التي لا دليل على ثبوتها ولا قاطع يدل على امتناعها
And if the existence of that which has no end is impossible due to a definitive proof that indicates its impossibility, then analogy upon it is prohibited, meaning analogy from finite matters that have no evidence upon them and no definitive proof of their impossibility, because the distinction is apparent at that time. Otherwise, if there is no definitive proof, the judgment of necessary non-existence is prevented in it, and its existence is permitted in reality like other matters that have no evidence for their existence and no definitive proof of their impossibility.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage defines the rule regarding infinite regress. It states that if something infinite is proven impossible by a definitive proof, then you cannot use analogy from finite things to argue about it. The reason is that there is a clear distinction between the case where a definitive proof exists and where it does not. If there is no definitive proof, then you cannot claim that the infinite thing must be non-existent; rather, it could possibly exist. The author emphasizes that the judgment of necessary non-existence is only valid when there is a definitive proof.

Key Terms
ما لا نهاية له (infinite)
قاطع (definitive proof)
قياس (analogy)
امتناع (impossibility)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That lack of evidence for existence implies non-existence even when a definitive proof is absent.
Correction: The passage shows that without a definitive proof, existence is possible; you cannot claim necessary non-existence.

Checkpoint
When is analogy from finite things to infinite things prohibited according to the passage?
A) When there is no evidence at all.
B) When a definitive proof shows the infinite is impossible.
C) When the finite things are similar.
D) When the infinite is possible.

Key Takeaway
Analogical reasoning about the infinite is invalid when a definitive proof has already established impossibility; otherwise, existence remains a possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you rely on analogy to draw conclusions about matters of faith, are you making sure that no definitive proof from revelation or reason contradicts your assumption? How can you guard against overextending analogy where God has already provided certainty?
?
Consider an area of your belief where you have assumed that if something is not proven to exist, it must be impossible. How does this passage challenge you to hold open the possibility of what God has not definitively closed off?
?
Are there times when you have dismissed a theological truth because it did not align with your finite reasoning? How can you submit to definitive proofs even when they seem counterintuitive?

The Weakness of Qiyas al-Gha'ib 'ala al-Shahid
Al-Jurjani defines qiyas al-gha'ib and explains the difficulty of establishing a common cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why qiyas al-gha'ib is considered a weak method and the specific difficulty of establishing a common cause with certainty.

Core Idea
Analogy fails when certainty about the shared cause is impossible.

In this lesson, we see al-Jurjani define the second weak method of reasoning about Allah: analogy of the unseen to the witnessed. He explains that while this method resembles legal analogy, it faces a fundamental problem: the common cause cannot be established with certainty due to possible conditions in the original or impediments in the branch. Understanding this critique is essential for recognizing why kalam requires a different methodology.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the method
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that the author describes this method as used by those trying to establish a ruling for Allah by analogizing Him to contingent beings.
The term 'al-gha'ib' is applied to Allah because He is hidden from the senses.
2
Grasp the two key problems
Focus on the two possibilities that undermine certainty: a particularity of the original might be a condition for the ruling, or a particularity of the branch might be a preventer. These prevent the establishment of a common cause.
The author emphasizes that this makes the method very problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق ( الثاني ) من ذينك الطريقين الضعيفين (قياس الغائب على الشاهد) وإنما يسلكونه إذا حاولوا إثبات حكم لله سبحانه، فيقيسونه على الممكنات قياساً فقهياً، ويطلقون اسم الغائب عليه تعالى لكونه غائباً عن الحواس مطلقاً ( ولا بد ) في هذا القياس بل في القياس الفقهي ) من إثبات علة مشتركة ) بين المقيس والمقيس عليه (وهو) أي هذا الإثبات بطريق اليقين (مشكل) جداً لجواز كون خصوصية (الأصل الذي هو المقيس عليه ( شرطاً ) لوجود الحكم فيه ( أو ) كون خصوصية (الفرع) الذي هو المقيس (مانعا ) من وجوده فيه، وعلى لا يثبت بينهما علة مشتركة ) ولهم فيه ) أي في إثبات العلة المشتركة وبيان عليتها للحكم ( طرق ) كثيرة مفصلة في كتب أصول الفقه ( أشهرها أمور ) ثلاثة أحدها الطرد والعكس وهو المسمى بالدوران وجوداً وعدماً، أي كلما وجد
The second method of those two weak methods is analogy of the unseen to the witnessed. They employ it only when they attempt to establish a ruling for God Almighty, so they analogize Him to contingent beings in a legal analogy, and they apply the name 'unseen' to Him because He is absolutely hidden from the senses. And in this analogy, indeed in legal analogy, it is necessary to establish a common cause between the analogized and the analogized-upon. And this, i.e., establishing it by way of certainty, is very problematic because of the possibility that a particularity of the original, which is the analogized-upon, is a condition for the existence of the ruling in it, or that a particularity of the branch, which is the analogized, is a preventer of its existence in it, and thus a common cause between them is not established. And for them, i.e., in establishing the common cause and demonstrating its causality for the ruling, there are many methods detailed in books of usul al-fiqh. The most famous of them are three things: one of them is al-tard wa al-aks, which is called al-dawran in existence and non-existence, i.e., whenever it is found...
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage defines and critiques the second weak method: qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid (analogy of the unseen to the witnessed). The author states that this method is used by those who try to establish a ruling for God by analogizing Him to contingent beings in a manner similar to legal analogy (qiyas fiqhi). They call God 'al-gha'ib' because He is hidden from the senses. The key requirement for such analogy, as in legal analogy, is the establishment of a common cause (illa mushtaraka) between the original (al-asl) and the branch (al-far'). However, establishing this common cause with certainty is extremely problematic. There are two main possibilities that prevent certainty: (1) a particularity of the original might be a condition for the ruling in that original, so the ruling does not transfer; (2) a particularity of the branch might be an impediment (mani') to the ruling in the branch, so the ruling does not apply. Because of these possibilities, a common cause cannot be established with certainty between them. Then the author mentions that there are many methods in usul al-fiqh to establish the common cause and demonstrate its causality, and the most famous are three, the first being al-tard wa al-aks (co-extensiveness and co-exclusiveness), also called al-dawran (correlation) in existence and non-existence. The passage ends mid-definition of tard.

الطرد والعكس
الدوران

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that qiyas al-gha'ib is a sound method because it resembles legal analogy, which is widely used in fiqh.
Correction: The passage highlights that the analogy fails because the common cause cannot be established with certainty due to possible conditions in the original or impediments in the branch, making the method weak.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason that qiyas al-gha'ib is considered a weak method?
A) God cannot be analogized to anything.
B) Legal analogy is not applicable to theological matters.
C) It is very difficult to establish a common cause with certainty because of possible conditions in the original or impediments in the branch.
D) The method of al-tard wa al-aks is unreliable.

Key Takeaway
Qiyas al-gha'ib is weak because establishing a certain common cause between Allah and contingent beings is problematic due to potential conditions or impediments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God, do you catch yourself unconsciously comparing Him to things you know? In what specific area of your faith (e.g., mercy, justice, power) are you most tempted to rely on analogy rather than revelation, and what is that costing you?
?
This lesson shows that certainty about God through analogy is impossible because of hidden conditions or preventers. Consider a belief you hold about God that you arrived at through reasoning alone—are you willing to submit it to the test of revelation, even if it means letting go of a comfortable understanding?
?
The passage warns that assuming a common cause between God and creation can lead to error. Is there a divine attribute you have made 'common' in your mind—thinking it works just like a human attribute? Are you ready to accept that God's attribute is utterly unique, even if that makes Him feel more distant? How will you seek intimacy with Him without relying on analogies?

The Weakness of Qiyas al-Gha'ib 'ala al-Shahid
Al-Jurjani defines the second weak method and explains its requirement for a common cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid is considered a weak method in kalam and the challenge of establishing a common cause.

Core Idea
Analogy from creation to Creator fails because we cannot prove the cause is common.

In this passage, al-Jurjani introduces the second of two weak methods used by some theologians: drawing analogies from the visible world to Allah, who is invisible to the senses. While this resembles juristic analogy, it carries a critical flaw: we can never be certain that the hidden conditions in the original (the created) or obstacles in the branch (the Creator) are absent. This lesson unpacks that problem.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Jurjani uses a very concise style.
2
Identify the key components
Find the terms 'original', 'branch', and 'common cause' in the passage. Understand how the analogy is structured.
The original is the analogized-upon (contingent things), the branch is the analogized (Allah).
3
Understand the problem
Al-Jurjani says establishing the common cause is 'very problematic'. Ask yourself why. The passage gives two reasons: a particularity of the original might be a condition, or a particularity of the branch might be a hindrance.
This is the core of the weakness.
4
Reflect on the consequence
What does this mean for arguments that use this method? They cannot be certain, so they are weak. Think about why theologians still sometimes use it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق ( الثاني ) من ذينك الطريقين الضعيفين (قياس الغائب على الشاهد) وإنما يسلكونه إذا حاولوا إثبات حكم لله سبحانه، فيقيسونه على الممكنات قياساً فقهياً، ويطلقون اسم الغائب عليه تعالى لكونه غائباً عن الحواس مطلقاً ( ولا بد ) في هذا القياس بل في القياس الفقهي ) من إثبات علة مشتركة ) بين المقيس والمقيس عليه (وهو) أي هذا الإثبات بطريق اليقين (مشكل) جداً لجواز كون خصوصية (الأصل الذي هو المقيس عليه ( شرطاً ) لوجود الحكم فيه ( أو ) كون خصوصية (الفرع) الذي هو المقيس (مانعا ) من وجوده فيه، وعلى لا يثبت بينهما علة مشتركة
The second of those two weak methods is the analogy from the invisible to the visible. They only employ it when they attempt to establish a ruling for God the Exalted, so they analogize Him to contingent things by way of juristic analogy, and they apply the name 'invisible' to Him the Exalted because He is absolutely hidden from the senses. And it is necessary in this analogy—indeed in juristic analogy—to establish a common cause between the analogized and the analogized-upon, and this establishment (i.e., by a certain method) is very problematic because it is possible that a particularity of the original (which is the analogized-upon) is a condition for the existence of the ruling in it, or that a particularity of the branch (which is the analogized) is a hindrance to its existence in it, and so a common cause is not established between them.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage defines the second weak method of argumentation in kalam: qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid (analogy from the invisible to the visible). Al-Jurjani explains that this method is used when attempting to prove a ruling for God by analogizing Him to created things, calling Him 'invisible' because He is beyond sensory perception. The problem with this analogy is that it requires a common cause (illa mushtaraka) between the original (God) and the analogized (contingent things) for the ruling to transfer. However, establishing this common cause with certainty is very difficult. The particularities of the original might be a necessary condition for the ruling, or the particularities of the branch might prevent the ruling. Thus, without establishing that no such condition or hindrance exists, the common cause remains unproven, making the analogy weak.

Key Terms
قياس الغائب على الشاهد
علة مشتركة
أصل
فرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Analogical reasoning from creation to Creator is always valid and certain.
Correction: Al-Jurjani shows it is weak because the common cause cannot be established with certainty due to possible hidden conditions or obstacles.

Checkpoint
Why is the method of qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid considered weak according to al-Jurjani?
A) Because the invisible cannot be known at all.
B) Because establishing the common cause is problematic due to possible hidden conditions or obstacles.
C) Because it relies on sense perception which is unreliable.
D) Because it is forbidden in Islamic law.

Key Takeaway
Qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid is weak because we cannot prove with certainty that the original's particularities are not conditions or the branch's particularities are not hindrances, making the common cause unestablished.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Have you ever, even unknowingly, assumed that Allah must act like a human king because that is your standard of power? This lesson shows that such analogies are flawed because they assume a common cause you cannot prove. Will you examine your own thoughts about Allah and repent from any subtle comparisons you have made?
?
When you describe Allah’s attributes, do you rely on your own reasoning or on revelation? The fact that an analogy from creation to Creator is weak warns you not to trust your own assumptions. How will you train yourself to submit to text alone?
?
What particularities of your own existence (like time, space, need) have you inadvertently projected onto Allah? The lesson warns that such conditions may be hindrances. Write down one assumption you held and now see as dangerous, and commit to replacing it with what the Qur'an and Sunnah say.

The Method of al-Tard wa al-'Aks (Co-extensiveness)
Al-Jurjani defines al-tard wa al-'aks and gives an example from the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the method of al-tard wa al-'aks, its definition, and the Mu'tazilite example of using it to prove the cause of ugliness.

Core Idea
Co-extensiveness in existence and non-existence is used to identify causes.

Al-Jurjani introduces the most famous method for establishing a common cause: al-tard wa al-'aks. This method observes that whenever a certain feature is present, the ruling is present, and when absent, the ruling is absent. The Mu'tazila applied this to prove that the ugliness of an act is caused by the presence of harm without justification. In this lesson, we explore how this method works and see it applied.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of al-tard wa al-'aks
First, read how Al-Jurjani defines the method: it is called dawaran (co-rotation) in existence and non-existence. Understand the logical structure: whenever the common feature is present, the ruling is present; whenever it is absent, the ruling is absent.
This is the core of the method.
2
Study the Mu'tazilite example
Read the example: causing harm without prior wrongdoing or subsequent compensation is ugly in human experience. Notice how the ugliness co-exists with these conditions and disappears when any condition is removed.
The example demonstrates how co-extensiveness is used in practice.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that since ugliness co-extends with these considerations, the ugliness of injustice is caused by them. Consider how this conclusion is derived from the observation of co-extensiveness.
This is the intended result of the method.

و لهم فيه ) أي في إثبات العلة المشتركة وبيان عليتها للحكم ( طرق ) كثيرة مفصلة في كتب أصول الفقه ( أشهرها أمور ) ثلاثة أحدها الطرد والعكس وهو المسمى بالدوران وجوداً وعدماً، أي كلما وجد أن ذلك المشترك وجد الحكم وكلما عدم عدم وذلك مثل ما قالت المعتزلة : من الإضرار بلا جناية سابقة، ولا عوض لاحق قبيح في الشاهد ثم إذا تأملنا وجدنا أن الفعل إذا وقع على هذه الوجوه كلها كان قبيحاً، وإذا زال عنه شيء من هذه القيود زال قبحه، فقد دار القبح مع هذه الاعتبارات وجوداً، وعدماً فعلمنا أن قبح الظلم معلل بها، فلو صدر عن الله تعالى لوجب أن يحكم بقبحه لوجود علته ( ولو صح ) ما ذكر أن الدوران يدل على علية المدار للدائر ) دل على علية المعلول) المساوي لعلته، فإن العلة دائرة معه وجوداً وعدماً، وكونه علة لها محال قطعاً، وكذا المشروط دائر كذلك مع الشرط المساوي والمعلول أيضاً دائر مع الجزء الأخير من شيء من هذين المدارين علة ،لدائره فالاستدلال بالدوران على العلية منقوض بهذه الصورة، فإن قلت : كون المدار صالحاً للعلية معتبر عندهم، وليس شيء من المدارات التي ذكرتم صالحاً لها، فلا نقض قلت : فليس الاستدلال بالدوران وحده، وأيضاً كون [؟] من العلة، وليس [؟]
And they have concerning it—i.e., concerning establishing the common cause and showing its causality for the ruling—many methods detailed in the books of principles of jurisprudence, the most famous of which are three things, one of which is al-tard wa al-'aks, which is called co-extensiveness and co-exclusiveness (al-dawaran) in existence and non-existence, meaning: whenever that commonality is found, the ruling is found, and whenever it is absent, the ruling is absent. Such as the example of the Mu'tazila: that causing harm without prior wrongdoing and without subsequent compensation is ugly in the visible realm; then if we reflect, we find that when the act occurs with all these aspects it is ugly, and when any of these restrictions is removed, its ugliness disappears. Thus ugliness co-extends with these considerations in existence and non-existence, so we know that the ugliness of injustice is caused by them. If it were to occur from God the Exalted, it would be necessary to judge its ugliness because its cause exists. And if what is mentioned were sound—that co-extensiveness indicates the causality of the co-extensive for that which co-extends—then it would indicate the causality of the effect equal to its cause, for the cause co-extends with it in existence and non-existence, and it being a cause for it is absolutely impossible. Likewise, the conditioned co-extends with the equal condition, and the effect also co-extends with the last part (of the cause). None of these two co-extensives is a cause for its co-extensive. Therefore, reasoning from co-extensiveness to causality is refuted by this picture. If you say: that the co-extensive being suitable for causality is considered by them, and none of the co-extensives you mentioned are suitable for it, so there is no refutation. I say: then reasoning is not by co-extensiveness alone, and also... from the cause, and not [؟].
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents the most famous method for proving a common cause: al-tard wa al-'aks (co-extensiveness and co-exclusiveness), also called dawaran (co-rotation) in existence and non-existence. Al-Jurjani defines it: whenever the common feature exists, the ruling exists; whenever it is absent, the ruling is absent. He gives the example of the Mu'tazila: causing harm without prior wrongdoing or subsequent compensation is deemed ugly in human experience; the ugliness co-exists with these conditions and disappears when any is removed, proving they are the cause of the ugliness. If God were to do such harm, they argue, it would be ugly. Then al-Jurjani raises a powerful objection: if co-extensiveness proves causality, then the effect would also co-extend with its cause, implying the effect is the cause of the cause—an impossibility. Similarly, a conditioned thing co-extends with its equal condition, and the effect co-extends with the last part of its cause, none of which are actual causes. Thus the argument from co-extensiveness is undermined. He then considers a potential response: that the co-extensive must be 'suitable' (salih) for causality, and the counterexamples are not suitable. Al-Jurjani replies: that means the proof does not rely on co-extensiveness alone, and the discussion continues, though the text becomes fragmented.

علة

Checkpoint
What does the method of al-tard wa al-'aks require to show that a feature is a cause?
A) The feature and the ruling must always occur together and never separately.
B) The feature must be the only possible cause.
C) The feature must be observable by the senses.
D) The feature must be mentioned in revelation.

Key Takeaway
Al-tard wa al-'aks (co-extensiveness) is a method that infers causality from the regular correlation of a feature and a ruling in existence and non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a moral judgment you hold strongly. Can you identify the specific conditions that must be present for you to maintain that judgment? If one condition were removed, would your judgment change? This is the test of co-extensiveness. Are you consistent in your moral reasoning?
?
The Mu'tazila used rational inquiry to argue about divine justice. How often do you examine the logical grounds of your beliefs about God? Are you willing to follow reason wherever it leads, even if it challenges comfortable assumptions?
?
When you see a pattern in life (e.g., every time A happens, B follows), do you automatically assume A causes B? How can the method of al-tard wa al-'aks help you become more rigorous in your thinking, and where might it fall short? Commit to examining one such correlation this week.

The Critique of al-Tard wa al-'Aks
Al-Jurjani critiques al-tard wa al-'aks by showing it leads to impossible implications and discusses the suitability condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that al-tard wa al-'aks would imply the effect is the cause of its own cause, and the response regarding suitability.

Core Idea
Co-extensiveness alone does not prove causality; it must be combined with suitability.

Al-Jurjani does not accept al-tard wa al-'aks as a conclusive proof. He raises a devastating objection: if co-extensiveness proves causality, then the effect would also be a cause of its cause. He also discusses a potential response involving the condition of suitability. This lesson examines this critical exchange.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection from circularity
Al-Jurjani argues that if co-extensiveness proves causality, then the effect, which co-extends with its cause, would also be a cause of the cause—an impossibility. Similarly, the conditioned co-extends with its condition, and the effect with the last part of the cause. None of these are actual causes.
This shows that co-extensiveness alone leads to absurd conclusions.
2
Consider the potential response: suitability
The passage considers a possible reply: that the co-extensive must be 'suitable' (salih) for causality, and the counterexamples are not suitable. This would block the objection.
Suitability is an additional condition.
3
Al-Jurjani's rejoinder
Al-Jurjani replies that if suitability is required, then the proof is not by co-extensiveness alone. The text becomes fragmentary but implies the method is still incomplete.
Thus the method needs more than co-extensiveness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The condition of suitability is added to avoid false causation. Is there a practice in your worship that you do routinely but have never asked whether it is truly suitable as a cause for drawing closer to Allah? How will you seek the 'suitability condition' from the Qur'an and Sunnah?
?
If co-extensiveness alone were sufficient, even the effect would be cause of its cause. Could it be that you have reversed the order of reliance—thinking your deeds cause Allah's mercy rather than His mercy being the cause of your ability to do good? What specific change in your dua tonight would reflect this correction?

Why al-Ilzāmāt Are Not a Separate Method
The author criticizes counting ilzāmāt among the methods for establishing causality, arguing it is a type of qiyās.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why counting al-ilzāmāt as a separate method for establishing causality is considered an oversight, and how it relates to qiyās.

Core Idea
Al-ilzāmāt are not a separate method; they are a type of qiyās.

This passage examines a methodological classification by al-Rāzī, critiquing his inclusion of ilzāmāt among the weak methods for establishing causality. The author argues that ilzāmāt are essentially a form of analogical reasoning (qiyās), and thus listing them separately is a mistake. This criticism highlights the importance of precise definitions in uṣūl al-fiqh and logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage includes both the author's critique and a counterargument.
2
Identify the author's main claim
What is the 'saḥw' (oversight) that the author points out? Find the sentence that states it directly.
The claim is that counting ilzāmāt among methods for causality is a mistake.
3
Trace the reference to al-Rāzī
The author traces the error back to al-Rāzī's classification of four weak methods. List those four methods as mentioned in the passage.
The four methods: (1) negating without proof, (2) qiyās, (3) ilzāmāt, (4) transmitted proofs.
4
Understand why ilzāmāt reduce to qiyās
The author explains that ilzāmāt only establish the branch judgment by the cause of the original, which is exactly what qiyās does. Note the phrase 'qiyās murakkab al-aṣl'.
This is the core reasoning: ilzāmāt are not a different method, just a type of qiyās.
5
Consider the counterargument
The passage presents a possible counterargument: that being a type of qiyās does not prevent it from being a distinct method. How does the author respond? (The passage is incomplete, but note the beginning of the rebuttal.)
The counterargument suggests that ilzāmāt could still be a specific method within qiyās.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن عد الإلزامات من طرق إثبات العلية سهو من المصنف لأنه قسم من القياس بلا شبهة كما تحققته وهو معترف بذلك حيث قال : وهو القياس إلى آخره وإنما وقع منه هذا السهو بناء على أن الإمام الرازي قال في النهاية الطرق الضعيفة أربعة الأول قولهم ما لا دليل عليه يجب نفيه وبين ضعفه ثم قال : الثاني القياس الذي من أنواعه رد الغائب إلى الشاهد أو بالعكس، والمقام المشكل فيه بيان كون الحكم في الأصل معللا بعلة موجودة في الفرع ولهم في بيان ذلك طرق الأول الطرد والعكس واستوفى مباحثه، ثم قال الطريق الثاني في إثبات علة الأصل في الأقيسة العقلية السبر والتقسيم وضعفه ثم قال : والثالث الإلزامات وهي بالحقيقة من أنواع القياس وأراد أن الإلزامات ثالث الطرق الأربعة الضعيفة التي جعل رابعها التمسك بالأدلة النقلية قوله : (جعل) أي الإمام وما قيل من أن كونها من القياس لا ينافي كونها طريقاً لإثبات أن يرجع التفاوت إلى الكيف قوله : ( واعلم أن عد الإلزامات إلخ إذ الإلزامات لا تزيد على إثبات الحكم في الفرع بوجود علة حكم الأصل فيه المتفق على عليتها في الأصل على زعمه على قياس القياس المركب الأصل، وإنه ليس من الطرق المثبتة للعلة المشتركة، وقد يقال : كون الإلزامات نوعاً من القياس لا ينافي اشتمالها على نوع مخصوص من أنواع طرق الإثبات للعلة المشتركة، فإن التمسك بها بناء على أن خصمه في زعمه معترف بحكم الأصل وبعليته التي يدعي المتمسك أنها علة، ولذلك لا يشتغل بإثبات علية تلك العلة بطريق آخر ، فكان اعتراف الخصم بعلية علة حكم الأصل، ولو في زعم المتمسك طريقاً ثالثاً فى إثبات علية علة القياس فيقول : الخصم ما زعمت من تعيين الحكم
Know that counting al-ilzāmāt (obligatory implications) among the methods for establishing causality is an oversight (sahw) from the author, because it is a type of qiyās without doubt as I have verified, and he himself admits that by saying 'and it is qiyās etc.' This oversight occurred because Imām al-Rāzī said in al-Nihāya: 'The weak methods are four: first, their saying 'what has no proof must be negated,' and he showed its weakness. Then he said: second, qiyās, which includes analogies from the absent to the present or vice versa, and the problem there is establishing that the judgment in the original is caused by a cause found in the branch, and they have methods to show that: first, al-ṭard wa al-ʿaks (co-extensiveness and co-exclusiveness), and he exhaustively discussed it. Then he said: the second method for establishing the cause of the original in rational analogies is al-sabr wa al-taqsīm (investigation and division), and he showed its weakness. Then he said: the third is al-ilzāmāt, which are in reality a type of qiyās, and he meant that al-ilzāmāt are the third of the four weak methods, of which he made the fourth the reliance on transmitted proofs.' He said: (He made) i.e., the Imām. And what is said that its being qiyās does not prevent it from being a way to establish that the difference reverts to quality. He said: (And know that counting al-ilzāmāt etc.) For al-ilzāmāt are no more than establishing the judgment in the branch by the existence of the cause of the original judgment in it, which is agreed upon as a cause in the original according to his claim, on the model of the compound source analogy (qiyās murakkab al-aṣl), and it is not among the methods for establishing the common cause. It may be said: that al-ilzāmāt are a type of qiyās does not conflict with their comprising a specific type of methods for establishing the common cause. For reliance on them is based on the fact that the opponent, in his claim, acknowledges the original judgment and its causality which the claimant claims as the cause, and therefore he does not busy himself with establishing the causality of that cause by another method. Thus, the opponent's acknowledgment of the causality of the cause of the original judgment, even if in the claimant's claim, is a third method for establishing the causality of the cause of the analogy. He says: the opponent, what you claim concerning the specification of the judgment... (incomplete).
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage criticizes the classification of al-ilzāmāt (obligatory implications) as a separate method for establishing causality. The author argues that it is actually a type of qiyās (analogical reasoning), and therefore its inclusion among the weak methods is an oversight (sahw). The oversight arises from following al-Rāzī's classification in al-Nihāya, where weak methods are enumerated as four: (1) the claim that what lacks proof must be negated; (2) qiyās (which includes analogies from absent to present); (3) al-ilzāmāt; and (4) reliance on transmitted proofs. The commentary explains that al-ilzāmāt are nothing but establishing the judgment in the branch by the cause of the original judgment, which is in effect a compound source analogy (qiyās murakkab al-aṣl). However, some argue that being a type of qiyās does not preclude it from being a method for establishing a common cause, because the opponent's acknowledgment of the cause serves as a third method. The passage ends mid-sentence, noting that the opponent may argue about specification.

Key Terms

القياس
العلة
الطرق الضعيفة
السبر والتقسيم
الطرد والعكس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-ilzāmāt are a distinct method for establishing causality, separate from qiyās.
Correction: The author argues that ilzāmāt are actually a type of qiyās, as they involve establishing a branch judgment by the cause of the original, which is the essence of analogical reasoning.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is counting al-ilzāmāt among the methods for establishing causality an oversight?
A) Because they are too weak to be considered.
B) Because they are actually a type of qiyās.
C) Because they are not mentioned by al-Rāzī.
D) Because they are only used in fiqh.

Key Takeaway
Al-ilzāmāt are not a separate method for establishing causality; they are fundamentally a form of qiyās (analogical reasoning), and listing them separately is a methodological oversight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study a topic, do you ever accept a classification or list from a respected scholar without questioning its logical consistency? This passage shows an author correcting al-Rāzī himself. What assumptions do you hold in your own understanding that might need re-examination, and what would it take for you to admit they are flawed?
?
Have you ever been like al-Rāzī in this passage—making a confident claim that later turned out to have an oversight? How can you develop the humility to welcome correction and the courage to revise your views when presented with sound reasoning?
?
The author argues that ilzāmāt are not separate from qiyās but a subtype. Is there a belief you hold that functions like a 'separate method' in your life—something you treat as disconnected from other truths, when in fact it is deeply rooted in a larger framework? How might recognizing that connection change the way you apply that belief?

Clarifying the Status of Ilzāmāt in Proving Causality
Corrects the mistaken inclusion of ilzāmāt among methods of proving causality and explains their nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-ilzāmāt are not an independent method of proving causality but rather a type of analogical reasoning, and recognize the error of classifying them as such.

Core Idea
Al-ilzāmāt are a type of qiyās, not a separate method for proving causality.

This lesson examines a common mistake in Islamic legal theory: counting al-ilzāmāt among the methods for proving causality. The author corrects this by showing that al-ilzāmāt are inherently a form of analogical reasoning (qiyās). We will explore the source of this confusion in al-Rāzī's classification and understand the true nature of these arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening claim
The author states that including al-ilzāmāt in methods of proving causality is an oversight. Note his reasoning.
2
Examine the source of confusion
The author quotes al-Rāzī's four weak methods to show how the mistake occurred.
Al-Rāzī listed ilzāmāt as the third weak method, but not as a method for proving the common cause.
3
Consider the refutation
The author addresses a potential objection and concludes that al-ilzāmāt's weakness lies in relying on the opponent's concession.

واعلم أن عد الإلزامات من طرق إثبات العلية سهو من المصنف لأنه قسم من القياس بلا شبهة كما تحققته وهو معترف بذلك حيث قال : وهو القياس إلى آخره وإنما وقع منه هذا السهو بناء على أن الإمام الرازي قال في النهاية الطرق الضعيفة أربعة الأول قولهم ما لا دليل عليه يجب نفيه وبين ضعفه ثم قال : الثاني القياس الذي من أنواعه رد الغائب إلى الشاهد أو بالعكس، والمقام المشكل فيه بيان كون الحكم في الأصل معللا بعلة موجودة في الفرع ولهم في بيان ذلك طرق الأول الطرد والعكس واستوفى مباحثه، ثم قال الطريق الثاني في إثبات علة الأصل في الأقيسة العقلية السبر والتقسيم وضعفه ثم قال : والثالث الإلزامات وهي بالحقيقة من أنواع القياس وأراد أن الإلزامات ثالث الطرق الأربعة الضعيفة التي جعل رابعها التمسك بالأدلة النقلية في المباحث العقلية التي يطلب بها اليقين فتوهم المصنف أنه أراد ثالث الطرق المثبتة للعلة المشتركة . وقد يقال : كون الإلزامات نوعاً من القياس لا ينافي اشتمالها على نوع مخصوص من أنواع طرق الإثبات للعلة المشتركة، فإن التمسك بها بناء على أن خصمه في زعمه معترف بحكم الأصل وبعليته التي يدعي المتمسك أنها علة، ولذلك لا يشتغل بإثبات علية تلك العلة بطريق آخر ، فكان اعتراف الخصم بعلية علة حكم الأصل، ولو في زعم المتمسك طريقاً ثالثاً فى إثبات علية علة القياس فيقول : الخصم ما زعمت من تعيين الحكم وعلته عندي غير واقع، بل إن تعين الحكم فبغير تلك العلة، وإن تعين العلة فلغير ذلك الحكم، أما تغيير عبارة نهاية العقول وجعلها من طرق إثبات العلة فلعله للتنبيه على ما هو الصحيح. فإن الإلزامات من حيث هي أقيسة طردية أو عكسية ليست ضعيفة، بل ضعفها من حيث إحالة تعيين الحكم والعلة إلى الخصم ، وقد عقبه الخصم بإنكار أحدهما هذا ، وأنت بعدما علمت خلاصة الإلزامات، فكن الحاكم الفيصل. فوهم لأن مجرد زعم القائس كيف يكون طريقاً لإثبات العلية.
And know that counting al-ilzāmāt among the methods of proving causality is an oversight by the author (al-muṣannif) because it is a type of qiyās without doubt, as I have verified, and he acknowledged that where he said: 'it is qiyās' etc. This oversight occurred only because Imām al-Rāzī said in al-Nihāyah: 'The weak methods are four: first, their saying "what there is no evidence for must be negated" and he explained its weakness; then he said: second, qiyās, of which one type is returning the absent to the witnessed or vice versa, and the problematic point is the demonstration that the ruling in the original case is caused by a cause present in the branch, and they have for that demonstration methods: first, al-ṭard wa al-‘aks, and he exhaustively discussed it; then he said: the second method for proving the cause of the original in intellectual analogies is al-sabr wa al-taqsīm, and its weakness; then he said: third, al-ilzāmāt, and they are in reality a type of qiyās, and he intended that al-ilzāmāt is the third of the four weak methods, the fourth of which he made the adherence to transmitted evidences in intellectual investigations by which certainty is sought. So the author mistakenly thought that he meant the third of the methods for proving the common cause. And it may be said that the fact that al-ilzāmāt are a type of qiyās does not preclude their containing a specific type of methods for proving the common cause, because adherence to them is based on the fact that one's opponent, in his claim, acknowledges the ruling of the original and its cause which the adherent claims is the cause, and therefore he does not busy himself with proving the causality of that cause by another method. Thus the opponent's acknowledgment of the causality of the cause of the original ruling, even in the adherent's claim, is a third method for proving the causality of the cause in the qiyās, so he says: 'O opponent, what you claimed of the specification of the ruling and its cause is not real in my view; rather, if the ruling is specified, it is by a cause other than that cause, and if the cause is specified, it is for a ruling other than that ruling.' As for changing the wording of Nihāyat al-‘Uqūl and making it among the methods of proving the cause, perhaps it is to alert to what is correct. For al-ilzāmāt, inasmuch as they are analogies of inclusion or exclusion, are not weak; rather, their weakness is from the aspect of referring the specification of the ruling and the cause to the opponent, and the opponent then meets it by denying one of them. This, and after you have known the essence of al-ilzāmāt, be the decisive judge. So it is an error because how can the mere claim of the analogist be a method for proving causality?
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of the status of al-ilzāmāt (arguments by imposition) as methods for proving causality. The author begins by stating that counting al-ilzāmāt among the methods of proving causality is an oversight (sahw) by the original author (al-muṣannif), because al-ilzāmāt are clearly a type of qiyās (analogical reasoning). The author then explains the source of the confusion: al-Rāzī in his Nihāyah listed four weak methods, and the third was al-ilzāmāt, but al-Ījī (the author of al-Mawāqif) mistakenly thought al-Rāzī meant it as a third method for proving the common cause. The passage then examines a possible objection: that even though al-ilzāmāt are a type of qiyās, they might still include a specific kind of method for proving the common cause, because the arguer relies on the opponent's admission of the cause in the original case. The author refutes this by showing that the opponent's mere admission in the arguer's claim cannot be a genuine method for proving causality. The weakness of al-ilzāmāt is not that they are analogies per se, but that they defer the determination of the ruling and cause to the opponent, who can simply deny them. The passage concludes by calling for a balanced judgment on the issue.

القياس
الطرق الضعيفة
السبر والتقسيم
الطرد والعكس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-ilzāmāt are among the methods for proving causality.
Correction: Al-ilzāmāt are a type of analogical reasoning (qiyās) and not an independent method for proving causality.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the inclusion of al-ilzāmāt among the methods of proving causality an oversight?
A) Because al-ilzāmāt are too weak to be used at all
B) Because al-ilzāmāt are actually a type of qiyās, not an independent method
C) Because al-ilzāmāt are only used in transmitted evidences
D) Because al-ilzāmāt are based on the opponent's admission, which is unreliable

Key Takeaway
Al-ilzāmāt are properly understood as a form of analogical reasoning (qiyās) and should not be counted among the independent methods for proving causality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author points out that al-ilzāmāt are not weak in form but in reliance on the opponent's concession. In your own life, do you sometimes depend on others' endorsements to support your positions rather than establishing truth independently? Commit to examining one belief you hold and verifying its foundation directly from the primary texts.
?
When you encounter a disagreement in matters of knowledge, do you rush to defend a familiar view, or do you pause to clarify the correct classification—like distinguishing a method from a type? What would it mean for you to 'be the decisive judge' in your own learning, as the author urges?

Definition and Division of Premises (Muqaddimat)
Defines muqaddimat and divides them into certain and probable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what premises (muqaddimat) are in kalam and how they are divided into certain and probable types.

Core Idea
Premises are the building blocks of proofs, divided into certain and probable.

Before we can assess any argument in kalam, we need to understand its building blocks. The author begins by defining 'premises' as the propositions under investigation, and immediately classifies them into two fundamental kinds. This sets the stage for all subsequent reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the key terms like 'muqaddimat', 'qat'iyya', and 'dhanniyya'.
2
Understand the definition of muqaddimat
Identify how the author defines premises: propositions in which investigation occurs regarding proof, which is the path to affirmation.
The word 'absolutely' indicates this applies whether the affirmation is certain or probable.
3
Note the division into two types
The passage explicitly divides premises into certain (qat'iyya) and probable (dhanniyya). Certain premises are used in certain proofs; probable premises are used in probable indicators (imara).
This division is foundational for all subsequent discussions of proofs.
4
Reflect on why this division matters
Consider how different types of premises lead to different levels of certainty in conclusions. This classification helps in evaluating the strength of arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ المقصد السادس : في المقدمات ] أي القضايا التي يقع فيها النظر المتعلق بالدليل الذي هو الطريق إلى التصديق مطلقاً على قسمين قطعية تستعمل في الأدلة القطعية وظنية تستعمل في الإمارة. قوله : (مطلقاً) أي يقينياً كان أو ظنياً. قوله : ( على قسمين خبر مبتدأ محذوف أي هي على قسمين قدر هذا الكلام لتصحيح الفاء المذكورة في قوله فالقطعية.
[The Sixth Objective: On Premises] That is, the propositions in which investigation occurs regarding the proof which is the path to affirmation absolutely, divided into two types: certain ones used in certain proofs, and probable ones used in the indicator. He said: (absolutely) meaning, whether certain or probable. He said: (into two types) is a predicate of an omitted subject, i.e., they are of two types. He estimated this statement to correct the fa mentioned in his statement 'so the certain ones'.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'al-muqaddimat' (premises) and divides them into two types. The author begins by interpreting 'al-muqaddimat' as the propositions in which investigation (nazar) occurs regarding the proof (dalil), which is the path (tariq) to affirmation (tasdiq). The word 'absolutely' (mudlaqan) indicates that this applies whether the affirmation is certain or probable. The division is then explicitly stated: premises are either certain (qat'iyya) or probable (dhanniyya). Certain premises are used in certain proofs (adilla qat'iyya), while probable premises are used in probable indications (imara). The commentary then clarifies the grammatical point: the phrase 'into two types' ('ala qismayn) is the predicate of an omitted subject, meaning 'they are of two types'. This grammatical clarification is needed to correctly understand the structure of the sentence. The overall function of the passage is to introduce the foundational classification of premises that will be used throughout the discussion of logical proofs.

Correction: The passage clarifies that premises are used for both certain and probable proofs. Certain premises are used in certain proofs, while probable premises are used in probable indicators (imara).

Could admitting that uncertainty change how you present your faith to others? Will you commit today to trace one of your beliefs back to its premises and test their certainty?
