Hierarchy of Certain Premises and Proof for Others
Classifies seven certain premises and their convincing power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven types of certain premises in kalam and which ones are universally convincing versus only personally convincing.

Core Idea
Only four of the seven certain premises convince others; the rest are personal proofs.

This passage establishes the key principle that not all certain premises are equally convincing to others. While some are self-evident or founded on common experience, others depend on personal experience or intuition. Understanding this hierarchy is crucial for constructing valid theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to get an overview of the classification. Note the author's emphasis on the first seven premises.
The passage uses technical terms; don't worry about memorizing them yet.
2
Identify the two groups of premises
Notice that the author distinguishes the first four (primary axioms, innate propositions, observed, imaginal) from the last three (empirical, intuitive, recurrent) based on their power to convince others. Underline or list each category.
The first four are 'proof for oneself and others'; the last three are 'proof for oneself only' unless conditions are met.
3
Reflect on the inductive restriction
The author provides a reason for restricting to seven categories: if mere conception of subject and predicate suffices for judgment, it is a primary axiom; otherwise, it falls into another category. Consider how this principle ties the classification together.
This is a meta-level explanation of why there are exactly seven.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن العمدة من هذه المبادي الأول السبعة الأوليات إذ لا يتوقف فيها إلا ناقص الغريزة كالبله والصبيان أو مدنس الفطرة بالعقائد المضادة للأوليات كما لبعض الجهال، والعوام ثم القضايا الفطرية القياس، ثم المشاهدات ثم الوهميات، وأما المجربات والحدسيات والمتواترات فهي وإن كانت حجة للشخص مع نفسه لكنها ليست حجة له على غيره إلا إذا شاركه في الأمور المقتضية لها من التجربة والحدس والتواتر، فلا يمكن أن يقنع جاحدها على سبيل المناكرة. ووجه الحصر الاستقرائي في هذه السبع أن تصور الطرفين إن كفي في حكم العقل فهو الأوليات، وإن لم يكف...
قوله: (والمعقولات الصرفة) وإن كانت غير مختصة بالمجردات.
قوله: (بأحكام المحسوسات) أي بأحكام مختصة بالمحسوسات.
قوله: (إن العمدة) أي باعتبار كونها حجة في نفسه وعلى الغير أيضاً.
قوله: (ثم القضايا إلخ) لكونها في حكم الأوليات كما مر.
قوله: (ثم المشاهدات) أي قسم منها وهي المحسوسات وهي إنما تكون حجة على الغير إذا شاركه في المشعر والشعور، وكذا الوهميات ولم يقيدهما بذلك لظهوره، وإنما كانت بعد القضايا الفطرية لكونها أحكاماً جزئية لا تفاوت بينهما في القطعية.
قوله: (ثم الوهميات) لكون مدركها قوة باطنة محتاجة إلى شهادة العقل بها.
قوله: (بأن يقنع) من الإقناع بمعنى الإرضاء والمناكرة المقابلة والمحاربة متعلق بقوله جاحدها أي لا يمكن إرضاء جاحد الأقسام الثلاثة إذا كان جحوده على سبيل المخاصمة والمحاربة بخلاف ما إذا كان جحوده على سبيل الاستفادة فإنه يمكن إرضاؤه إذا اعترف بالاشتراك فيما يقتضيها.
Know that the most important of these premises are the first seven: the primary [axioms] (al-awwaliyat), for only those deficient in instinct, like idiots and children, or those whose nature is defiled with beliefs contrary to the primary [axioms], as with some ignorant people and commoners, hesitate about them. Then the innate propositions (al-qadaya al-fitriyyah) that are analogous, then the observed (al-mushahadat), then the imaginal (al-wahmiyyat). As for the empirical (al-mujarrabat), the intuitive (al-hadsiyyat), and the recurrent (al-mutawatirat), although they are proof for a person with himself, they are not proof for him against others unless [the others] share with him in the matters that require them, such as experience, intuition, and recurrent transmission. So he cannot convince one who denies them by way of opposition. The reason for the inductive restriction to these seven is that if the conception of the two extremes suffices for the judgment of reason, then it is a primary [axiom]; if not...
He said: (And pure intelligibles) even if they are not confined to abstract things.
He said: (With judgments of sensory things) i.e., with judgments specific to sensory things.
He said: (The most important) i.e., considering that they are proof for oneself and also for others.
He said: (Then the propositions, etc.) because they are in the same ruling as the primary axioms, as has passed.
He said: (Then the observed) i.e., a type of them, namely sensory things, and they are only proof for others if [the others] share in the sense organ and sensation. Similarly the imaginal, though he did not restrict them by that due to its obviousness, and they are only after the innate propositions because they are particular judgments with no difference between them in certainty.
He said: (Then the imaginal) because their perceiver is an inner faculty that needs the testimony of reason.
He said: (That he can convince) from 'iqna' meaning satisfaction, and 'munaakara' is opposition and war, attached to his saying 'one who denies them', i.e., it is not possible to satisfy one who denies the three types if his denial is by way of contention and war, unlike if his denial is by way of seeking benefit, for then it is possible to satisfy him if he acknowledges sharing in what requires them.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage classifies the seven certain premises and discusses which are universal proofs. It states that the most important are the first seven: primary axioms (al-awwaliyat), innate propositions analogous to them (al-fitriyyat), observed sensory premises (al-mushahadat), and imaginal premises (al-wahmiyyat). Only those with deficient intellect or corrupted beliefs doubt these. The remaining three—empirical, intuitive, and recurrent—are proof for oneself but not for others unless the other person shares the same experience, intuition, or transmission. Thus, one cannot convince a denier through argument. The inductive restriction is explained: if the mere conception of subject and predicate suffices for reason's judgment, it is an awwali; otherwise, it falls into another category. The commentary elaborates: 'the most important' means they are proof for both oneself and others. Observed and imaginal premises require the other to share sensory faculties. Imaginal premises are placed after innate because their perceiver is an inner faculty needing reason's testimony. Finally, convincing a denier is only impossible when he is hostile; if he seeks understanding, he can be convinced if he acknowledges shared foundations.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All seven certain premises are equally convincing to everyone.
Correction: The passage clarifies that empirical, intuitive, and recurrent premises are only proof for oneself unless the other person shares the relevant experience; they are not universally convincing like the first four.

Key Takeaway
Only four of the seven certain premises are universally convincing; the remaining three depend on shared experience.

Classification of Evidence: Rational, Traditional, or Composite
Classifies evidence into rational, transmitted, and composite types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the threefold classification of evidence into rational, transmitted, and composite, and the criterion for each type.

Core Idea
Evidence is either purely rational, purely transmitted, or a mix of both.

In this lesson, we explore the seventh station from al-Mawaqif, where al-Iji classifies evidence into three categories. This classification helps us understand the foundations of arguments in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three types
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the three categories: rational, transmitted, and composite.
The passage is short; the commentary adds context.
2
Understand rational evidence
Rational evidence is defined as having all premises (immediate and mediate) based on reason.
This includes both direct and indirect rational premises.
3
Understand transmitted evidence
Transmitted evidence requires all premises to be from revelation or tradition (naqli).
If any premise is rational, the evidence is not purely transmitted.
4
Consider composite evidence
Composite evidence mixes rational and transmitted premises. The passage implies this third type.
The commentary notes that the division applies when 'evidence' means ordered premises.
5
Reflect on the misconception
Some think transmitted evidence is just any scriptural argument, but the passage insists all premises must be naqli.
This strictness ensures correct classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد السابع : الدليل إما عقلي أو نقلي أو مركب ] ( الدليل إما عقلي بجميع مقدماته ( قريبة كانت أو بعيدة ( أو نقلي بجميعها )
قوله : (الدليل إما عقلي إلخ هذا التقسيم إذا أريد بالدليل المقدمات المترتبة، وأما إذا
The seventh station: Evidence is either rational, or transmitted (naqli), or composite. (Evidence is either rational with all its premises, whether near or far, or transmitted with all of them.)
He said: (Evidence is either rational etc.) This division is if what is meant by evidence is the arranged premises. But if...
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage begins the seventh section (المقصد السابع) on the classification of evidence. It states that evidence (الدليل) is of three types: rational (عقلي), transmitted/narrative (نقلي), or composite (مركب). The rational type is defined as having all premises (both immediate and mediate) based on reason. The transmitted type has all premises based on revelation or tradition. The composite type presumably mixes both. The beginning of a commentary (قوله) explains that this division applies when 'evidence' means the ordered premises, but the text cuts off.

Misconception: Transmitted evidence is simply based on scripture regardless of the nature of its premises.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Evidence in kalam is classified based on the nature of its premises: all rational, all transmitted, or a mixture.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The composite approach combines both—are you actively seeking to strengthen your faith by employing both intellect and scripture, or do you lean entirely on one side? Commit today to study the evidences from both angles.

Classification of Proof: Rational, Transmitted, and Composite
Classification of proof and impossibility of purely transmitted proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why purely transmitted proof is impossible and how proofs are classified into rational, transmitted (composite), and the relationship between them.

Core Idea
No proof can be purely transmitted; reason is always required.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif tackles a foundational issue in Islamic epistemology: how do we classify proofs? The author divides proofs into rational, transmitted, and composite, but then argues that a purely transmitted proof is actually impossible. This is because even scriptural evidence requires rational verification of the reporter's truthfulness. Let's see how the argument unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The text includes both the matn and the commentary (qala).
2
Identify the three types of proof
The passage begins by dividing proof into rational, transmitted, and composite. Find each definition and note the key characteristics.
3
Understand why purely transmitted proof is impossible
The author argues that purely transmitted proof is inconceivable because the truthfulness of the reporter must be established by reason, otherwise it leads to circularity or infinite regress. Think about why reason is necessary here.
This is the core argument of the passage.
4
See how composite proof is what we call transmitted
After explaining the impossibility of pure naqli, the author says that the composite proof is what is commonly called transmitted. Notice the shift in terminology.
5
Reflect on the final limitation to two types
The passage concludes that after reflection, proofs are limited to rational and composite. Pause to consider why the initial three-type division is only preliminary.
The commentary clarifies that the division depends on whether we mean premises or sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من [ المقصد السابع : الدليل إما عقلي أو نقلي أو مركب ] ( الدليل إما عقلي بجميع مقدماته ( قريبة كانت أو بعيدة ( أو نقلي بجميعها ) أو مركب منهما والأول ) هو الدليل ( العقلي ) المحض الذي لا يتوقف على السمع أصلا ) والثاني ) وهو الدليل النقلي المحض ( لا يتصور إذ صدق المخبر لا بد منه ) حتى يفيد الدليل النقلي العلم بالمدلول ( وإنه لا يثبت إلا بالعقل) وهو في المعجزة الدالة على صدقه ولو أريد إثباته بالنقل دار أو تسلسل ( والثالث ) يعني المركب منهما ( هو الذي نسميه بالنقلي ) لتوقفه على النقل في الجملة، فانحصر هذا الدليل في قسمين العقلي المحض والمركب من العقلي والنقلي هو التحقيق
قوله : الدليل إما عقلي إلخ هذا التقسيم إذا أريد بالدليل المقدمات المترتبة، وأما إذا أريد مأخذها كالعالم للصانع والكتاب والسنة والإجماع للأحكام، فلا معنى له فطريق القسمة أن استلزامه للمطلوب إن كان بحكم العقل فعقلي وإلا فنقلي كذا في شرح المقاصد، والأظهر أن يقال : إن هذا التقسيم على تقدير كونه مفرداً بعد النظر في أحواله
قوله : لا يتصور فالقسمة المذكورة قسمة بحسب بادئ الرأي
قوله : فانحصر الدليل أي بعد التأمل
From the seventh objective: The proof is either rational with all its premises (near or distant) or transmitted with all of them, or composite from both. The first is the purely rational proof that does not depend on scripture at all. The second is the purely transmitted proof—it is inconceivable, for the truthfulness of the reporter is necessary so that the transmitted proof yields knowledge of the signification, and it can only be established by reason, which is in the miracle indicating his truthfulness; if one attempted to establish it by transmission, it would lead to circularity or infinite regress. The third, meaning the composite from both, is what we call transmitted, because of its dependence on transmission in general. So this proof is limited to two sections: the purely rational and the composite from rational and transmitted—that is the verification.
He said: "The proof is either rational etc." This division is when by "proof" is meant the arranged premises. But if by it is meant their sources, like the universe for the Creator, and the Book, Sunna, and consensus for rulings, then it has no meaning. So the method of division is that if its entailment of the required is by judgment of reason, it is rational; otherwise, it is transmitted. Thus in Sharh al-Maqasid. And the more apparent is to say: this division is on the assumption of its being singular after considering its states.
He said: "it is inconceivable" – so the mentioned division is a division according to initial opinion.
He said: "so this proof is limited" – i.e., after reflection.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage classifies proof (dalil) into three types: rational (‘aqli), transmitted (naqli), and composite (murakkab). The author argues that purely transmitted proof is impossible because it requires the truthfulness of the reporter (sidq al-mukhbir), which can only be known by reason, specifically through miracles (mu‘jiza). Attempting to prove the reporter's truthfulness by transmission leads to circularity or infinite regress. Therefore, the only feasible proofs are purely rational and composite, and the composite is what is commonly called transmitted. The commentary (qala) clarifies that this division applies when 'proof' refers to the arranged premises (muqaddimat murattaba), not to their sources. If sources are meant, the division is meaningless. The correct method is based on whether the proof's entailment is by rational judgment. The marginal notes also note that the division is preliminary (bi hasbi badi’ al-ra’y) and that the limitation to two types is after reflection.

الدليل العقلي
الدليل النقلي
الدليل المركب
المقدمات
صدق المخبر
المعجزة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a proof can be purely scriptural (naqli) without any rational grounding.
Correction: The passage shows that even scriptural proof requires a rational verification of the reporter's truthfulness, making it composite. Pure naqli is impossible.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that purely transmitted proof (naqli mahd) is impossible?
A) Because all proofs require empirical data.
B) Because the truthfulness of the reporter must be established by reason.
C) Because transmitted proofs are always weak.
D) Because the Quran is self-authenticating.

Key Takeaway
Only rational and composite (called transmitted) proofs are valid; pure transmitted proof is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a Quranic verse or a hadith, do you ever pause to consider why you accept it as true? This lesson forces you to admit that your trust in the Prophet's truthfulness is itself a rational conviction. Have you ever consciously examined that conviction, or have you simply taken it for granted? What would you say to someone who asks you for a rational reason to believe in the Prophet?
?
You believe that the Quran is from God because of its miracles—but have you ever personally pondered one of those miracles as a logical proof? If not, why have you avoided doing the very thing this lesson says is necessary? Will you commit today to studying one miracle of the Quran (like its linguistic inimitability) as a rational argument?
?
This lesson reveals that 'transmitted' knowledge always depends on reason. That means your faith is not a blind leap but a reasonable step. Does your daily relationship with Allah reflect that confident rationality, or do you sometimes feel as though you are following inherited beliefs without thinking? If the latter, what is holding you back from engaging your intellect in matters of faith?

Three Types of Proof Based on Premises
Sanusi enumerates three types of proof based on the nature of their near premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of proof based on the nature of their near premises: purely rational, purely transmitted, and mixed (composite).

Core Idea
Proofs are classified by their premises: rational, transmitted, or mixed.

This lesson introduces a further subdivision of proof based on its near premises. Scholars categorize proofs as purely rational (e.g., logical syllogisms about the world), purely transmitted (e.g., scriptural arguments), or composite (a mix of both). This classification helps identify the source of certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Note the three examples given.
2
Identify the three categories
Find the purely rational example, the purely transmitted example, and the composite example in the text.
3
Understand the method of division
Notice the rule: if the entailment is by reason, it's rational; otherwise, transmitted. This determines the category.
This comes from Sharh al-Maqasid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنه قد يقسم الدليل إلى ثلاثة أقسام فيقال : مقدماته القريبة قد تكون عقلية محضة كقولنا : العالم متغير وكل متغير حادث وقد تكون نقلية محضة كقولنا : تارك المأمور به عاص، لقوله تعالى : أفعصيت أمري [طه:٩٣]، وكل عاص يستحق العقاب، لقوله : ومن يعص الله ورسوله فإن له نار جهنم [النساء:١٤]، وقد يكون بعضها مأخوذة من العقل وبعضها من النقل كقولنا : هذا تارك المأمور به وكل تارك للمأمور به عاص فلا بأس أن يسمى هذا القسم الأخير بالمركب من أريد مأخذها كالعالم للصانع والكتاب والسنة والإجماع للأحكام، فلا معنى له فطريق القسمة أن استلزامه للمطلوب إن كان بحكم العقل فعقلي وإلا فنقلي كذا في شرح المقاصد، والأظهر أن يقال : إن هذا التقسيم على تقدير كونه مفرداً بعد النظر في أحواله
قوله : لا يتصور فالقسمة المذكورة قسمة بحسب بادئ الرأي
قوله : فانحصر الدليل أي بعد التأمل
قوله : ثم إنه إلخ أشار بتقدير هذا الكلام وإرجاع ضمير قوله، ثم مقدماته إلى الدليل إلى أنه معطوف على قوله الدليل إما عقلي لا كما يوهمه الظاهر من كونه معطوفا على قوله، والثالث هو الذي نسميه بالنقل لأنه حينئذ تكون هذه الأقسام المذكورة أقسام القسم، والمقصود تصحيح القسمة المثلثة للدليل على ما وقع في كلام البعض
قوله: تارك المأمور به عاص أي تارك ما ثبت بالأمر المطلق أعني الواجب ينسب إليه العصيان، ويطلق عليه عاص شرعاً لقوله تعالى أفعصيت أمري وما قيل : أن المراد بالعصيان على تقدير كونه شرعياً استحقاق العقاب فوهم لأنه لا يدل الدليل المذكور عليه ولا يتحقق الحمل في الكبرى
قوله : هذا تارك المأمور به فإنه يحكم به العقل ولو بواسطة الحس، ولا يتوقف على النقل
قوله : تارك المأمور به عاص أي أمراً مطلقاً، وإنما قيدنا بهذا لأن المندوب مأمور به عند الجمهور ولیس ترکه بعاص
قوله : هذا تارك المأمور به إنما أطلق العقلي على هذه المقدمة مع أنها مستندة إلى الحس بناء على أن المراد بالعقلي ها هنا ما يقابل النقلي فيندرج فيه الحسي
قوله : وكل تارك المأمور به عاص قد يراد بالعصيان ترك الامتثال بالأوامر والنواهي، ولا
Then it may be that proof is divided into three categories: it is said: its near premises may be purely rational, like our saying: the world is changing, and every changing thing is temporal; and may be purely transmitted, like our saying: the one who leaves the commanded act is a sinner, due to His saying: "Have you disobeyed my command?" [Taha 93], and every sinner deserves punishment, due to His saying: "And whoever disobeys God and His Messenger, then indeed for him is the fire of Hell" [Nisa 14]; and it may be that some of them are taken from reason and some from transmission, like our saying: this one leaves the commanded act, and everyone who leaves the commanded act is a sinner. So there is no harm in calling this last section "composite" – from one intends their sources like the universe for the Creator, and the Book, Sunna, and consensus for rulings, then it has no meaning. So the method of division is that if its entailment of the required is by judgment of reason, it is rational; otherwise, it is transmitted. Thus in Sharh al-Maqasid. And the more apparent is to say: this division is on the assumption of its being singular after considering its states.
He said: "it is inconceivable" – so the mentioned division is a division according to initial opinion.
He said: "so this proof is limited" – i.e., after reflection.
He said: "Then it may be etc." He indicated by presenting this speech and referring the pronoun of his saying "then its premises" back to "proof" that it is coordinated with his saying "the proof is either rational" not as the apparent suggests that it is coordinated with his saying "and the third is what we call transmitted", because then these mentioned categories would be categories of the section, and the purpose is to correct the threefold division of proof as occurred in the words of some.
He said: "the one who leaves the commanded act is a sinner" – i.e., the one who leaves what is established by the absolute command, meaning the obligatory, is ascribed disobedience, and is called a sinner legally due to His saying: "Have you disobeyed my command?" And what is said that the intended by disobedience on the assumption of its being legal is deserving punishment is a mistake, because the mentioned proof does not indicate it nor is the predication realized in the major premise.
He said: "this one leaves the commanded act" – for reason judges it, even if through sense, and it does not depend on transmission.
He said: "the one who leaves the commanded act is a sinner" – i.e., absolutely; and we restricted it with this because the recommended (al-mandub) is commanded according to the majority, but its abandonment is not disobedience.
He said: "this" one leaves the commanded act – he only called this premise rational even though it is based on sense, based on the fact that "rational" here means what is opposed to transmitted, so sense is included in it.
He said: "and everyone who leaves the commanded act is a sinner" – disobedience may mean failure to comply with commands and prohibitions, and not...
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage presents a further subdivision of proof based on the nature of its near premises (muqaddimat qariba). A proof can have premises that are purely rational (e.g., 'the world is changing, and every changing thing is temporal'), purely transmitted (e.g., 'the one who leaves the commanded act is a sinner' and 'every sinner deserves punishment', both derived from Quranic verses), or mixed (e.g., 'this one leaves the commanded act' [rational] and 'everyone who leaves the commanded act is a sinner' [transmitted]). The mixed type is called composite (murakkab). The commentary then explains various nuances: the division only makes sense when considering the premises as sources; otherwise, the method is to see if the entailment is by reason. It also clarifies that the 'composite' here is not the same as the earlier transmitted proof. The marginal notes (qala) further elaborate on the examples: 'leaving the commanded act' refers to abandoning an obligatory act, not a recommended one; the premise 'this one leaves the commanded act' is rational (based on sense observation), and 'everyone who leaves the commanded act is a sinner' is transmitted but requires proper understanding of disobedience. The passage ends with an incomplete note on the meaning of disobedience.

Key Terms
مقدمات قريبة
عقلية محضة
نقلية محضة
مركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the composite type is purely transmitted because both premises seem scriptural.
Correction: In the composite, one premise is rational (observed) and the other transmitted; the combination makes it mixed.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a purely rational premise according to the passage?
A) 'Whoever disobeys God deserves punishment'
B) 'The world is changing'
C) 'The one who leaves the commanded act is a sinner'
D) 'This person leaves the commanded act'

Key Takeaway
Proofs are classified into rational, transmitted, or composite based on whether their near premises come from reason, revelation, or a mix.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm a belief in Islam, do you know whether its proof is rational, transmitted, or composite? If not, what does that reveal about how deeply you have examined the foundations of your faith?
?
If someone told you that only proofs from the Quran and Sunnah are valid, how would you respond using Sanusi's classification? Would you be able to defend the role of reason using this lesson?
?
Think of a personal conviction you hold about God. Is it based purely on reason, purely on revelation, or a combination? Does the strength of your conviction match the strength of its proof?

Understanding the Example of 'Leaving the Commanded Act'
Commentary on the example of 'tark al-ma'mur bihi' to clarify the rational and transmitted elements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nuances of the example 'the one who leaves the commanded act is a sinner', including the meaning of 'leaving' and the rational vs. transmitted status of its premises.

Core Idea
The premise 'he leaves the commanded act' is rational based on sense observation.

The example of 'the one who leaves the commanded act is a sinner' appears in the discussion of transmitted and composite proofs. This lesson unpacks its meaning: what constitutes 'leaving', why the minor premise is rational, and the legal definition of disobedience. It clarifies common confusions about the source of these premises.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentary passage
Read the Arabic passage that explains the example, focusing on the explanation of 'tark al-ma'mur bihi'.
2
Understand 'leaving the commanded act'
Note that it refers to abandoning an obligatory act, not a recommended one. The term 'absolute command' (al-amr al-mutlaq) is used.
This is a legal nuance.
3
Recognize the rational premise
The premise 'this person leaves the commanded act' is rational because it is based on sense observation, even though it involves a legal concept.
It is not transmitted, even though the conclusion uses a Quranic term.
4
Clarify the transmitted premise
The premise 'everyone who leaves the commanded act is a sinner' is transmitted, but note that 'sinner' here is a legal status, not necessarily deserving punishment immediately.
Avoid misunderstanding that the proof automatically implies punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the premise 'this person leaves the commanded act' is transmitted because it involves a religious command.
Correction: It is based on observation of someone's action, which is a matter of sense and reason, not revelation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the premise 'this person leaves the commanded act' considered rational?
A) Because it is derived from the Quran.
B) Because it is known through sense observation.
C) Because it is a necessary truth.
D) Because it is agreed upon by scholars.

Key Takeaway
The minor premise in the composite proof is rational because it is based on sense observation, even when the act involves a religious command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a Muslim neglect an obligation like prayer, what first arises in your heart? Do you immediately assign them a verdict about their final state? This lesson teaches that the label 'sinner' is a legal fact, not a sentence to Hell. Will you now pause before assuming punishment for others? Can you commit today to separate observation from judgment?
?
You often combine what you observe (someone missing prayer) with assumptions about their intention or destiny. The minor premise is purely observational; the rest is revelation. How much of your judgment of others is actually your own speculation mixed with revelation? Will you strip away your assumptions today?
?
If the proof only establishes the label 'sinner' and not punishment, then how do you view your own sins? Do you live as if every sin automatically merits punishment, forgetting God's mercy? Or do you separate the fact of sin from God's decree? Which belief actually drives your relationship with Allah right now? Take a moment to repent of any despair or presumption.

The Threefold Classification and Impossibility of Pure Naqli
The author classifies evidence and argues that pure textual evidence is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial division of evidence into rational, textual, and composite, and why pure textual evidence is impossible without rational grounding.

Core Idea
Pure textual evidence is impossible; all evidence ultimately requires reason.

This lesson introduces the fundamental classification of evidence in Islamic theology. The author presents three categories—rational, textual, and composite—but then shows that pure textual evidence is inconceivable, reducing the classification to two: purely rational and composite (which is commonly called textual due to its reliance on revelation).

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to understand the overall argument.
Notice the author's initial threefold division.
2
Identify the three categories
Find and list: rational, textual, composite. Note how the author defines each.
3
Understand why pure naqli is impossible
The author argues that pure textual evidence is inconceivable because it requires rational proof for the truthfulness of the reporter (via miracles). Recognize the circular/infinite regress argument.
This is the key insight of the passage.
4
See the reduction to two categories
Since pure naqli is impossible, the actual division is purely rational and composite (which is called naqli). Confirm this with the author's statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد السابع : الدليل إما عقلي أو نقلي أو مركب أن ينظر كذلك ) أو مركب منهما والأول ) هو الدليل ( العقلي ) المحض الذي لا يتوقف على السمع أصلا ) والثاني ) وهو الدليل النقلي المحض ( لا يتصور إذ صدق المخبر لا بد منه ) حتى يفيد الدليل النقلي العلم بالمدلول ( وإنه لا يثبت إلا بالعقل) وهو في المعجزة الدالة على صدقه ولو أريد إثباته بالنقل دار أو تسلسل ( والثالث ) يعني المركب منهما ( هو الذي نسميه بالنقلي ) لتوقفه على النقل في الجملة، فانحصر هذا الدليل في قسمين العقلي المحض والمركب من العقلي والنقلي هو التحقيق، (ثم) إنه قد يقسم الدليل إلى ثلاثة أقسام فيقال : ( مقدماته القريبة قد تكون عقلية محضة ) كقولنا : العالم متغير وكل متغير حادث ( وقد تكون نقلية محضة ) . كقولنا : تارك المأمور به عاص، لقوله تعالى : لا أفعصيت أمري ﴾ [طه:٩٣]، وكل عاص يستحق العقاب، لقوله : ومن يعص الله ورسوله فإن له نار جهنم الله ورسوله فإن له نار جهنم ﴾ [النساء : ١٤ ] ، ( وقد يكون بعضها مأخوذة من العقل وبعضها من النقل ) كقولنا : هذا تارك المأمور به وكل تارك للمأمور به عاص فلا بأس أن يسمى هذا القسم الأخير ( بالمركب ) من أريد مأخذها كالعالم للصانع والكتاب والسنة والإجماع للأحكام، فلا معنى له فطريق القسمة أن استلزامه للمطلوب إن كان بحكم العقل فعقلي وإلا فنقلي كذا في شرح المقاصد، والأظهر أن يقال : إن هذا التقسيم على تقدير كونه مفرداً بعد النظر في أحواله . ، قوله : ( لا يتصور) فالقسمة المذكورة قسمة بحسب بادئ الرأي. قوله : ( فانحصر الدليل) أي بعد التأمل . قوله : ( ثم إنه إلخ ) أشار بتقدير هذا الكلام وإرجاع ضمير قوله، ثم مقدماته إلى الدليل إلى أنه معطوف على قوله الدليل إما عقلي لا كما يوهمه الظاهر من كونه معطوفا على قوله، والثالث هو الذي نسميه بالنقل لأنه حينئذ تكون هذه الأقسام المذكورة أقسام القسم، والمقصود تصحيح القسمة المثلثة للدليل على ما وقع في كلام البعض. قوله: تارك المأمور به (عاص أي تارك ما ثبت بالأمر المطلق أعني الواجب ينسب إليه العصيان، ويطلق عليه عاص شرعاً لقوله تعالى أفعصيت أمري وما قيل : أن المراد بالعصيان على تقدير كونه شرعياً استحقاق العقاب فوهم لأنه لا يدل الدليل المذكور عليه ولا يتحقق الحمل في الكبرى. قوله : ( هذا تارك المأمور به فإنه يحكم به العقل ولو بواسطة الحس، ولا يتوقف على النقل. قوله : ( تارك المأمور به عاص أي أمراً مطلقاً، وإنما قيدنا بهذا لأن المندوب مأمور به عند الجمهور ولیس ترکه بعاص. قوله : (هذا) تارك المأمور (به إنما أطلق العقلي على هذه المقدمة مع أنها مستندة إلى الحس بناء على أن المراد بالعقلي ها هنا ما يقابل النقلي فيندرج فيه الحسي. قوله : ( وكل تارك المأمور به (عاص قد يراد بالعصيان ترك الامتثال بالأوامر والنواهي، ولا يمتنع العقلي والنقلي، فظهر صحة تثليث القسمة كما وقع في عبارة بعضهم )
The sixth observation - the seventh purpose: The evidence is either rational or textual or composite. That is, it is either rational or textual or composite of both. The first is the purely rational evidence that does not depend on revelation at all. The second, which is the purely textual evidence, is inconceivable because the truthfulness of the reporter is necessary so that the textual evidence yields knowledge of the indicated, and it is not established except by reason, and that is in the miracle indicating his truthfulness. If one wanted to prove it by textual evidence, it would be circular or infinite regress. And the third, meaning the composite of both, is what we call textual because of its dependence on transmission in general. Thus this evidence is restricted to two divisions: the purely rational and the composite of rational and textual; that is the verification. Then it may be that evidence is divided into three divisions, and it is said: Its proximate premises may be purely rational, like our saying: The world is changing and everything changing is originated; and they may be purely textual, like our saying: The one who leaves the commanded thing is disobedient, due to His statement: 'Did you then disobey my command?' [20:93], and every disobedient deserves punishment, due to His statement: 'And whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger, then indeed for him is the Fire of Hell' [4:14]; and some of them may be taken from reason and some from transmission, like our saying: This is a leaver of the commanded thing, and every leaver of the commanded thing is disobedient. Then there is no harm in calling this last division composite. For if their source is considered, like the world for the Maker, and the Book, Sunnah, and consensus for rulings, then it is meaningless. So the way of division is that its entailment of the sought, if it is by the judgment of reason, then it is rational; otherwise, textual. Thus in Sharh al-Maqasid. And the most apparent is to say that this division is on the assumption that it is a single thing after considering its states. His statement: 'is inconceivable': So the mentioned division is a division according to initial view. His statement: 'Thus the evidence is restricted': i.e., after reflection. His statement: 'Then indeed it may be divided' etc.: He indicated by presenting this statement and referring the pronoun of 'then its premises' to the evidence that it is a conjunction to his statement 'the evidence is either rational' not as the appearance suggests that it is a conjunction to his statement 'and the third is what we call textual', because then these mentioned divisions would be divisions of the division, and the intention is to correct the triple division of evidence as occurred in the speech of some. His statement: 'the one who leaves the commanded thing is disobedient': i.e., the one who leaves what is established by the absolute command, meaning the obligatory, is attributed to disobedience, and is called disobedient legally due to His statement: 'Did you then disobey my command?' And what is said that disobedience, assuming it is legal, means deserving punishment, is an error, because the mentioned evidence does not indicate it, nor is the predication realized in the major premise. His statement: 'this is a leaver of the commanded thing': then it is judged by reason, even through sense, and does not depend on transmission. His statement: 'the one who leaves the commanded thing is disobedient': i.e., absolutely, and we have restricted it with this because the recommended is commanded according to the majority, and leaving it is not disobedience. His statement: 'this is a leaver of the commanded thing': He only called this premise rational even though it is based on sense, because what is meant by rational here is what is opposite of textual, so the sensory is included. His statement: 'and every leaver of the commanded thing is disobedient': Disobedience may be intended as leaving compliance with commands and prohibitions, and the rational and textual are not impossible, so the validity of the triple division appears as occurred in the expression of some.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage defines the classification of evidence. The author begins by stating that evidence is either 'aqli (rational), naqli (textual), or composite. Then clarifies that pure 'aqli does not rely on revelation at all. Pure naqli is inconceivable because the truthfulness of the reporter must be established, which requires 'aql (via miracles). Thus, the actual division reduces to two: pure 'aqli and composite (which is called naqli). Then the author mentions a secondary division: based on the nature of proximate premises, evidence can be purely rational (like 'the world is changing, every changing thing is originated'), purely textual (like 'the one who leaves a commanded act is disobedient, and every disobedient deserves punishment'—these rely on revealed texts), or mixed (one premise rational, one textual, e.g., 'this is a leaver of the commanded act' by observation, and 'every leaver of the commanded act is disobedient' from revelation). This mixed type is sometimes called composite, but the author notes it should not be named separately. The commentary clarifies each point: e.g., the first division is preliminary; after reflection, it reduces to two. The second division is actually a subdivision of the composite type. The commentator discusses the nature of disobedience and whether the premises are rational or revealed, noting that 'tark al-ma'mur bihi' is 'asī in the linguistic sense (rational) but in the shar'i sense of deserving punishment, it becomes a revealed premise. The passage ends by affirming the validity of the triple division as some have stated.

Key Terms
دليل عقلي
دليل نقلي
دليل مركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that textual evidence can stand alone without any rational basis.
Correction: The passage shows that pure textual evidence is impossible because the truthfulness of the reporter must be established by reason.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is purely textual evidence impossible?
A) Because it requires rational proof for the truthfulness of the reporter.
B) Because textual evidence is always composite.
C) Because the intellect can never accept textual evidence.
D) Because textual evidence requires no rational basis.

Key Takeaway
Evidence is ultimately either purely rational or composite; pure textual evidence is inconceivable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted something in your faith purely because someone told you, without ever thinking about why that person is trustworthy? How does this lesson challenge you to examine the basis of your beliefs?
?
This lesson argues that even the Qur'an's authority ultimately rests on a rational proof (the miracle). Does this make you more or less confident in your faith? Why?
?
When you come across a hadith that seems weak, do you investigate its chain and the reliability of narrators? How does this lesson push you to take responsibility for verifying what you accept as true?

Further Classification by Proximate Premises
The author enumerates three subtypes of evidence based on the nature of its proximate premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second classification of evidence based on whether its proximate premises are rational, textual, or mixed.

Core Idea
Evidence can have purely rational, purely textual, or mixed proximate premises.

After reducing evidence to two main categories, the author presents a secondary division based on the nature of its proximate premises. He gives examples of each type: purely rational (like the cosmological argument), purely textual (like deriving punishment from Quranic verses), and mixed (one premise from reason, one from revelation). This lesson explores these subtypes and clarifies that they are subdivisions of the composite category.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the three types of premises and their examples.
Pay attention to the examples given for each type.
2
Identify the three types
List: purely rational premises, purely textual premises, mixed premises. Understand the basis of each.
3
Examine the examples
For rational: 'The world is changing, every changing thing is originated.' For textual: 'The one who leaves a commanded act is disobedient' supported by Quran. For mixed: 'This is a leaver of the commanded act' (observed) and 'Every leaver is disobedient' (from revelation).
4
Understand the relationship to the earlier classification
This triple division applies to the composite type of evidence, not to the purely rational. The author notes that some call the mixed type 'composite' but it is more precise to see it as part of the composite category.

Do Transmitted Proofs Yield Certainty? The Mu'tazila and Ash'ari Position
The author presents the debate on whether transmitted proofs yield certainty and outlines the conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila and majority Ash'aris argue that transmitted proofs do not yield certainty unless certain conditions regarding linguistic assignment and the proof of God's power are met.

Core Idea
Transmitted proofs yield certainty only if linguistic assignment and divine attributes are independently verified.

This lesson examines a crucial methodological debate in Islamic theology: can transmitted proofs (from revelation and hadith) provide certain knowledge? The author presents the position of the Mu'tazila and Ash'aris that they do not, due to their dependence on knowledge of linguistic assignment and other conditions. The discussion also touches on proving prophethood and the nature of linguistic assignment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the argument. Notice the main claim and the reason given.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the claim and its basis
The claim is that transmitted proofs do not yield certainty. The basis is that they depend on knowledge of linguistic assignment (al-waḍ').
This is the core of the Mu'tazila and Ash'ari position.
3
Understand the conditions
The passage discusses two conditions: (1) knowledge of real assignment (not figurative), and (2) proving God's power through temporal origination of the world. Reflect on why these are needed.
The temporal origination argument connects to establishing prophethood.
4
Consider the clarification on assignment
The final part clarifies that knowledge of intention alone is insufficient without knowledge of assignment, and assignment is either personal or generic.
This refines the initial condition.

المقصد الثامن : الدلائل النقلية هل تفيد اليقين. الدلائل النقلية هل تفيد اليقين بما يستدل بها عليه من المطالب أو لا قيل : لا تفيد وهو مذهب المعتزلة وجمهور الأشاعرة لتوقفه على العلم بالوضع أي وضع الألفاظ المنقولة عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بإزاء معانيها وإلى غير ذلك. قوله : ( لتوقفه إلخ) فإن إفادتها موقوفة على إرادة معانيها بالوضع فلا بد من العلم بها . قوله : على العلم بالوضع أي الوضع الحقيقي بقرينة قوله وعلى عدم التجوز يعني يتوقف على العلم بوضع الألفاظ التي وقعت في الدليل النقلي للمعاني المفهومة منها، وإنما خص البيان بالألفاظ الحقيقية لأنها الأصل في الإفادة والمجازية متفرعة عليها . حتى يثبت كونه تعالى مرسلاً للرسل، وإثبات القدرة يتوقف على حدوث العالم، فصحة النقل يتوقف على الحدوث اللهم إلا أن يقال : يكفي في إثبات النبوة والإرسال وجود الصانع، وعلمه ولا يخفى أنه مكابرة إذ كان لهم دليل على القدرة غير متوقف على الحدوث. قوله : ( لتوقفه على العلم بالوضع لا يخفى أن العلم بالإرادة كاف إلا أنه لا يتم بدون العلم بالوضع، أما في الحقائق فظاهر، وأما في المجازات فلانها بالانتقال من الموضوع له، ولك أن تقول : المراد من الوضع أعم من الشخصي والنوعي.
The eighth section: Do transmitted proofs yield certainty? Do transmitted proofs yield certainty regarding the demands that are inferred from them, or not? It is said: They do not yield certainty, and this is the doctrine of the Mu'tazila and the majority of the Ash'aris, because it depends on knowledge of linguistic assignment (al-waḍ'), i.e., the assignment of the transmitted words from the Prophet, peace be upon him, to their meanings, and so on. His statement: “because it depends, etc.” for their benefit depends on the intention of their meanings by assignment, so knowledge of it is necessary. His statement: “on knowledge of assignment” i.e., real (ḥaqīqī) assignment, by the context of his statement “and on the absence of figurative usage,” meaning it depends on knowledge of the assignment of the words that occur in the transmitted proof to the meanings understood from them. He specifically limited the explanation to real words because they are the foundation in conveying meaning, and figurative ones are subsidiary to them. Until it is established that He, may He be exalted, is a sender of messengers, and the proof of power depends on the temporal origination of the world, so the soundness of transmission depends on temporal origination. Unless it is said: it is sufficient in proving prophethood and sending the existence of the Maker and His knowledge. And it is not hidden that this is obstinacy, since they had a proof for power not dependent on temporal origination. His statement: “because it depends on knowledge of assignment” it is not hidden that knowledge of intention is sufficient, but it is not complete without knowledge of assignment. As for real (ḥaqīqī) words, it is apparent; as for figurative (majāzī) words, it is because they transfer from the original meaning. And you may say: the intended meaning of assignment is more general, including personal (shakhṣī) and generic (naw'ī) assignment.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage addresses the eighth section: whether transmitted proofs (al-dalā'il al-naqliyya) yield certainty about the demands they prove. The position presented is that they do not yield certainty, which is the doctrine of the Mu'tazila and the majority of Ash'aris. The reason given is that their benefit depends on knowledge of linguistic assignment (al-waḍ'), i.e., knowing the conventional meanings of the words transmitted from the Prophet. The commentator explains that the benefit of transmitted proofs is conditional upon the intention of the meanings by assignment, so one must know the assignment. This knowledge must be of real (ḥaqīqī) assignment, not figurative, because real assignment is fundamental. The passage then shifts to a related argument: to establish that God is a sender of messengers, one needs to prove His power, which might depend on the temporal origination of the world. Some argue that existence and knowledge suffice, but the commentator dismisses this as obstinacy. Finally, the commentator clarifies that knowledge of intention is sufficient but not complete without knowledge of assignment, and that assignment can be personal or generic.

Correction: This passage shows that transmitted proofs depend on knowledge of linguistic assignment and other conditions; their certainty is not automatic.

Transmitted proofs are not inherently certain; they require prior rational knowledge of linguistic assignment and divine attributes.

Do Transmitted Proofs Yield Certainty?
Al-Jurjani introduces the question and gives the mainstream negation with reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why transmitted proofs (al-adilla al-naqliyya) do not yield certainty according to the Mu'tazila and most Ash'aris, and how this conclusion follows from the conjectural nature of linguistic transmission.

Core Idea
Transmitted proofs cannot yield certainty because they rely on human language and transmission, which are inherently conjectural.

This lesson introduces a central methodological issue in Islamic theology: whether transmitted proofs from revelation can provide absolute certainty. Al-Jurjani presents the mainstream view that they cannot, because their certainty depends on knowledge of linguistic conventions and the speaker's intention, both of which are established through processes that are themselves only conjectural. The passage unpacks this reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before interpreting.
2
Identify the two conditions for certainty
The passage states that transmitted proofs yield certainty only if we know (1) the linguistic convention (al-wad') and (2) the intention (al-irada). Notice how these are defined.
These conditions are the key to the argument.
3
Examine why the first condition is conjectural
The first condition depends on lugha, nahw, and sarf, which themselves rely on isolated reports (riwayat al-ahad) and analogical reasoning (qiyas). Both are explicitly called conjectural (dhanni).
Note the example of Imru' al-Qais to show error is possible.
4
Reflect on the second condition
The second condition requires that the words have not been transferred from their original meanings. This also depends on historical transmission, which is uncertain.
This completes the argument that transmitted proofs cannot yield certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثامن : الدلائل النقلية هل تفيد اليقين. قيل : لا تفيد وهو مذهب المعتزلة وجمهور الأشاعرة لتوقفه على العلم بالوضع أي وضع الألفاظ المنقولة عن النبي بإزاء معان مخصوصة والإرادة أي وعلى العلم بأن تلك المعاني مرادة منه والأول وهو العلم بالوضع إنما يثبت بنقل اللغة حتى يتعين مدلولات جواهر الألفاظ ونقل النحو حتى يتحقق مدلولات الهيئات التركيبية ونقل الصرف حتى يعرف مدلولات هيئات المفردات وأصولها تثبت برواية الآحاد لأن مرجعها إلى أشعار العرب وأمثالها وأقوالها التي يرويها عنهم الناس كالأصمعي والخليل وسيبويه وعلى تقدير صحة الرواية يجوز الخطأ فإن امرأ القيس قد خطئ في مواضع عديدة مع كونه من أكابر شعراء الجاهلية وفروعها تثبت بالأقيسة وكلاهما يعني رواية الآحاد والقياس دليلان ظنيان بلا شبهة والثاني وهو العلم بالإرادة يتوقف على عدم النقل أي نقل تلك الألفاظ عن معانيها المخصوصة التي كانت موضوعة بإزائها في زمن النبي إلى معان أخر.
The eighth topic: Do transmitted proofs yield certainty? It is said: They do not yield certainty, and this is the doctrine of the Mu'tazila and the majority of the Ash'aris, because it (i.e., their yielding certainty) depends on knowledge of the convention (al-wad'), i.e., the conventional assignment of the transmitted expressions from the Prophet to specific meanings, and (depends on) the intention (al-irada), i.e., on the knowledge that those meanings are intended by them. The first (i.e., knowledge of the convention) is established only through the transmission of lexicography (al-lugha) to determine the meanings of the individual word-roots, and the transmission of grammar (al-nahw) to ascertain the meanings of syntactic structures, and the transmission of morphology (al-sarf) to know the meanings of morphological forms. Their principles (usul) are established through isolated reports (riwayat al-ahad) because they go back to the poems of the Arabs, their proverbs, and their sayings which people like al-Asma'i, al-Khalil, and Sibawayh narrate. Even assuming the correctness of the report, error is possible, for Imru' al-Qais was corrected in many places despite being one of the greatest pre-Islamic poets. Their branches (furu') are established by analogical reasoning (al-qiyas). Both (i.e., isolated reports and analogical reasoning) are conjectural (dhanniyan) without doubt. The second (i.e., knowledge of the intention) depends on the absence of transfer (al-naql), i.e., the transfer of those expressions from their specific meanings for which they were conventionally assigned in the time of the Prophet to other meanings.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a proper statement of the problem. It begins by posing the question: Can transmitted proofs (such as the Qur'an and hadith) yield certainty (yaqin) regarding the theological claims they support? It then reports the dominant position: they do not yield certainty, and this is the view of the Mu'tazila and the majority of Ash'aris. The reason provided is that their certainty depends on two things: (1) knowledge of the linguistic convention (al-wad') – that is, the original assignment of words to meanings at the time of the Prophet – and (2) knowledge that those meanings are intended by the speaker (the Prophet). The passage then explains that the first condition (knowledge of convention) can only be established through the transmitted sciences of language (lugha), grammar (nahw), and morphology (sarf). These sciences, in turn, are based on principles (usul) that are derived from isolated reports (riwayat al-ahad) – for instance, the poems and sayings of the Arabs as collected by philologists like al-Asma'i, al-Khalil, and Sibawayh. Even if the chain of transmission is sound, error is still possible in the original utterances, as exemplified by the famous pre-Islamic poet Imru' al-Qais, who was corrected in many places. The branches (furu') of these sciences are established through analogical reasoning (qiyas). The passage concludes that both isolated reports and analogical reasoning are conjectural (dhanni) without any doubt, so the foundation of the transmitted proof is itself conjectural. The second condition (knowledge of intention) depends on the absence of transfer (naql) – that is, the words must not have been shifted from their original meanings to new ones between the time of the Prophet and the later period. Thus, the argument is that transmitted proofs cannot yield certainty because they rely on conjectural methods of linguistic transmission.

يقين
وضع
إرادة
رواية الآحاد
قياس
ظني
لغة
نحو
صرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that transmitted proofs are certain because they come from revelation.
Correction: The passage shows that even revelation reaches us through human language and transmission, which are conjectural, so transmitted proofs cannot yield certainty.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do transmitted proofs not yield certainty according to the Mu'tazila and most Ash'aris?
A) Because the Qur'an and hadith contain contradictions.
B) Because they depend on linguistic conventions and intentions, which are established by conjectural methods.
C) Because the Prophet's speech is itself uncertain.
D) Because analogical reasoning is not valid in matters of creed.

Key Takeaway
Transmitted proofs do not yield certainty because their foundation—knowledge of linguistic convention and intention—is established through conjectural methods like isolated reports and analogical reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you recite or hear a verse of the Qur'an, do you ever stop to wonder how certain you are of its intended meaning? What if the human process of transmission has introduced something less than certainty into your understanding? How does that affect your worship?

?
If the language of revelation itself is subject to the limitations of human transmission, how does that shape your humility before Allah's word? Are you willing to admit that your interpretation might be fallible, and how would that change your engagement with the text?

The Probabilistic Foundations of Linguistic Transmission
The author explains that knowledge of word meanings depends on isolated narrations and analogies, both probabilistic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the foundations of language, grammar, and morphology are probabilistic rather than certain.

Core Idea
Isolated narrations and analogies are probabilistic proofs, not certain.

In this passage, the author examines how we come to know the meanings of words and grammatical structures. He argues that the entire edifice of transmitted proofs—language, grammar, morphology—rests on isolated narrations and analogies, both of which yield only probability, not certainty. This challenges the claim that transmitted proofs are inherently certain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once, then the translation. Notice how the author lists three types of transmission: language, grammar, and morphology.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two sources of knowledge
The author distinguishes between the foundations (usul) and branches (furu') of linguistic sciences. Foundations come from isolated narrations, branches from analogies.
Both are labeled as probabilistic.
3
Understand the example of Imru' al-Qais
Even the greatest Arab poet can be in error. This shows that isolated narrations are not infallible.
This reinforces the probabilistic nature.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author states without doubt that both sources are probabilistic. This is a direct challenge to those who claim certainty for transmitted proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
آحاد من معان مخصوصة والإرادة أي وعلى العلم بأن تلك المعاني مرادة منه والأول وهو العلم بالوضع إنما يثبت بنقل اللغة حتى يتعين مدلولات جواهر الألفاظ ونقل النحو حتى يتحقق مدلولات الهيئات التركيبية ونقل الصرف حتى يعرف مدلولات هيئات المفردات وأصولها أي أصول هذه العلوم الثلاثة تثبت برواية الآحاد لأن مرجعها إلى أشعار العرب وأمثالها وأقوالها التي يرويها عنهم الناس، كالأصمعي والخليل وسيبويه وعلى تقدير صحة الرواية يجوز الخطأ العرب فإن امرأ القيس قد خطئ في مواضع عديدة مع كونه من أكابر شعراء الجاهلية وفروعها تثبت بالأقيسة وكلاهما يعني رواية الآحاد والقياس دليلان ظنيان بلا شبهة
Individual units from specific meanings and the will, meaning upon the knowledge that those meanings are intended from it, and the first, which is knowledge of the linguistic meaning, is established only through the transmission of language so that the significations of the essences of words become determined, and the transmission of grammar so that the significations of syntactic structures become verified, and the transmission of morphology so that the significations of the forms of individual words become known. And their foundations, meaning the foundations of these three sciences, are established through isolated narration because their reference is to the poems of the Arabs, their proverbs, and their statements that people relate from them, like al-Asma'i, al-Khalil, and Sibawayh. And assuming the correctness of the narration, error is permissible for the Arabs, for Imru' al-Qais was indeed made an error in numerous places despite being among the greatest poets of the Jahiliyyah. And their branches are established through analogies, and both of them, meaning isolated narration and analogy, are probabilistic proofs without doubt.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage explains the first element required for certainty in transmitted proofs: knowledge of the linguistic meaning (العلم بالوضع). The author states that such knowledge is obtained through three types of transmission: transmission of language (نقل اللغة) to determine the meanings of word roots (جواهر الألفاظ), transmission of grammar (نقل النحو) to understand the meanings of syntactic structures (هيئات تركيبية), and transmission of morphology (نقل الصرف) to know the meanings of word forms (هيئات المفردات). The foundations (أصول) of these three sciences—the evidence from Arabic poetry, proverbs, and sayings—are established by isolated narrations (رواية الآحاد) from authoritative figures like al-Asma'i, al-Khalil, and Sibawayh. However, even when a narration is authentic, error is possible, as even the great pre-Islamic poet Imru' al-Qais was corrected in multiple instances. The branches (فروع) of these sciences are derived through analogies (الأقيسة). Both isolated narration and analogy are probabilistic (ظنيان) without any doubt. Thus, the very foundation of transmitted proofs rests on probabilistic materials, undermining the claim that such proofs yield certainty.

The Six Obstacles to Certainty of Intended Meaning
Lists six obstacles that prevent knowledge of the speaker's intention, making transmitted proofs probabilistic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six factors that must be negated for certainty about the speaker's intention in transmitted proofs, and why their mere possibility prevents certainty.

Core Idea
Six linguistic possibilities make transmitted certainty only probable.

When we read a transmitted text (like the Qur'an or Hadith), we assume we understand the speaker's intention. But the author points out that there are six potential obstacles that could change the intended meaning. Only if all are ruled out can we be certain. This passage lists them and explains why certainty is hard to achieve.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a list of six absences required.
The passage is dense with technical terms.
2
Identify each of the six obstacles
Go through the passage and list each condition: absence of transfer, homonymy, metaphor, ellipsis, specification, and reordering.
Each is introduced by 'عدم' (absence of).
3
Understand why each undermines certainty
For each obstacle, consider how its presence would change the intended meaning. For example, if a word has been transferred (naql), the intended meaning is the original, not the current one.
The author gives a brief rationale for each.
4
Grasp the overall conclusion
The passage ends by stating that each of these is possible in speech, so we cannot be certain of their absence—only probability (zann) is achieved.
This is the key takeaway: transmitted proofs are probabilistic, not certain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني وهو العلم بالإرادة يتوقف على عدم النقل أي نقل تلك الألفاظ عن معانيها المخصوصة التي كانت موضوعة بإزائها في زمن النبي إلى معان أخرى، إذ على تقدير النقل يكون المراد بها تلك المعاني الأولى لا المعاني الأخرى التي نفهمها الآن منها، وعلى عدم الاشتراك إذ مع وجوده جاز أن يكون المراد معنى آخر مغايراً لما فهمناه وعدم المجاز إذ على تقدير التجوز يكون المراد المعنى المجازي لا الحقيقي الذي تبادر إلى أذهاننا وعدم الإضمار إذ لو أضمر في الكلام شيء تغير معناه عن حاله، وعدم التخصيص إذ على تقدير التخصيص كان المراد بعض ما تناوله اللفظ لا جميعه كما اعتقدناه وعدم التقديم والتأخير، فإنه إذا فرض هناك تقديم وتأخير كان المراد معنى آخر لا ما أدركناه والكل أي كل واحد من النقل وأخواته لجوازه في الكلام بحسب نفس الأمر لا يجزم بانتفائه بل غايته الظن
And the second, which is knowledge of intention, depends on the absence of transfer (naql), meaning the transfer of those words from their specific meanings that were placed in correspondence with them in the time of the Prophet to other meanings, for if transfer is assumed, the intended meaning would be those first meanings, not the other meanings that we now understand from them; and on the absence of homonymy (ishtirak), for if it exists, it is possible that the intended meaning is another meaning different from what we understood; and the absence of metaphor (majaz), for if metaphor is assumed, the intended meaning would be the metaphorical meaning, not the literal meaning that occurs to our minds; and the absence of ellipsis (idmar), for if something is omitted in the speech, its meaning changes from its state; and the absence of specification (takhsis), for if specification is assumed, the intended meaning would be part of what the word encompasses, not all of it as we believed; and the absence of reordering (taqdim wa ta'khir), for if reordering is assumed there, the intended meaning would be another meaning, not what we perceived. And all of them, meaning each one of naql and its counterparts, because their occurrence in speech according to the actual state of affairs is possible, cannot be negated with certainty, but at most they yield probability.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage discusses the second requirement for certainty in transmitted proofs: knowledge that the speaker intended the apparent meaning (العلم بالإرادة). This knowledge depends on negating six potential issues that could alter the intended meaning. The six are: 1) نقل (naql): transfer of a word from its original meaning to a new meaning, as might have occurred after the Prophet's time. If such transfer happened, the intended meaning is the original, not the current one. 2) اشتراك (ishtirak): homonymy, where a word has multiple meanings; if present, the intended meaning could be different from what we assume. 3) مجاز (majaz): metaphor; if the word is used metaphorically, the intended meaning is figurative, not literal. 4) إضمار (idmar): ellipsis; omission of part of the speech changes its meaning. 5) تخصيص (takhsis): specification; the word may be intended to refer to only part of its general scope. 6) تقديم وتأخير (taqdim wa ta'khir): reordering of elements, which can alter meaning. For each of these, the author argues that since their occurrence is possible in principle, we cannot be certain they are absent; at best we have probability (ظن). Thus, certainty in transmitted proofs is undermined.

Key Terms
العلم بالإرادة
النقل
الاشتراك
المجاز
الإضمار
التخصيص
التقديم والتأخير
ظن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: In normal speech, we assume absence of these factors, so we can be certain of intended meaning.
Correction: The author argues that the mere possibility of each factor is enough to prevent certainty; only probability is attained unless each absence is explicitly proven.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the six factors that must be negated for certainty about the intended meaning?
A) Transfer (naql)
B) Homonymy (ishtirak)
C) Abrogation (naskh)
D) Ellipsis (idmar)

Key Takeaway
Certainty in transmitted proofs is impossible because six linguistic factors could alter the intended meaning, and their absence cannot be definitively proven.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Third Condition: Absence of Rational Counter-Evidence
States that even after negating linguistic obstacles, one must also rule out rational evidence contradicting the transmitted proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why certainty in transmitted proofs depends not only on linguistic clarity but also on the absence of contradictory rational evidence.

Core Idea
Certainty in scripture requires ruling out any rational proof that contradicts it.

After ensuring both the literal meaning and the intended meaning of a transmitted proof are clear, a third, less obvious condition must be met: there must be no rational argument that proves the opposite. This passage from Sanusi makes it clear that if such a rational counter-evidence existed, it would take precedence over the transmitted proof. Thus, to achieve certainty, one must first be certain of its absence.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic sentence slowly, then read its literal translation. Notice that it follows a conditional structure: 'after these two matters... it is necessary to know the absence of a rational counter-evidence.'
The two earlier matters are knowledge of linguistic meaning and knowledge of intention, covered in previous lessons.
2
Identify the three conditions
The passage mentions three necessary conditions for certainty: (1) knowledge of the linguistic meaning, (2) knowledge of the intended meaning, and (3) knowledge that no rational counter-evidence exists. This lesson focuses on the third.
3
Understand the precedence rule
The passage states: if a rational counter-evidence existed, it 'would definitely take precedence over the transmitted proof.' Reason: rational proofs are certain and foundational, while transmitted proofs are probabilistic. So when they conflict, the rational must be preferred.
This is a key principle of traditional Islamic theology.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: How does this condition affect the way we interpret religious texts? It means that a clear rational argument can override a scriptural text, requiring allegorical interpretation or other reconciliation. This protects the primacy of reason in Islamic thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم بعد هذين الأمرين أعني العلم بالوضع والعلم بالإرادة لا بد من العلم بعدم المعارض العقلي الدال على نقيض ما دل عليه الدليل النقلي إذ لو وجد ذلك المعارض لقدم على الدليل النقلي قطعاً
Then after these two matters, meaning knowledge of the linguistic meaning and knowledge of intention, it is necessary to have knowledge of the absence of a rational counter-evidence that indicates the opposite of what the transmitted proof indicates, for if such a counter-evidence existed, it would definitely take precedence over the transmitted proof.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage completes the conditions for certainty in transmitted proofs. After establishing knowledge of the linguistic meaning (العلم بالوضع) and knowledge of intention (العلم بالإرادة), a third condition is required: certainty that there is no rational counter-evidence (معارض عقلي) that contradicts the transmitted proof. If such a rational proof exists, it must take precedence over the transmitted proof, and the transmitted proof must be interpreted allegorically or set aside. This is because rational proofs are certain and foundational, while transmitted proofs, as shown, are only probabilistic. Thus, for a transmitted proof to yield certainty, one must also be certain that no rational argument opposes it.

C) The transmitted proof is given precedence because it is scripture

Key Takeaway
Certainty from transmitted proofs requires that no rational proof contradicts them; if such rational evidence exists, it takes precedence.

Conditions for Certainty from Transmitted Proofs: Linguistic Ambiguities
Lists the linguistic conditions that must be ruled out for transmitted proofs to yield certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six linguistic conditions that must be assumed or ruled out for transmitted proofs to yield certainty, and why they only produce probability (ẓann).

Core Idea
Transmitted proofs yield only probability unless six linguistic ambiguities are eliminated.

This passage from a classical kalam work addresses a crucial methodological question: can transmitted proofs (naqli dalā'il) produce certainty? The author lists six conditions—including the assumption of transmission and the absence of five linguistic ambiguities—that must be in place. He then argues that because each of these possibilities cannot be ruled out with certainty, transmitted proofs yield only probability. This lesson unpacks each condition and its impact on interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the question
Read the opening sentence to grasp the topic: do transmitted proofs yield certainty? Then note the six conditions listed.
The first condition is the assumption that the transmitted meaning is the original meaning.
2
Examine each ambiguity
Go through each of the five linguistic ambiguities: homonymy, metaphor, ellipsis, specification, and fronting/delay. For each, consider how it could change the intended meaning.
3
Grasp the conclusion
The passage concludes that each of these possibilities cannot be ruled out with certainty, so transmitted proofs only give probability. Reflect on why that matters for theological argumentation.
4
Consider the scholarly note
Read the final remark about dropping ellipsis and including abrogation. Understand how different scholars categorize these linguistic issues.
Abrogation is seen as a form of specification by time.

The Sixth Observatory - The Eighth Topic: Do Transmitted Proofs Yield Certainty?
... (and) upon [the assumption of] transmission, the intended meaning would be those first meanings, not the other meanings we now understand from them; (and) upon the absence of homonymy, for if it exists, it is possible that the intended meaning is another meaning different from what we understood; (and) the absence of metaphor, for upon the assumption of figurative usage, the intended meaning is the metaphorical meaning, not the literal one that first came to our minds; (and) the absence of ellipsis, for if something is omitted in speech, its meaning changes from its original state; (and) the absence of specification, for upon the assumption of specification, the intended meaning is some of what the word covers, not all of it as we believed; (and) the absence of fronting and delaying, for if fronting and delaying are assumed, the intended meaning is another meaning, not what we perceived. And all of these—that is, each of transmission and its counterparts—due to its possibility in speech according to the actual state of affairs, one cannot be certain of its absence; rather, its utmost is probability. And know that some dropped ellipsis based on its inclusion in metaphor by deficiency, and mentioned abrogation, and it is as if the author included it in specification, because abrogation as it is said is specification according to times.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage lists the linguistic conditions that must be ruled out for transmitted proofs to yield certainty. It begins by stating the topic: whether transmitted proofs yield certainty. Then it enumerates five potential ambiguities: homonymy (اشتراك), metaphor (مجاز), ellipsis (إضمار), specification (تخصيص), and fronting/delay (تقديم وتأخير). For each, it explains that if any of these are present, the intended meaning may differ from what we initially understand. The passage then concludes that each of these is possible in speech, so one cannot be certain of their absence; at best one has probability (ظن). Finally, it notes a scholarly disagreement: some include ellipsis under metaphor by deficiency, and mention abrogation (نسخ) as a form of specification by time.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Transmitted proofs always yield certainty.
Correction: They only yield probability because linguistic ambiguities cannot be ruled out with certainty.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Transmitted proofs yield only probability (ẓann), not certainty, because the possibility of linguistic ambiguities cannot be definitively ruled out.

The Three Preconditions for Transmitted Proofs
Lists three preconditions for transmitted proofs and states the rule for rational contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three preconditions that must be satisfied for a transmitted proof to yield certainty: knowledge of literal meaning, knowledge of the speaker's intention, and knowledge of the absence of any rational contradiction.

Core Idea
Certainty from transmitted proofs requires literal meaning, intention, and no rational contradiction.

Before we can rely on any scriptural evidence, we must ensure three things: the words are understood literally, they are intended by the speaker, and no rational proof contradicts them. This passage establishes these foundational prerequisites and explains that if a rational contradiction exists, it takes precedence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions
Read the passage and locate the three things that must be known before a transmitted proof can be relied upon.
The passage mentions them in order: literal meaning, intention, and absence of rational contradiction.
2
Understand why each is necessary
Reflect on why knowledge of literal meaning, intention, and absence of rational contradiction are each essential for certainty.
Literal meaning ensures correct understanding; intention confirms the speaker means it; absence of rational contradiction avoids conflict with reason.
3
See the consequence if a rational contradiction exists
The passage states that if a rational contradiction exists, it takes precedence over the transmitted proof decisively.
This sets up the argument for why rational proof is prioritized.
