(Commentary: ...) 
His statement: (building on its inclusion in metaphor by deficiency) and the author's view is more precise because ellipsis is absolutely more general than metaphor by deficiency, because in metaphor by deficiency there is a change in grammatical inflection due to deletion, like 'Ask the village' [Quran 12:82], unlike ellipsis like 'Strike the rock with your staff, and it gushed forth' [Quran 2:60] i.e., he struck and it gushed forth. And he did not address metaphor by increase like 'Nothing is like Him' because it does not effect a change in meaning, so it has no bearing on the absence of intention.
His statement: (and knowledge of the intention) i.e., that it is intended with respect to the actual words due to the removal of the mentioned obstacles.
His statement: (it is necessary to know etc.) i.e., in order for it to yield certainty that it is intended by the speaker, it is necessary [to know] the absence of a rational proof. So it is not objected that after it is determined to be intended, it cannot be interpreted otherwise; otherwise it would not be intended, so there would be no rational proof for it due to the necessity of the Lawgiver's lying. Because what is meant by not knowing that it is intended with respect to words, it is necessary for it to be intended by the speaker to know the absence of a rational proof.
His statement: (and the absence of metaphor) indicates that the discussion is about proofs whose words are literal. And you may say: no proof exists unless some of its words are literal. Then the author did not mention increase like His statement 'Nothing is like Him' [Quran 42:11], nor 'I swear' and 'lest they know', so it is as if he included it in metaphor according to the opinion of some.
His statement: (building on its inclusion in metaphor by deficiency) it is not hidden that some ellipses can be included therein, like His statement 'Ask the village' but not others like His statement 'Send me, Joseph' [Quran 12:45], so the author's view is precise.
His statement: (and it takes precedence over the transmitted proof decisively by interpreting the transmitted etc.) If you say: the commentator explained the precedence...
[Page 54] The Sixth Observatory - The Eighth Topic: Do Transmitted Proofs Yield Certainty?
as that the transmitted proof is interpreted from its meaning to another meaning. An example is His statement 'The Most Merciful established Himself upon the Throne' [Quran 20:5], for it indicates sitting, and a rational proof indicating the impossibility of sitting in His case is in conflict with it, so 'establishment' is interpreted as 'taking control' or the sitting upon the Throne is made a metaphor for sovereignty. The rational proof is given precedence over the transmitted proof because it is not possible to act on both by judging the affirmation of the implication of each, as that entails the conjunction of two contradictories; nor to act on their opposites by judging the negation of the implication of each, as that entails the removal of both contradictories. And giving precedence to the transmitted over the rational by judging the affirmation of what the transmitted proof implies without what the rational proof implies is an invalidation of the principle by the branch, for the transmitted cannot be established except by reason, because the way to establish the Creator and to know prophethood and all else on which the validity of the transmitted depends is only reason. So it is the principle for the transmitted, on which its validity depends. So if one gives precedence to the transmitted over it and judges the affirmation of its implication alone, he has invalidated the principle by the branch. And in that—i.e., in invalidating the principle by the branch—there is also an invalidation of the branch, for then the validity of the transmitted becomes dependent on the judgment of reason, which is possible to be corrupt and invalid, so the transmitted would not be certainly valid. Thus, from the correction of the transmitted by giving it precedence over reason results its own lack of validity. And if the affirmation of something and its correction leads to its invalidation and corruption, it is self-contradictory—i.e., it entails its own opposite and is contrary to it—so it is invalid and impossible.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents a key theological argument: after establishing the literal meaning and the speaker's intention in a transmitted proof, one must also ensure there is no rational proof contradicting it. If such a rational proof exists, it takes precedence. The commentator explains why: acting on both is impossible due to contradiction, acting on neither is also impossible, and giving priority to transmitted over rational would undermine the foundation of transmitted proofs themselves, since their validity depends on rational proofs (e.g., proving God's existence and prophethood). This leads to a self-contradiction: trying to affirm the transmitted by prioritizing it over reason actually denies its validity. Therefore, the rational proof must be given precedence, and the transmitted proof is interpreted allegorically.

الوضع
الإرادة
المعارض العقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that knowing the literal meaning and intention alone is sufficient for certainty from a transmitted proof.
Correction: This passage shows that in addition to those, one must also ensure the absence of any rational contradiction, which otherwise would overturn the transmitted proof.

Key Takeaway
Transmitted proofs require literal meaning, intention, and no rational contradiction to yield certainty; if a rational contradiction exists, it takes precedence.

Why Rational Proof Takes Precedence Over Transmitted Proof
Presents the four possible responses to a conflict between rational and transmitted proof and argues for the precedence of rational proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument for giving rational proof precedence over transmitted proof when they conflict, including the example of 'istawa' and the self-defeating nature of prioritizing transmitted proof.

Core Idea
Prioritizing transmitted proof over rational proof invalidates the very foundation of transmitted proof.

When a transmitted text seems to contradict a rational proof, we face four possible responses. This passage systematically examines them and shows that only one is coherent: interpreting the text allegorically while upholding the rational proof. Trying to prioritize the transmitted text leads to a logical contradiction, as it undermines the rational basis on which the transmitted text itself relies.

Guided Path
1
Examine the four possible responses
The passage lists four ways to deal with a conflict: act on both, act on neither, prioritize transmitted, prioritize rational. Note which are rejected and why.
Acting on both leads to contradiction; acting on neither leads to removal of both contradictories.
2
Understand the self-defeating nature of prioritizing transmitted
The passage argues that prioritizing transmitted over rational invalidates the principle (reason) by the branch (transmitted), which also invalidates the branch itself.
Because transmitted proof depends on rational proof for its own validity (e.g., proving God's existence).
3
Learn the example of 'istawa'
The verse 'The Most Merciful established Himself upon the Throne' is interpreted allegorically to avoid contradiction with the rational proof that sitting is impossible for God.
This illustrates how allegorical interpretation works in practice.
4
Conclude the necessity of rational precedence
The passage concludes that prioritizing rational proof is the only way to avoid self-contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

المرصد السادس - المقصد الثامن : الدلائل النقلية هل تفيد اليقين
،
بأن يؤول الدليل النقلي عن معناه إلى معنى آخر مثاله قوله تعالى : الرحمن على العرش استوى ﴾ [طه:٥] فإنه يدل على الجلوس، وقد عارضه الدليل العقلي الدال على استحالة الجلوس في حقه تعالى، فيؤول الاستواء بالاستيلاء أو يجعل الجلوس على العرش كناية عن الملك، وإنما قدم المعارض العقلي على الدليل النقلي ( إذ لا يمكن العمل بهما بأن يحكم بثبوت مقتضى كل منهما لاستلزامه اجتماع النقيضين ( ولا بنقيضهما ) بأن يحكم بانتفاء مقتضى كل منهما لاستلزامه ارتفاع النقيضين ( وتقديم النقل على العقل بأن يحكم بثبوت ما يقتضيه الدليل النقلي دون ما يقتضيه الدليل العقلي إبطال للأصل (بالفرع فإن النقل لا يمكن إثباته إلا بالعقل لأن الطريق إلى إثبات الصانع ومعرفة النبوة، وسائر ما يتوقف صحة النقل عليه ليس إلا العقل، فهو أصل للنقل الذي تتوقف صحته عليه فإذا قدم النقل عليه، وحكم بثبوت مقتضاه وحده فقد أبطل الأصل بالفرع ( وفيه ) أي في إبطال الأصل بالفرع (إبطال للفرع أيضاً، إذ حينئذ يكون صحة النقل متفرعة على حكم العقل الذي يجوز فساده وبطلانه، فلا يكون النقل مقطوع الصحة، فقد لزم من تصحيح النقل بتقديمه على العقل عدم صحته ( وإذا أدى إثبات الشيء ) وتصحيحه (إلى إبطاله ) وإفساده كان مناقضاً لنفسه ) أي مستلزماً لنقيض نفسه ومنافياً لها ( فكان باطلاً)
Then after these two matters—namely, knowledge of the literal meaning and knowledge of the intention—there must be knowledge of the absence of a rational proof that indicates the opposite of what the transmitted proof indicates, for if that rational proof exists, it would take precedence over the transmitted proof decisively.
(Commentary: ...) 
His statement: (building on its inclusion in metaphor by deficiency) and the author's view is more precise because ellipsis is absolutely more general than metaphor by deficiency, because in metaphor by deficiency there is a change in grammatical inflection due to deletion, like 'Ask the village' [Quran 12:82], unlike ellipsis like 'Strike the rock with your staff, and it gushed forth' [Quran 2:60] i.e., he struck and it gushed forth. And he did not address metaphor by increase like 'Nothing is like Him' because it does not effect a change in meaning, so it has no bearing on the absence of intention.
His statement: (and knowledge of the intention) i.e., that it is intended with respect to the actual words due to the removal of the mentioned obstacles.
His statement: (it is necessary to know etc.) i.e., in order for it to yield certainty that it is intended by the speaker, it is necessary [to know] the absence of a rational proof. So it is not objected that after it is determined to be intended, it cannot be interpreted otherwise; otherwise it would not be intended, so there would be no rational proof for it due to the necessity of the Lawgiver's lying. Because what is meant by not knowing that it is intended with respect to words, it is necessary for it to be intended by the speaker to know the absence of a rational proof.
His statement: (and the absence of metaphor) indicates that the discussion is about proofs whose words are literal. And you may say: no proof exists unless some of its words are literal. Then the author did not mention increase like His statement 'Nothing is like Him' [Quran 42:11], nor 'I swear' and 'lest they know', so it is as if he included it in metaphor according to the opinion of some.
His statement: (building on its inclusion in metaphor by deficiency) it is not hidden that some ellipses can be included therein, like His statement 'Ask the village' but not others like His statement 'Send me, Joseph' [Quran 12:45], so the author's view is precise.
His statement: (and it takes precedence over the transmitted proof decisively by interpreting the transmitted etc.) If you say: the commentator explained the precedence...
[Page 54] The Sixth Observatory - The Eighth Topic: Do Transmitted Proofs Yield Certainty?
as that the transmitted proof is interpreted from its meaning to another meaning. An example is His statement 'The Most Merciful established Himself upon the Throne' [Quran 20:5], for it indicates sitting, and a rational proof indicating the impossibility of sitting in His case is in conflict with it, so 'establishment' is interpreted as 'taking control' or the sitting upon the Throne is made a metaphor for sovereignty. The rational proof is given precedence over the transmitted proof because it is not possible to act on both by judging the affirmation of the implication of each, as that entails the conjunction of two contradictories; nor to act on their opposites by judging the negation of the implication of each, as that entails the removal of both contradictories. And giving precedence to the transmitted over the rational by judging the affirmation of what the transmitted proof implies without what the rational proof implies is an invalidation of the principle by the branch, for the transmitted cannot be established except by reason, because the way to establish the Creator and to know prophethood and all else on which the validity of the transmitted depends is only reason. So it is the principle for the transmitted, on which its validity depends. So if one gives precedence to the transmitted over it and judges the affirmation of its implication alone, he has invalidated the principle by the branch. And in that—i.e., in invalidating the principle by the branch—there is also an invalidation of the branch, for then the validity of the transmitted becomes dependent on the judgment of reason, which is possible to be corrupt and invalid, so the transmitted would not be certainly valid. Thus, from the correction of the transmitted by giving it precedence over reason results its own lack of validity. And if the affirmation of something and its correction leads to its invalidation and corruption, it is self-contradictory—i.e., it entails its own opposite and is contrary to it—so it is invalid and impossible.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents a key theological argument: after establishing the literal meaning and the speaker's intention in a transmitted proof, one must also ensure there is no rational proof contradicting it. If such a rational proof exists, it takes precedence. The commentator explains why: acting on both is impossible due to contradiction, acting on neither is also impossible, and giving priority to transmitted over rational would undermine the foundation of transmitted proofs themselves, since their validity depends on rational proofs (e.g., proving God's existence and prophethood). This leads to a self-contradiction: trying to affirm the transmitted by prioritizing it over reason actually denies its validity. Therefore, the rational proof must be given precedence, and the transmitted proof is interpreted allegorically.

Key Terms
تقديم العقل على النقل
إبطال الأصل بالفرع
النقيضين
تأويل
الاستواء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that when reason and scripture conflict, scripture should always win because it is divine.
Correction: This passage shows that reason is the foundation of scripture's validity; prioritizing scripture over reason actually undermines scripture itself, leading to a self-contradiction.

Checkpoint
Why does the passage argue that giving precedence to the transmitted proof over the rational proof is invalid?
A) Because transmitted proofs are always weaker
B) Because it leads to the conjunction of two contradictories
C) Because it invalidates the principle (reason) by the branch (transmitted), and thus also invalidates the branch
D) Because rational proofs are always certain

Key Takeaway
When rational proof conflicts with transmitted proof, rational proof takes precedence, and the transmitted proof is interpreted allegorically; prioritizing the transmitted over rational leads to self-contradiction and invalidates the transmitted itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a verse that seems to describe God in human-like terms, do you immediately reject rational reasoning as prideful? How does this lesson challenge you to examine whether you have truly trusted the intellect God gave you?
?
This passage says that prioritizing scripture over reason actually invalidates scripture itself. Is there an area in your life where you have avoided thinking deeply about your beliefs, afraid that reason might weaken your faith? What would it mean for you to honestly face that fear?
?
If rational proof is the foundation for the validity of transmitted proof, then using your intellect is not optional but essential to faith. Are you actively using your intellect to understand the Qur'an and Sunnah, or have you neglected this duty? What is one step you can take this week to engage your mind more deeply with revelation?

Method of Learning
intermediate

Responding to an Objection: Interpretation After Certainty
Addresses an objection about interpreting transmitted texts after their meaning is already determined.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator refutes the objection that interpretation of transmitted texts is still possible after the literal meaning and intention are already known.

Core Idea
After the literal meaning and intention are certain, rational interpretation is not freely available.

This lesson continues the discussion of conflicting proofs. The author presents a response to a potential objection: if we already know the literal meaning and the speaker's intention, how can we reinterpret the text in light of a rational proof? The commentator clarifies the limits of interpretation, especially regarding allegory (tamthīl) and metonymy (kināyah). By the end, you will see that once meaning is determined, the possibility of reinterpretation is closed.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector thinks that after knowledge of the literal meaning and intention, interpretation is still possible. Read the passage to see how the commentator frames this.
The objector assumes that a rational proof could still be possible after the conditions are met.
2
Examine the commentator's initial reply
The commentator says the discourse is complete without this objection and that the author intended an additional point about preferring certain rational proofs.
The commentator hints that he will later prove the impossibility of such a rational proof.
3
Analyze the discussion of tamthīl and kināyah
The commentator shows that even if one suggests interpretation as allegory or metonymy, these do not allow changing the meaning arbitrarily. Pay attention to the commentator's own views on how these figures of speech work.
In kināyah, the word is not used in its original meaning at all, so it cannot support the objection.
4
Conclude the response
The commentator states that after knowledge of literal meaning and intention, there is no possibility for such interpretations, thus the objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (إذ لو وجد إلخ) لا يخفى أن الكلام يتم بدون هذا البيان بأن يقال : لا بد من العلم بعدم المعارض وإلا تساقطا لامتناع الترجيح بلا مرجح ، إلا أنه قصد إفادة أمر زائد على المقصود وهو أنه يقدم العقلي القطعي على النقلي عند التعارض.
بتأويل النقلي عن معناه إلى معنى آخر ويؤيده مثاله ولا شك أن هذا لا يصح لأن الكلام بعد العلم بالوضع والإرادة قلت : هذا بناء على ظن السائل باحتمال المعارض العقلي وجريانه بعدهما، وسيحقق الشارح فيما سيأتي عدم الجريان حيث قال وأما عدم المعارض العقلي فيعلم من صدق القائل فليس على الشارح لائمة، وقد يظن الاستبعاد للتأويل والتصرف في الكلام بضرب ما مع ثبوت الأمرين أعني العلم بالوضع والإرادة مثل الحمل على التمثيل أو الكناية، فإن المفردات الواقعة فيهما يراد بها معانيها الأصلية لكن إرادتها لإفادة المعاني الأخر، وانتقال الذهن منها إليها وحينئذ فلا اتجاه أيضاً لما يقال من أنه إذا تعين المراد بأي وجه كان دل على انتفاء المعارض العقلي، وحصل العلم بعدمه وأنت خبير بأن المختار عند الشارح كما حققه في شرح المفتاح أن اللفظ في الكناية ليس بمستعمل في المعنى الأصلي، ولم يرد هذا المعنى معه وأن التمثيل مجاز في الهيئة التركيبية كما صرح به شرح التلخيص ،وغیره، فبعد العلم بالوضع والإرادة
لا احتمال لها قطعا
His statement: (for if it existed etc.) It is not hidden that the discourse is complete without this explanation, such that one says: knowledge of the absence of a rational proof is necessary, otherwise they would both fall due to the impossibility of preference without a preponderator. However, he intended to convey an additional point beyond the intended meaning: that a certain rational proof is given precedence over the transmitted proof when they conflict, by interpreting the transmitted from its meaning to another meaning, and his example supports this. And there is no doubt that this is not valid because the discourse, after knowledge of the literal meaning and intention... I say: this is based on the questioner's assumption of the possibility of a rational proof and its occurrence after them. And the commentator will verify later its non-occurrence, where he said: 'As for the absence of a rational proof, it is known from the truthfulness of the speaker.' So there is no blame on the commentator. And one might think it unlikely to interpret and manipulate the speech with something while the two matters—knowledge of the literal meaning and intention—are established, such as interpreting as allegory (tamthīl) or metonymy (kināyah). For the individual words occurring in them are intended with their original meanings, but their intention is to convey other meanings, and the mind transfers from them to those meanings. Then there is no direction either for what is said: that if the intended meaning is determined in any way, it indicates the absence of a rational proof, and knowledge of its absence is obtained. And you are aware that the chosen position according to the commentator, as he verified in Sharh al-Miftah, is that the word in metonymy is not used in its original meaning, nor is that meaning intended with it; and that allegory is a metaphor in the composite structure, as stated in Sharh al-Talkhis and others. So after knowledge of the literal meaning and intention, there is absolutely no possibility for them (i.e., these interpretations).
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage responds to an objection. The objector seems to argue that if we already know the literal meaning and the speaker's intention in a transmitted text, then we cannot later reinterpret it in light of a rational proof. The commentator (شارح) explains that the author's statement about the necessity of ruling out a rational proof is complete without further explanation. The objector's scenario assumes that a rational proof could still be possible after the conditions are met, but the commentator will later show that it is not possible. He then discusses two types of interpretation: tamthīl (allegory) and kināyah (metonymy). In tamthīl, the individual words retain their original meanings but are used to convey other meanings metaphorically in the composite structure. In kināyah, according to the commentator's own view (from his works), the word is not used in its original meaning at all. Therefore, after the literal meaning and intention are known, such interpretations have no possibility. Thus the objection is invalid.

Key Terms
تمثيل
كناية
تأويل
المعارض العقلي
شرح المفتاح
شرح التلخيص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: After knowing the literal meaning and the speaker's intention, one can still reinterpret the text in a different sense when a rational proof conflicts.
Correction: The commentator shows that such reinterpretation is not possible because the meaning is already determined, and figures like tamthīl and kināyah do not allow changing the meaning arbitrarily.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the status of the word in kināyah (metonymy)?
A) It is used in its original meaning with additional implication
B) It is not used in its original meaning
C) It is always used metaphorically
D) It has multiple meanings

Key Takeaway
Once the literal meaning and the speaker's intention are known, there is no room for further reinterpretation in light of rational proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a clear command from Allah in the Quran, what is the state of your heart? Do you submit immediately, or do you search for interpretations that allow you to avoid its demand? How does this lesson expose your inclination to rationalize disobedience?
?
Think of a moment when you knew exactly what a verse meant, yet you allowed your desires or cultural norms to twist its meaning. What would it take for you to repent from that and accept the plain truth?
?
If you are certain about the meaning and intention of a divine text, what rational arguments would you accept to reinterpret it? Is there any such argument that could ever be more reliable than the truthfulness of the Speaker? How does this lesson challenge your ultimate allegiance?

Method of Learning

Resolving Conflict Between Reason and Revelation: The Method of Ta'wil
Sanusi defines ta'wil and gives the Throne verse as an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how ta'wil (interpretation) is used to harmonize scriptural texts that seem to contradict rational proofs, with the example of the Throne verse.

Core Idea
Ta'wil allows scripture to be understood metaphorically when reason requires it.

When a Qur'anic verse appears to attribute a physical action to Allah, such as sitting, rational theology demands an interpretation that upholds divine transcendence. Sanusi introduces the concept of ta'wil as the method of shifting the literal meaning to a metaphorical one, illustrating this with the famous verse about Allah's establishment on the Throne.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of ta'wil
Read the first sentence where the author defines ta'wil as interpreting the transmitted proof from its apparent meaning to another meaning.
2
Study the example
Look at the example of the Throne verse. Notice how the apparent meaning (sitting) is replaced by 'domination' or interpreted as a metaphor for sovereignty.
This example shows ta'wil in practice.
3
Understand the rationale
Reflect on why ta'wil is necessary: because a rational proof (from reason) proves the impossibility of sitting for Allah.
Reason is the criterion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بأن يؤول الدليل النقلي عن معناه إلى معنى آخر مثاله قوله تعالى : الرحمن على العرش استوى ﴾ [طه:٥] فإنه يدل على الجلوس، وقد عارضه الدليل العقلي الدال على استحالة الجلوس في حقه تعالى، فيؤول الاستواء بالاستيلاء أو يجعل الجلوس على العرش كناية عن الملك، وإنما قدم المعارض العقلي على الدليل النقلي ( إذ لا يمكن العمل بهما بأن يحكم بثبوت مقتضى كل منهما لاستلزامه اجتماع النقيضين ( ولا بنقيضهما ) بأن يحكم بانتفاء مقتضى كل منهما لاستلزامه ارتفاع النقيضين ( وتقديم النقل على العقل بأن يحكم بثبوت ما يقتضيه الدليل النقلي دون ما يقتضيه الدليل العقلي إبطال للأصل (بالفرع فإن النقل لا يمكن إثباته إلا بالعقل لأن الطريق إلى إثبات الصانع ومعرفة النبوة، وسائر ما يتوقف صحة النقل عليه ليس إلا العقل، فهو أصل للنقل الذي تتوقف صحته عليه فإذا قدم النقل عليه، وحكم بثبوت مقتضاه وحده فقد أبطل الأصل بالفرع ( وفيه ) أي في إبطال الأصل بالفرع (إبطال للفرع أيضاً، إذ حينئذ يكون صحة النقل متفرعة على حكم العقل الذي يجوز فساده وبطلانه، فلا يكون النقل مقطوع الصحة، فقد لزم من تصحيح النقل بتقديمه على العقل عدم صحته ( وإذا أدى إثبات الشيء ) وتصحيحه (إلى إبطاله ) وإفساده كان مناقضاً لنفسه ) أي مستلزماً لنقيض نفسه ومنافياً لها ( فكان باطلاً ومحالاً إذ
قوله : (إذ لو وجد إلخ) لا يخفى أن الكلام يتم بدون هذا البيان بأن يقال : لا بد من العلم بعدم المعارض وإلا تساقطا لامتناع الترجيح بلا مرجح ، إلا أنه قصد إفادة أمر زائد على المقصود وهو أنه يقدم العقلي القطعي على النقلي عند التعارض. بتأويل النقلي عن معناه إلى معنى آخر ويؤيده مثاله ولا شك أن هذا لا يصح لأن الكلام بعد العلم بالوضع والإرادة قلت : هذا بناء على ظن السائل باحتمال المعارض العقلي وجريانه بعدهما، وسيحقق الشارح فيما سيأتي عدم الجريان حيث قال وأما عدم المعارض العقلي فيعلم من صدق القائل فليس على الشارح لائمة، وقد يظن الاستبعاد للتأويل والتصرف في الكلام بضرب ما مع ثبوت الأمرين أعني العلم بالوضع والإرادة مثل الحمل على التمثيل أو الكناية، فإن المفردات الواقعة فيهما يراد بها معانيها الأصلية لكن إرادتها لإفادة المعاني الأخر، وانتقال الذهن منها إليها وحينئذ فلا اتجاه أيضاً لما يقال من أنه إذا تعين المراد بأي وجه كان دل على انتفاء المعارض العقلي، وحصل العلم بعدمه وأنت خبير بأن المختار عند الشارح كما حققه في شرح المفتاح أن اللفظ في الكناية ليس بمستعمل في المعنى الأصلي، ولم يرد هذا المعنى معه وأن التمثيل مجاز في الهيئة التركيبية كما صرح به شرح التلخيص ،وغیره، فبعد العلم بالوضع والإرادة لا احتمال لها قطعا
ثم لو أمكن لأمكن اجتماع النقيضين أعني نفسه ،ونقيضه، وإذا لم يمكن العمل بهما ولا بنقيضهما ولا تقديم النقلي على العقلي، فقد تعين تقديم العقلي على النقلي وهو المطلوب
That the transmitted proof (al-dalīl al-naqlī) is interpreted from its meaning to another meaning, e.g. His saying, the Exalted: 'The Most Merciful is established upon the Throne' [Ṭāhā:5]; for it indicates sitting, and the rational proof (al-dalīl al-‘aqlī) indicating the impossibility of sitting with respect to Him, the Exalted, has opposed it; so istiwā’ is interpreted as domination or sitting upon the throne is made a metaphor for sovereignty. And the rational opponent (al-mu‘āriḍ al-‘aqlī) is only given precedence over the transmitted proof because it is impossible to act by both by judging the existence of the requisite of each because that would entail the conjunction of two contradictories (ijtimā‘ al-naqīḍayn); nor by their negations by judging the non-existence of the requisite of each because that would entail the removal of both contradictories (irtifā‘ al-naqīḍayn); and giving precedence to the transmitted over the rational by judging the existence of what the transmitted proof requires without what the rational proof requires is an invalidation of the root (aṣl) by the branch (far‘). For the transmitted cannot be established except by reason, because the way to establishing the Maker and knowledge of prophethood and everything else on which the soundness of the transmitted depends is only reason; so it is the root for the transmitted whose soundness depends on it. So if one gives the transmitted precedence over it and judges the existence of its requisite alone, he has invalidated the root by the branch. And in it – i.e. in invalidating the root by the branch – there is an invalidation of the branch also, because then the soundness of the transmitted would be dependent upon the judgment of reason whose corruption and falsity is possible; so the transmitted would not be of certain (maqṭū‘) soundness. So from correcting the transmitted by giving it precedence over reason there necessarily follows its lack of soundness. And if the establishment of a thing and its correction leads to its invalidation and corruption, it is contradictory to itself – i.e. entails its own opposite and is contrary to it – so it is false and impossible. Then his saying: ('if it existed, etc.') – it is not hidden that the speech is complete without this explanation by saying: it is necessary to know the absence of an opponent (al-mu‘āriḍ) otherwise they would fall together due to the impossibility of preponderance without a preponderator; except that he intended to convey an additional matter beyond the intended, namely that he gives the certain rational precedence over the transmitted at the time of conflict, by interpreting the transmitted from its meaning to another meaning, and his example supports it. And there is no doubt that this is not correct, because speech after knowledge of the linguistic convention (al-waḍ‘) and the speaker's intention (al-irāda) – I say: this is based on the questioner's assumption of the possibility of a rational opponent and its occurrence after them; and the commentator (al-shāriḥ) will verify later its non-occurrence where he said: 'As for the absence of a rational opponent, it is known from the truthfulness of the speaker', so there is no blame on the commentator. And one might imagine avoidance of interpretation and modification of speech in a certain way while the two matters – i.e. knowledge of the linguistic convention and the intention – are established, such as carrying it upon allegory (tamthīl) or metonymy (kināya). For the individual words occurring in them are intended in their original meanings, but intending them is for conveying other meanings and the mind's transition from them to them. Then there is also no direction to what is said: that when the intended is determined in any way, it indicates the non-existence of the rational opponent and knowledge of its absence is obtained. And you are an expert that the chosen view with the commentator, as he verified in Sharḥ al-Miftāḥ, is that the word in metonymy is not used in the original meaning, nor is that meaning intended along with it, and that allegory is a metaphor in the compositional structure as explained by Sharḥ al-Talkhīṣ and others. So after knowledge of the linguistic convention and the intention, there is absolutely no possibility for it. Then if it were possible, the conjunction of two contradictories would be possible – i.e. itself and its opposite. And if it is not possible to act by both, nor by their negations, nor to give precedence to the transmitted over the rational, then giving precedence to the rational over the transmitted is determined, and that is the desired.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a proof and argument for giving precedence to rational proof over transmitted proof when they conflict, using the example of the verse about the Throne. It begins by defining ta'wīl (interpretation) as taking the transmitted proof from its apparent meaning to another meaning, as in interpreting 'istiwā'' as 'domination' or making it a metaphor for sovereignty. The author then explains why the rational proof must be given precedence: it is impossible to act by both proofs (that would require affirming both contradictories), impossible to act by the negation of both (that would require negating both, also contradictory), and impossible to prefer transmitted over rational because that would invalidate the root (reason) by the branch (transmitted), since reason is the foundation for establishing the validity of transmitted sources like the existence of God and prophethood. Moreover, if one invalidates the root, one also invalidates the branch, because then the transmitted would depend on a merely possible rational judgment, making it uncertain. The argument concludes that preferring the transmitted leads to a self-contradictory situation: the attempt to establish the transmitted actually undermines its own certainty. The marginal note clarifies that this is based on assuming rational possibility, but the commentator will later show that rational opposition does not occur when the speaker is truthful. The text then resumes with the logical conclusion: since none of the other alternatives are possible, giving precedence to rational proof over transmitted proof is necessary.

Key Terms
التأويل
الدليل النقلي
الدليل العقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ta'wil means rejecting the literal meaning entirely without basis.
Correction: Ta'wil is a principled method of metaphorical interpretation based on rational necessity, not arbitrary rejection.

Checkpoint
Why does the author use the Throne verse as an example?
A) To show that the verse is false
B) To illustrate how ta'wil resolves an apparent conflict between reason and scripture
C) To prove that Allah sits on a throne
D) To argue that reason should be rejected

Key Takeaway
Ta'wil allows Muslims to maintain both rational integrity and scriptural authority by interpreting anthropomorphic verses metaphorically.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a Quranic verse that seems to contradict what your reason affirms, do you tend to dismiss your reason or force a literal interpretation? How does this lesson challenge you to adopt a more disciplined approach like ta'wil?
?
The example of ta'wil here concerns Allah's attributes. Have you ever struggled with anthropomorphic descriptions of Allah? Did you simply accept them superficially or reject them? What would it look like for you to humbly reinterpret your initial understanding in light of reason and revelation?
?
Ta'wil requires the humility to admit that the apparent meaning may not be the intended meaning. In what area of your life—whether in theology, relationships, or personal beliefs—are you clinging to a literal interpretation despite clear evidence against it? What step will you take today to practice intellectual humility?

Method of Learning

Why Reason Takes Precedence: The Logical Argument for Prioritizing Rational Proof
Sanusi provides the logical proof for prioritizing rational proof over transmitted proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that reason must take precedence over transmitted proof when they conflict, because transmitted proof depends on reason for its own validation.

Core Idea
Subordinating reason to revelation would destroy the very foundation of revelation.

Sanusi now presents the core argument: when a rational proof and a transmitted proof conflict, the rational proof must be given priority. He shows that neither acting by both nor acting by neither is possible, and that giving priority to the transmitted would invalidate its own foundation, because reason is the basis upon which the transmitted is established. This leads to a self-contradiction, making the alternative impossible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three impossible alternatives
The author lists three impossible ways to handle the conflict: act by both, act by neither, or prefer the transmitted. Understand why each fails.
The first two lead to contradictories.
2
Understand the root-branch argument
The author argues that preferring the transmitted over reason invalidates the root (reason) by the branch (transmitted), because reason is the foundation for establishing the transmitted.
This is the key logical move.
3
Follow the self-contradiction
The argument shows that preferring the transmitted leads to its own invalidation, so it is contradictory.
Therefore the only option is to prefer reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بأن يؤول الدليل النقلي عن معناه إلى معنى آخر مثاله قوله تعالى : الرحمن على العرش استوى ﴾ [طه:٥] فإنه يدل على الجلوس، وقد عارضه الدليل العقلي الدال على استحالة الجلوس في حقه تعالى، فيؤول الاستواء بالاستيلاء أو يجعل الجلوس على العرش كناية عن الملك، وإنما قدم المعارض العقلي على الدليل النقلي ( إذ لا يمكن العمل بهما بأن يحكم بثبوت مقتضى كل منهما لاستلزامه اجتماع النقيضين ( ولا بنقيضهما ) بأن يحكم بانتفاء مقتضى كل منهما لاستلزامه ارتفاع النقيضين ( وتقديم النقل على العقل بأن يحكم بثبوت ما يقتضيه الدليل النقلي دون ما يقتضيه الدليل العقلي إبطال للأصل (بالفرع فإن النقل لا يمكن إثباته إلا بالعقل لأن الطريق إلى إثبات الصانع ومعرفة النبوة، وسائر ما يتوقف صحة النقل عليه ليس إلا العقل، فهو أصل للنقل الذي تتوقف صحته عليه فإذا قدم النقل عليه، وحكم بثبوت مقتضاه وحده فقد أبطل الأصل بالفرع ( وفيه ) أي في إبطال الأصل بالفرع (إبطال للفرع أيضاً، إذ حينئذ يكون صحة النقل متفرعة على حكم العقل الذي يجوز فساده وبطلانه، فلا يكون النقل مقطوع الصحة، فقد لزم من تصحيح النقل بتقديمه على العقل عدم صحته ( وإذا أدى إثبات الشيء ) وتصحيحه (إلى إبطاله ) وإفساده كان مناقضاً لنفسه ) أي مستلزماً لنقيض نفسه ومنافياً لها ( فكان باطلاً ومحالاً إذ
قوله : (إذ لو وجد إلخ) لا يخفى أن الكلام يتم بدون هذا البيان بأن يقال : لا بد من العلم بعدم المعارض وإلا تساقطا لامتناع الترجيح بلا مرجح ، إلا أنه قصد إفادة أمر زائد على المقصود وهو أنه يقدم العقلي القطعي على النقلي عند التعارض. بتأويل النقلي عن معناه إلى معنى آخر ويؤيده مثاله ولا شك أن هذا لا يصح لأن الكلام بعد العلم بالوضع والإرادة قلت : هذا بناء على ظن السائل باحتمال المعارض العقلي وجريانه بعدهما، وسيحقق الشارح فيما سيأتي عدم الجريان حيث قال وأما عدم المعارض العقلي فيعلم من صدق القائل فليس على الشارح لائمة، وقد يظن الاستبعاد للتأويل والتصرف في الكلام بضرب ما مع ثبوت الأمرين أعني العلم بالوضع والإرادة مثل الحمل على التمثيل أو الكناية، فإن المفردات الواقعة فيهما يراد بها معانيها الأصلية لكن إرادتها لإفادة المعاني الأخر، وانتقال الذهن منها إليها وحينئذ فلا اتجاه أيضاً لما يقال من أنه إذا تعين المراد بأي وجه كان دل على انتفاء المعارض العقلي، وحصل العلم بعدمه وأنت خبير بأن المختار عند الشارح كما حققه في شرح المفتاح أن اللفظ في الكناية ليس بمستعمل في المعنى الأصلي، ولم يرد هذا المعنى معه وأن التمثيل مجاز في الهيئة التركيبية كما صرح به شرح التلخيص ،وغیره، فبعد العلم بالوضع والإرادة لا احتمال لها قطعا

Method of Learning
intermediate

Objection: Suspending Judgment on Both Proofs
Objection and response regarding suspension of judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why suspending judgment between conflicting rational and transmitted proofs is not a viable option.

Core Idea
Suspending judgment does not escape contradiction—it destroys certainty.

The objector proposes a seemingly neutral alternative: when rational and transmitted proofs conflict, simply suspend judgment on both. This would avoid affirming either contradictory. But does it really work? The response shows that suspension still undermines the certainty required for both proofs, making it a false refuge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the objector suggests suspension as a way out.
The objector claims that if one suspends judgment, none of the impossibilities follow.
2
Identify the objector's claim
The objector says that by suspending judgment on both proofs, one avoids having to accept either conclusion, thus avoiding logical impossibilities.
3
Follow the first response
The arguer responds that this denial does not harm the arguer. Because if a rational opponent exists, suspension does not remove it; certainty requires knowing the opponent is absent.
Suspension does not provide knowledge of absence, so transmitted proof remains uncertain.
4
Follow the second response
The second point: suspension also introduces possibility of error into the certain rational proof. If the rational proof becomes uncertain, the transmitted proof cannot be a certain proof that justifies suspension.
Thus, suspension fails to resolve the conflict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : جاز أن يتوقف فيهما فلا يحكم بثبوت مقتضى شيء منهما بعينه، فلا يلزم شيء من تلك المحالات لأنا نقول : هذا منع لا يضر المعلل لأن وجود المعارض العقلي إذا أوجب التوقف لم يفد الدليل النقلي اليقين ما لم يعلم عدم ذلك المعارض، وهذا هو الوجه الذي كان المستدل بصدده وأيضاً التوقف يوجب تطرق احتمال الخطأ في الدليل العقلي القطعي، وحينئذ لا يبقى النقلي حجة قطعية يتوقف لأجلها في الدلائل العقلية القطعية
It cannot be said: It is possible to suspend judgment concerning both (al-tawaqquf fīhimā), so that one does not judge the existence of the requisite of either one of them specifically, so nothing from those impossibilities follows. For we say: This is a denial that does not harm the arguer, because the existence of the rational opponent (al-mu‘āriḍ al-‘aqlī), if it necessitates suspension, does not make the transmitted proof yield certainty as long as one does not know the absence of that opponent; and this is the aspect with which the argument was concerned. Also, suspension entails the possibility of error in the certain rational proof (al-dalīl al-‘aqlī al-qaṭ‘ī), and then the transmitted proof does not remain a certain proof (ḥujja qaṭ‘iyya) for whose sake one may suspend judgment concerning certain rational proofs.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its response. The objector suggests a third option: when rational and transmitted proofs conflict, one might simply suspend judgment on both, neither affirming the rational nor the transmitted conclusion. This would avoid the logical impossibilities (i.e., affirming both contradictories or rejecting both). The response refutes this by pointing out two consequences. First, suspension still prevents the transmitted proof from yielding certainty because certainty requires knowledge that there is no rational opponent; suspension does not provide that knowledge. Second, suspension introduces the possibility of error into the rational proof itself, which is supposed to be certain (qaṭ‘ī). If the rational proof becomes uncertain, then the transmitted proof cannot be a certain proof that would justify suspending judgment over certain rational proofs. Thus, suspension is not a valid alternative.

Key Terms
التوقف
المعارض العقلي
اليقين
الدليل العقلي القطعي
الحجة القطعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Suspending judgment is a neutral, safe option that avoids all problems.
Correction: This passage shows that suspension still prevents certainty in the transmitted proof and undermines the certainty of the rational proof, so it fails as a resolution.

Checkpoint
According to the response, why does suspending judgment on both proofs fail to resolve the conflict?
A) Because suspension is logically impossible
B) Because suspension prevents the transmitted proof from yielding certainty and also introduces possibility of error into the certain rational proof
C) Because suspension is against scholarly consensus
D) Because suspension leads to affirm both contradictories

Key Takeaway
Suspending judgment on conflicting proofs is not a neutral escape; it destroys the certainty required for both proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter conflicting evidence regarding a belief you hold, do you tend to suspend judgment to avoid discomfort? How does this lesson reveal that such suspension actually destroys certainty rather than preserving it? What is one area of your faith where you have chosen suspension instead of seeking resolution?
?
If a rational argument against a core Islamic belief seems strong, do you ever 'pause' on that belief, thinking it safe to neither affirm nor deny? How does the concept of the 'rational opponent' force you to recognize that suspension leaves your transmitted proofs without certainty? What step can you take today to restore certainty in that area?

The Conjectural Nature of Transmitted Proofs
The author explains why transmitted proofs are conjectural.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why transmitted proofs are inherently conjectural and cannot yield certainty without additional indications.

Core Idea
Transmitted proofs are conjectural because they depend on ten conjectural matters.

We have seen that for a transmitted proof to give certainty, we must know that no rational opponent exists. But such knowledge is not certain, as non-finding does not guarantee non-existence. In this lesson, we examine the reasoning that leads to the conclusion that transmitted proofs are conjectural by nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the condition for certainty
Read the Arabic passage once, noting the claim that knowing the absence of a rational opponent is necessary but not certain.
Notice the phrase 'الغاية عدم الوجدان' which means 'the utmost is non-finding'.
2
Understand why non-finding is not decisive
The passage explains that non-finding does not yield certainty of non-existence because a rational opponent might exist without being found.
3
Grasp the dependence on ten conjectural matters
The transmitted proof's indication depends on ten conjectural matters, making the indication itself conjectural. The analogy of branch and root illustrates that the dependent cannot be stronger than its basis.
This leads to the conclusion that transmitted proofs are not profitable for certainty.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this argument challenges the reliability of transmitted proofs in theology without supporting qara'in.
This sets the stage for the correct view in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد ثبت أنه لا بد في إفادة الدليل النقلي اليقين من العلم بعدم المعارض العقلي ) لكن عدم المعارض العقلي غير يقيني، إذ الغاية عدم الوجدان ( مع المبالغة الكاملة في تتبع الأدلة العقلية (وهو) أي عدم الوجدان ( لا يفيد القطع ) والجزم ( بعدم الوجود ) إذ يجوز أن يكون هناك معارض عقلي لم نطلع عليه ( فقد تحقق أن (دلالتها أي دلالة الأدلة النقلية على مدلولاتها ( يتوقف على أمور ) عشرة ( ظنية فتكون ) دلالتها أيضاً ( ظنية لأن الفرع) الموقوف (لا يزيد على الأصل الذي هو الموقوف عليه (في القوة) والمتانة، وإذا كانت دلالتها ظنية لم تكن مفيدة لليقين بمدلولاتها هذا ما قيل ( والحق أنها ) أي الدلائل النقلية (قد تفيد اليقين أي في الشرعيات (بقرائن) مشاهدة) من المنقول عنه (أو متواترة نقلت إلينا تواتراً ( تدل ) تلك القرائن ( على انتفاء الاحتمالات) المذكورة ) فإنا نعلم استعمال لفظ الأرض والسماء ونحوهما من الألفاظ المشهورة المتداولة ) فيما بين جميع أهل اللغة ( في زمن الرسول في معانيها التي تراد منها الآن والتشكيك فيه سفسطة ) لا شبهة في بطلانها، وكذا الحال في صيغة الماضي والمضارع والأمر
قوله : ( بقرائن مشاهدة ) كما للحاضرين في صحبة النبي قوله : ( أو متواترة كما للغائبين عنها في مثل الدلائل الذالة على فرضية الصلاة والصوم .
قوله : ( فقد ثبت أنه لا بد إلخ ) قد وقع في بعض النسخ قبيل هذا، وإذا لم يمكن العمل بهما ولا بنقيضهما ولا تقديم النقلي على العقلي، فقد تعين تقديم العقلي على النقلي وهو المطلوب لا يقال : جاز أن يتوقف فيهما ولا يحكم بثبوت مقتضى شيء منهما بعينه، فلا يلزم شيء من تلك المحالات، لأنا نقول : هذا منع لا يضر المعلل لأن وجود المعارض العقلي، إذا أوجب التوقف لم يفد الدليل النقلي اليقين ما لم يعلم عدم ذلك المعارض، وهذا هو الذي كان المستدل بصدده، وأيضاً التوقف يوجب تطرق احتمال الخطأ في الدليل العقلي القطعي، وحينئذ لا يبقى النقل حجة قطعية يتوقف لأجلها في الدلائل العقلية القطعية إلى ها هنا كلام ذلك البعض من النسخ.
So it has been established that it is necessary, in order for the transmitted proof to yield certainty (al-yaqīn), to have knowledge of the absence of a rational opponent (al-mu‘āriḍ al-‘aqlī). But the absence of a rational opponent is not certain, for the utmost is the non-finding (even with complete thoroughness in pursuing rational proofs). And it – i.e. the non-finding – does not yield certainty (al-qaṭ‘) and decisiveness of non-existence, for it is possible that there is a rational opponent of which we have not become aware. So it has been realized that their indication – i.e. the indication of the transmitted proofs upon their meanings – depends upon ten conjectural (ẓanniyya) matters, so their indication is also conjectural, because the branch (the dependent) does not surpass the root (the depended-upon) in strength and firmness. And if their indication is conjectural, they are not profitable for certainty of their meanings. This is what was said. But the correct view (al-ḥaqq) is that they – i.e. the transmitted proofs – may yield certainty, i.e. in religious matters (al-shar‘iyyāt), by means of indications (qarā’in) that are witnessed (mushāhada) from the one reported from, or that are recurrent (mutawātira) transmitted to us recurrently. Those indications indicate the negation of the mentioned probabilities. For we know the usage of the word 'earth' and 'sky' and similar well-known common words among all the people of language in the time of the Messenger in their meanings that are intended now; and doubting that is sophistry (safsafa) whose falsehood is not doubtful. And likewise is the case with the past tense, the present tense, and the imperative. His saying: ('by witnessed indications') – as for those present in the companionship of the Prophet. His saying: ('or recurrent') – as for those absent from that, in the case of indications pointing to the obligation of prayer and fasting. His saying: ('So it has been established that it is necessary, etc.') – it has occurred in some copies before this: 'And if it is not possible to act by both, nor by their negations, nor to give precedence to the transmitted over the rational, then giving precedence to the rational over the transmitted is determined, and that is the desired. It cannot be said: It is possible to suspend judgment concerning both, so that one does not judge the existence of the requisite of either one of them specifically, so nothing from those impossibilities follows. For we say: This is a denial that does not harm the arguer, because the existence of the rational opponent, if it necessitates suspension, does not make the transmitted proof yield certainty as long as one does not know the absence of that opponent; and this is the aspect with which the argument was concerned. Also, suspension entails the possibility of error in the certain rational proof, and then the transmitted does not remain a certain proof for whose sake one may suspend judgment concerning certain rational proofs.' Up to here is the speech of that part of the copies.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents the conclusion of the argument and then the correct view. First, it states that for a transmitted proof to give certainty, one must know there is no rational opponent; but such knowledge is not certain because one may not find an opponent, but non-finding does not prove non-existence. Then it notes that the indication of transmitted proofs depends on ten conjectural matters, making the indication itself conjectural. However, the correct view (al-ḥaqq) is that transmitted proofs can yield certainty in religious matters through witnessed or recurrently transmitted indications (qarā’in) that eliminate the probabilities. Examples are the usage of common words like 'earth' and 'sky' which are known to have the same meaning in the time of the Prophet; any doubt about that is sophistry. The same applies to grammatical forms. The marginal notes clarify the types of indications: witnessed (for those present) and recurrent (for those absent, like the obligations of prayer and fasting). The final marginal note explains a variant reading in some copies that repeats the earlier argument about the necessity of giving precedence to rational proof and the rejection of suspension.

المعارض العقلي
الظن
الأصل
الفرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if no rational opponent is found, the transmitted proof automatically gives certainty.
Correction: The passage shows that non-finding is not certain; a rational opponent might exist undetected, so certainty is not attained.

Checkpoint
Why does the absence of a rational opponent not yield certainty?
A) Because rational opponents are always present
B) Because non-finding does not guarantee non-existence
C) Because the transmitted proof is weak
D) Because there are ten conjectural matters

Key Takeaway
Transmitted proofs are conjectural because their indication depends on ten conjectural matters, and the absence of a rational opponent cannot be known with certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a transmitted proof (e.g., a hadith), do you automatically treat it as certain, or do you honestly examine whether rational objections might exist? What would it cost you to acknowledge that your understanding might be conjectural?
?
If the strongest transmitted proof can only give conjecture, how does that change the way you hold your beliefs? Are you willing to re-evaluate any of your cherished beliefs in light of this?
?
This lesson says non-discovery does not prove non-existence. Where in your life have you assumed that because you haven't found a problem, there is none? Will you actively search for rational opponents to your beliefs, even if it feels uncomfortable?

How Transmitted Proofs Can Yield Certainty
The author affirms that transmitted proofs can yield certainty through qara'in.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that transmitted proofs can yield certainty in religious matters through witnessed or recurrently transmitted qara'in that eliminate probabilities.

Core Idea
Transmitted proofs yield certainty through qara'in that eliminate all probable doubts.

Although transmitted proofs are conjectural in principle, the correct view (al-ḥaqq) is that they can yield certainty when supported by qara'in. These are either witnessed directly from the Prophet or transmitted recurrently (mutawatir) to later generations. In this lesson, we explore how such indications remove doubt and affirm certainty in religious rulings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage presenting the correct view
Read the Arabic text from 'والحق أنها' to the explanation of 'بقرائن مشاهدة' and 'أو متواترة'.
Notice that the passage begins by stating that transmitted proofs may yield certainty, specifically in religious matters (الشرعيات).
2
Identify the two types of qara'in
The passage mentions witnessed indications (مشاهدة) for those present with the Prophet, and recurrent indications (متواترة) for those absent. Understand how each works.
Witnessed indications rely on direct observation, while recurrent indications rely on mass transmission.
3
Examine the example of word usage
The passage gives the example of common words like 'earth' and 'sky' whose meanings are known to be unchanged from the time of the Prophet. Doubting this is sophistry.
This illustrates how qara'in can eliminate the probability of semantic change.
4
Reflect on the significance of this view
Consider how this correct view reconciles the need for certainty in religious knowledge with the inherent conjectural nature of transmitted proofs.
This is important for establishing reliable theological and legal foundations.

Source Text

?

?

?
This lesson distinguishes between those who witnessed the Prophet (mushahada) and those who rely on mutawatir reports. You belong to the latter group. Are you truly living as if the mutawatir commands of prayer and fasting are certain, or do you treat them as optional suggestions? What would change in your life if you fully embraced the certainty this lesson describes?

Why Transmitted Proofs Do Not Yield Certainty
The author argues that transmitted proofs cannot yield certainty and depend on ten presumptive matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical theological argument that transmitted (naqli) proofs cannot provide certainty (yaqin) when they conflict with rational proofs, and why their dependence on ten presumptive matters makes them presumptive (zanni).

Core Idea
Transmitted proofs are presumptive because their certainty depends on uncertain rational checks.

This passage tackles a fundamental issue in Islamic epistemology: what happens when a transmitted proof (from revelation) seems to conflict with a rational proof? The author argues that transmitted proofs cannot yield certainty because resolving the conflict leads to contradictions or suspension, and the absence of a rational counterargument cannot be known with certainty. In the end, transmitted proofs rest on ten presumptive foundations, making their conclusions presumptive as well.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the full translation to grasp the overall flow of the argument.
Notice the compressed style typical of kalam texts.
2
Identify the initial conflict scenario
The passage starts by describing a conflict between a transmitted and a rational proof. Follow the logical possibilities: acting on both, acting on the opposite of both, or giving precedence. See why each leads to impossibility or undermines the transmitted proof.
3
Analyze the objection and the author's response
The author considers an objection: perhaps we can suspend judgment on both proofs. Understand why the author rejects this: suspension itself shows the transmitted proof lacks certainty, and it introduces doubt into the rational proof.
This is a key move in the argument.
4
Grasp the conclusion about the ten presumptive matters
The author concludes that transmitted proofs depend on ten presumptive matters and that the absence of a rational counterargument is not certain. Therefore, transmitted proofs are presumptive, not certain.
Reflect on why this matters for Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الثامن : الدلائل النقلية هل تفيد اليقين لو أمكن لأمكن اجتماع النقيضين أعني نفسه ونقيضه، وإذا لم يمكن العمل بهما ولا بنقيضهما ولا تقديم النقلي على العقلي، فقد تعين تقديم العقلي على النقلي وهو المطلوب لا يقال : جاز أن يتوقف فيهما فلا يحكم بثبوت مقتضى شيء منهما بعينه، فلا يلزم شيء من تلك المحالات لأنا نقول : هذا منع لا يضر المعلل لأن وجود المعارض العقلي إذا أوجب التوقف لم يفد الدليل النقلي اليقين ما لم يعلم عدم ذلك المعارض، وهذا هو الوجه الذي كان المستدل بصدده وأيضاً التوقف يوجب تطرق احتمال الخطأ في الدليل العقلي القطعي، وحينئذ لا يبقى النقلي حجة قطعية يتوقف لأجلها في الدلائل العقلية القطعية فقد ثبت أنه لا بد في إفادة الدليل النقلي اليقين من العلم بعدم المعارض العقلي لكن عدم المعارض العقلي غير يقيني، إذ الغاية عدم الوجدان مع المبالغة الكاملة في تتبع الأدلة العقلية وهو أي عدم الوجدان لا يفيد القطع والجزم بعدم الوجود إذ يجوز أن يكون هناك معارض عقلي لم نطلع عليه فقد تحقق أن دلالتها أي دلالة الأدلة النقلية على مدلولاتها يتوقف على أمور عشرة ظنية فتكون دلالتها أيضاً ظنية لأن الفرع الموقوف لا يزيد على الأصل الذي هو الموقوف عليه في القوة والمتانة، وإذا كانت دلالتها ظنية لم تكن مفيدة لليقين بمدلولاتها هذا ما قيل
The Sixth Observatory - Eighth Purpose: Do transmitted proofs yield certainty? If it were possible, it would necessitate the conjunction of two contradictories, namely itself and its opposite. And if it is not possible to act upon both, nor upon their opposites, nor to give precedence to the transmitted over the rational, then the precedence of the rational over the transmitted is determined, and that is what is sought. It is not said that it is permissible to suspend judgment concerning them, so no judgement is made about the affirmation of the requirement of either of them specifically, and thus none of those impossibilities follow, because we say that this is a denial that harms the argument, because the existence of a rational objection, if it necessitates suspension, does not make the transmitted proof yield certainty until the absence of that objection is known. And this is the point that the arguer was addressing. Also, suspension necessitates the possibility of error in the definitive rational proof, and then the transmitted would not remain a definitive proof for whose sake suspension in definitive rational proofs is made. So it is established that for a transmitted proof to yield certainty, it is necessary to have knowledge of the absence of a rational counterargument. But the absence of a rational counterargument is not certain, because the utmost is non-observation, despite complete diligence in tracing rational proofs, and it (i.e., non-observation) does not yield certainty and affirmation of non-existence, because it is possible that there is a rational counterargument that we have not come across. So it is realized that their indication, i.e., the indication of transmitted proofs of their meanings, depends upon ten presumptive matters, so their indication is also presumptive, because the dependent branch does not exceed the root upon which it depends in strength and solidity. And if their indication is presumptive, then they are not productive of certainty in their meanings. This is what was said.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents the argument that transmitted (naqli) proofs do not yield certainty. It begins by stating the problem: when rational and transmitted proofs conflict, if we try to act on both or their opposites, contradiction arises; if we give precedence to the transmitted, then rational proof is undermined—so the rational must be preferred (thus transmitted cannot give certainty against it). The author then considers an objection: perhaps we can suspend judgment on both, avoiding any impossibility. The answer is that this does not help, because suspension itself shows that the transmitted proof does not give certainty until the absence of a rational counterargument is known—which is precisely the claim. Moreover, suspending a definitive rational proof introduces the possibility of error in it, so the transmitted would no longer be a definitive basis for suspension. The argument then concludes that for a transmitted proof to give certainty, one must know there is no rational counterargument, but that absence is not certain: the best we have is non-observation, which does not prove non-existence. Therefore, the indication of transmitted proofs depends on ten presumptive (zanni) matters, so their indication itself is presumptive—the branch cannot be stronger than the root. Hence transmitted proofs do not yield certainty.

Key Terms
الدليل النقلي
الدليل العقلي
المعارض العقلي
التوقف
الظن
القطع
الأمور العشرة الظنية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that transmitted proofs (from revelation) are automatically certain because they come from divine sources.
Correction: This argument shows that transmitted proofs are not automatically certain; they depend on rational scrutiny and are only presumptive when they conflict with rational proofs.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that transmitted proofs do not yield certainty?
A) Because they always contradict rational proofs.
B) Because their indication depends on ten presumptive matters and the absence of a rational counterargument is uncertain.
C) Because they are not from the Prophet.
D) Because they are only for legal rulings.

Key Takeaway
Transmitted proofs cannot yield certainty because their indication relies on presumptive foundations and the absence of rational counterarguments can never be known with certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a religious belief based on scripture, do you ever pause to consider that it might be merely presumptive, not absolutely certain? How does that possibility make you feel, and what are you willing to do to examine that belief rationally?
?
Imagine you encounter a clear rational argument that contradicts a verse you have always accepted. Do you instinctively reject the rational argument, or do you honestly investigate? What does your reaction reveal about your trust in reason as a gift from God?
?
This lesson forces you to accept that transmitted proofs can never give you absolute certainty. Can you live with that uncertainty, or does it threaten your faith? What concrete step will you take today to embrace intellectual humility in your religious convictions?

Transmitted Proofs Yield Certainty in Shar‘ī Matters with Indications
The author states the correct view and provides linguistic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how transmitted proofs, when accompanied by observed or mass-transmitted indications, can yield definitive certainty in religious matters.

Core Idea
Transmitted proofs give certainty in shar‘ī matters through observed or mass-transmitted indications.

In this passage, al-Ījī asserts the correct view that transmitted proofs are not inherently uncertain. He argues that with the proper indications – either observed directly or transmitted via tawātur – these proofs can produce certainty, especially in shar‘ī matters. The examples of everyday language illustrate how knowledge of linguistic convention and speaker intention is achieved.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the examples of words like 'earth' and 'sky'.
2
Identify the key terms
Find the terms 'قرائن مشاهدة' (observed indications) and 'متواترة' (mass-transmitted). Understand how they function in the argument.
3
Understand the nine probabilities
The passage mentions 'الاحتمالات التسعة' (nine probabilities). Reflect on why they are negated by the indications.
The nine probabilities refer to the possible interpretations of a word that are eliminated by context.
4
Connect to the broader debate
Consider the context of the debate on whether transmitted proofs yield certainty. This passage presents the author's position.

Source Text

والحق أنها أي الدلائل النقلية قد تفيد اليقين أي في الشرعيات بقرائن مشاهدة من المنقول عنه أو متواترة نقلت إلينا تواتراً تدل تلك القرائن على انتفاء الاحتمالات المذكورة فإنا نعلم استعمال لفظ الأرض والسماء ونحوهما من الألفاظ المشهورة المتداولة فيما بين جميع أهل اللغة في زمن الرسول في معانيها التي تراد منها الآن والتشكيك فيه سفسطة لا شبهة في بطلانها، وكذا الحال في صيغة الماضي والمضارع والأمر قوله : ( بقرائن مشاهدة ) كما للحاضرين في صحبة النبي قوله : ( أو متواترة كما للغائبين عنها في مثل الدلائل الذالة [؟] على فرضية الصلاة والصوم . قوله : ( فقد ثبت أنه لا بد إلخ قد وقع في بعض النسخ قبيل هذا، وإذا لم يمكن العمل بهما ولا بنقيضهما ولا تقديم النقلي على العقلي، فقد تعين تقديم العقلي على النقلي وهو المطلوب لا يقال : جاز أن يتوقف فيهما ولا يحكم بثبوت مقتضى شيء منهما بعينه، فلا يلزم شيء من تلك المحالات، لأنا نقول : هذا منع لا يضر المعلل لأن وجود المعارض العقلي، إذا أوجب التوقف لم يفد الدليل النقلي اليقين ما لم يعلم عدم ذلك المعارض، وهذا هو الذي كان المستدل بصدده، وأيضاً التوقف يوجب تطرق احتمال الخطأ في الدليل العقلي القطعي، وحينئذ لا يبقى النقل حجة قطعية يتوقف لأجلها في الدلائل العقلية القطعية إلى ها هنا كلام ذلك البعض من النسخ.
المرصد السادس - المقصد الثامن : الدلائل النقلية هل تفيد اليقين
واسم الفاعل وغيرها، فإنها معلومة الاستعمال في ذلك الزمان فيما يراد منها في زماننا وكذا رفع الفاعل ونصب المفعول وجر المضاف إليه مما علم معانيها قطعا ، فإذا انضم إلى مثل هذه الألفاظ قرائن مشاهدة أو منقولة تواتراً تحقق العلم بالوضع والإرادة، وانتفت تلك الاحتمالات التسعة وأما عدم المعارض العقلي، فيعلم من صدق القائل، فإنه إذا تعين المعنى وكان مراداً له فلو كان هناك معارض عقلي لزم كذبه ( نعم في إفادتها اليقين في العقليات نظر لأنه ) أي كونها مفيدة لليقين ( مبني على أنه هل يحصل بمجردها أي بمجرد الدلائل النقلية والنظر فيها وكون قائلها صادقاً ) ( الجزم بعدم المعارض العقلي و أنه (هل للقرينة) التي تشاهد أو تنقل تواتراً ( مدخل في ذلك ) أي الجزم بعدم المعارض العقلي ( وهما ) أي حصول ذلك الجزم
قوله : ( إلى مثل هذه الألفاظ أي الألفاظ التي علم قطعاً استعمالها في معانيها المفهومة عنها من حيث جواهرها وهيئاتها .
قوله : (قرائن مشاهدة أو منقولة تواتراً تدل على نفي تلك الاحتمالات . قوله : (تحقق العلم بالوضع) أي بوضع تلك الألفاظ لتلك المعاني وإرادتها منها بالنظر إليها لا إرادتها بالنسبة إلى المتكلم.
قوله : ( فإنه إذا تعين المعنى بسبب كون اللفظ مستعملاً فيه قطعاً .
قوله: (وكان مراداً له أي تعين كونه مراداً للمتكلم بواسطة القرائن المشاهدة، أو المتواترة الدالة على انتفاء الاحتمالات المذكورة، وكونه شرعياً أي مستفاداً من خطاب الشارع، إذ لو لم يكن مراداً له مع انتفاء قرينة دالة على عدم الإرادة كان ذلك إضلالاً لا إرشاداً. قوله : ( لأنه مبني على أنه هل إلخ أي مبني على جواب هذا الاستفهام فإن كان حصول الجزم بعدم المعارض بمجرد الدلائل النقلية وصدق قائلها من غير مدخلية للقرينة في ذلك كانت مفيدة لليقين في العقليات أيضاً للاشتراك في العلة وإن كان للقرينة مدخل في حصول الجزم بعدم المعارض لم تكن مفيدة لليقين في العقليات لعدم تحقق تلك القرينة فيها، بخلاف الشرعيات وحاصل الاعتراض أن هذا الفرق نظري لأن مدار الجزم المذكور على صدق القائل فإن كان مجزوماً به حصل الجزم بعدم المعارض فيهما وإلا فلا وحاصل الجواب بيان الفرق بأن المراد بالشرعيات ما لا يدرك بدونه فإذا ورد الدليل النقلي فيما هو شرعي وكان هناك قرينة مشاهدة أو متواترة تنفي تلك الاحتمالات حصل الجزم يكون معناه مراداً للمتكلم قطعاً، وحصل الجزم يعدم المعارض إذ الحكم شرعي ليس للعقل طريق إلى إثباته ونفيه فإذا أخبر القائل الصادق بأحدهما بكلام لا يحتمل غير ذلك علم قطعاً أن الآخر منتف، وإلا لزم كذبه بخلاف الدليل النقلي الوارد فيما هو عقلي أي ما يكون للعقل طريق إلى إثباته ونفيه فإنه يجوز أن يكون من الممتنعات فالقرائن المشاهدة والمتواترة الدالة على نفي تلك الاحتمالات لا تفيد الجزم بكون معناه مراداً للمتكلم، لاحتمال أن يعتمد المتكلم في عدم إرادته على قرينة كونه من الممتنعات العقلية فإنه
But the correct view is that they, i.e., transmitted proofs, may yield certainty, i.e., in shar‘ī matters, by means of observed or mass-transmitted indications (qarā'in) that indicate the negation of the aforementioned probabilities. For we know the usage of the word 'earth' and 'sky' and similar common words among all the people of the language at the time of the Prophet in their meanings that are intended now, and doubt about it is sophistry, no doubt about its falsehood. Similarly, the situation is the same for the form of the past tense, the present tense, and the imperative. His words: 'by observed indications' as for those present in the company of the Prophet. His words: 'or mass-transmitted' as for those absent from it, such as the proofs indicating [؟] the obligation of prayer and fasting. His words: 'So it is established that it is necessary etc.' has occurred in some copies before this: 'And if it is not possible to act upon both, nor upon their opposites, nor to give precedence to the transmitted over the rational, then the precedence of the rational over the transmitted is determined, and that is what is sought. It is not said that it is permissible to suspend judgment...' up to here is the speech of that part of the copies.
The Sixth Observatory - Eighth Purpose: Do transmitted proofs yield certainty? And the noun of the actor and others, for their usage in that time in what is intended from them in our time is known. Similarly, the raising of the subject, the accusative of the object, and the genitive of the possessive, are matters whose meanings are known definitively. So when there are added to such words observed or mass-transmitted indications, knowledge of the convention and intention is realized, and those nine probabilities are negated. As for the absence of a rational counterargument, it is known from the truthfulness of the speaker, because if the meaning is determined and intended by him, then if there were a rational counterargument, it would necessitate his lying. Yes, in their giving certainty in rational matters there is consideration, because it (i.e., their being productive of certainty) is based on whether by them alone (i.e., by transmitted proofs and consideration of them and the truthfulness of their speaker) certainty of the absence of a rational counterargument is obtained, and whether the indication (qarīna) that is observed or mass-transmitted has an entry in that (i.e., in certainty of the absence of a rational counterargument). And they are (i.e., the obtaining of that certainty...). His words: 'to such words', i.e., words whose usage in their understood meanings is known definitively. His words: 'observed or mass-transmitted indications indicating the negation of those probabilities.' His words: 'knowledge of the convention and intention is realized', i.e., convention of those words for those meanings and their intention from them with consideration of them, not their intention with respect to the speaker. His words: 'for if the meaning is determined' due to the word being used in it definitively. His words: 'and was intended by him', i.e., its being intended by the speaker by means of observed or mass-transmitted indications indicating the negation of the aforementioned probabilities, and its being shar‘ī, i.e., derived from the address of the Lawgiver, for if it were not intended by him with the negation of an indication denoting non-intention, that would be misguidance, not guidance. His words: 'because it is based on whether etc.', i.e., based on the answer to this question. For if the obtaining of certainty of the absence of a counterargument is by transmitted proofs alone and the truthfulness of their speaker without any entry of the indication in that, then they would be productive of certainty in rational matters as well, due to the sharing of the cause. But if the indication has an entry in obtaining certainty of the absence of a counterargument, then they would not be productive of certainty in rational matters because that indication is not realized in them, unlike shar‘ī matters. The gist of the objection is that this distinction is theoretical, because the pivot of that certainty is the truthfulness of the speaker: if it is certain, then certainty of the absence of a counterargument is obtained in both, otherwise not. The gist of the answer is the clarification of the distinction: that what is meant by shar‘ī matters is what cannot be grasped without it. So when a transmitted proof occurs concerning something that is shar‘ī and there is an observed or mass-transmitted indication negating those probabilities, then certainty is obtained that its meaning is intended by the speaker definitively, and certainty of the absence of a counterargument is obtained, because the ruling is shar‘ī and the intellect has no way to affirm or deny it. So if the truthful speaker reports one of them with speech that does not admit anything else, it is known definitively that the other is negated; otherwise, his lying would follow. Unlike a transmitted proof occurring concerning something that is rational, i.e., what the intellect has a way to affirm and deny, for it is possible that it is among the impossibilities. So observed and mass-transmitted indications that negate those probabilities do not yield certainty that its meaning is intended by the speaker, due to the possibility that the speaker relies for its non-intention on the indication of its being among the intellectual impossibilities, for he...
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents the correct view (al-ḥaqq) that transmitted proofs can yield certainty in shar‘ī (religious) matters by means of observed (mushāhada) or mass-transmitted (mutawātira) indications (qarā'in). The author first gives examples: we know definitively that words like 'earth' and 'sky' were used in their current meanings at the time of the Prophet; doubting that is sophistry. Similarly, grammatical forms like past tense, active participle, etc., are known. When such words are accompanied by observed or mass-transmitted indications, knowledge of the convention (wad‘) and intention (irāda) is realized, and the nine probabilities (iḥtimālāt) are negated. Then the discussion turns to the absence of a rational counterargument: that is known from the truthfulness of the speaker—if the meaning is determined and intended, then a contrary rational proof would imply lying. The author then notes that there is consideration (naẓar) in whether transmitted proofs give certainty in rational matters ('aqliyyāt). This depends on two issues: whether the transmitted proof alone (with truthfulness) can give certainty of the absence of a rational counterargument, and whether the indication (qarīna) plays a role in that certainty. The commentary explains: if certainty of no counterargument comes solely from the transmitted proof and truthfulness (without the indication), then it would work for both shar‘ī and rational matters. But if the indication is needed, then it works only for shar‘ī matters because such indications are not available in rational matters. An objection is raised: this distinction is theoretical because it all depends on the certainty of the speaker's truthfulness. The answer clarifies: shar‘ī matters are those that the intellect cannot independently grasp. So when the truthful speaker gives a clear report about a shar‘ī matter, the lack of a rational counterargument is certain because reason has no independent basis; otherwise, lying would follow. But in rational matters, the intellect can affirm or deny, so there remains the possibility that the reported meaning is impossible, and thus the indications do not yield certainty of intention.

القرائن المشاهدة
المتواترة
الشرعيات
