Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that transmitted proofs can never yield certainty because they are based on human transmission.
Correction: The author shows that with proper observed or mass-transmitted indications, certainty is achievable in shar‘ī matters, giving linguistic examples.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is required for transmitted proofs to yield certainty in shar‘ī matters?
A) The proofs must be from the Qur'an only
B) Observed or mass-transmitted indications that negate probabilities
C) The use of rational arguments alongside
D) The acceptance by all scholars

Key Takeaway
Transmitted proofs can yield certainty in shar‘ī matters when accompanied by observed or mass-transmitted indications that negate possible interpretations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily practice of Islam, do you treat the transmitted knowledge from the Qur’an and Sunnah as certain, or do you allow doubts from interpretational possibilities to weaken your conviction? How can you actively strengthen your certainty by seeking the indications (qarā'in) that confirm the intended meaning?
?
When you encounter a verse or hadith that seems ambiguous, do you search for the observed or mass-transmitted indications that would resolve the ambiguity, or do you settle for personal opinion? What one change can you make today to pursue certainty in your understanding of shar‘ī matters?
?
The author argues that ordinary words like 'earth' and 'sky' are universally understood and that doubting them is sophistry. Are there areas of your faith where you have allowed intellectual doubts to obscure the clear, mass-transmitted meaning of the texts? Ask Allah for the tawfīq to submit to the evident truth.

The Distinction Between Shar‘ī and Rational Matters in Transmitted Proofs
The author discusses the limitation of transmitted proofs in rational matters and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why transmitted proofs yield certainty in shar‘ī matters but not in rational matters, based on the role of the intellect and the nature of the ruling.

Core Idea
Transmitted proofs give certainty in shar‘ī matters because the intellect cannot independently judge, but not in rational matters where the intellect can assess possibility.

This passage addresses a crucial nuance: while transmitted proofs can yield certainty in religious matters, their ability to produce certainty in rational matters is debatable. The author explains the distinction by considering whether the intellect has independent access to the subject. The objection that the distinction is theoretical is answered by clarifying the different roles of the intellect in each domain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Focus on the objection and the answer.
2
Identify the key concepts
Note the terms 'العقليات' (rational matters) and 'الشرعيات' (shar‘ī matters). Understand the role of the intellect in each.
3
Follow the objection
The objection claims the distinction is theoretical because certainty depends on truthfulness. See why it is raised.
4
Understand the answer
The answer clarifies that in shar‘ī matters, the intellect has no independent way, so a clear report gives certainty. In rational matters, the possibility of impossibility remains.

قوله : (قرائن مشاهدة أو منقولة تواتراً تدل على نفي تلك الاحتمالات . قوله : (تحقق العلم بالوضع) أي بوضع تلك الألفاظ لتلك المعاني وإرادتها منها بالنظر إليها لا إرادتها بالنسبة إلى المتكلم.
قوله : ( فإنه إذا تعين المعنى بسبب كون اللفظ مستعملاً فيه قطعاً .
قوله: (وكان مراداً له أي تعين كونه مراداً للمتكلم بواسطة القرائن المشاهدة، أو المتواترة الدالة على انتفاء الاحتمالات المذكورة، وكونه شرعياً أي مستفاداً من خطاب الشارع، إذ لو لم يكن مراداً له مع انتفاء قرينة دالة على عدم الإرادة كان ذلك إضلالاً لا إرشاداً. قوله : ( لأنه مبني على أنه هل إلخ أي مبني على جواب هذا الاستفهام فإن كان حصول الجزم بعدم المعارض بمجرد الدلائل النقلية وصدق قائلها من غير مدخلية للقرينة في ذلك كانت مفيدة لليقين في العقليات أيضاً للاشتراك في العلة وإن كان للقرينة مدخل في حصول الجزم بعدم المعارض لم تكن مفيدة لليقين في العقليات لعدم تحقق تلك القرينة فيها، بخلاف الشرعيات وحاصل الاعتراض أن هذا الفرق نظري لأن مدار الجزم المذكور على صدق القائل فإن كان مجزوماً به حصل الجزم بعدم المعارض فيهما وإلا فلا وحاصل الجواب بيان الفرق بأن المراد بالشرعيات ما لا يدرك بدونه فإذا ورد الدليل النقلي فيما هو شرعي وكان هناك قرينة مشاهدة أو متواترة تنفي تلك الاحتمالات حصل الجزم يكون معناه مراداً للمتكلم قطعاً، وحصل الجزم يعدم المعارض إذ الحكم شرعي ليس للعقل طريق إلى إثباته ونفيه فإذا أخبر القائل الصادق بأحدهما بكلام لا يحتمل غير ذلك علم قطعاً أن الآخر منتف، وإلا لزم كذبه بخلاف الدليل النقلي الوارد فيما هو عقلي أي ما يكون للعقل طريق إلى إثباته ونفيه فإنه يجوز أن يكون من الممتنعات فالقرائن المشاهدة والمتواترة الدالة على نفي تلك الاحتمالات لا تفيد الجزم بكون معناه مراداً للمتكلم، لاحتمال أن يعتمد المتكلم في عدم إرادته على قرينة كونه من الممتنعات العقلية فإنه
But the correct view is that they, i.e., transmitted proofs, may yield certainty, i.e., in shar‘ī matters, by means of observed or mass-transmitted indications (qarā'in) that indicate the negation of the aforementioned probabilities. For we know the usage of the word 'earth' and 'sky' and similar common words among all the people of the language at the time of the Prophet in their meanings that are intended now, and doubt about it is sophistry, no doubt about its falsehood. Similarly, the situation is the same for the form of the past tense, the present tense, and the imperative. His words: 'by observed indications' as for those present in the company of the Prophet. His words: 'or mass-transmitted' as for those absent from it, such as the proofs indicating [؟] the obligation of prayer and fasting. His words: 'So it is established that it is necessary etc.' has occurred in some copies before this: 'And if it is not possible to act upon both, nor upon their opposites, nor to give precedence to the transmitted over the rational, then the precedence of the rational over the transmitted is determined, and that is what is sought. It is not said that it is permissible to suspend judgment...' up to here is the speech of that part of the copies.
The Sixth Observatory - Eighth Purpose: Do transmitted proofs yield certainty? And the noun of the actor and others, for their usage in that time in what is intended from them in our time is known. Similarly, the raising of the subject, the accusative of the object, and the genitive of the possessive, are matters whose meanings are known definitively. So when there are added to such words observed or mass-transmitted indications, knowledge of the convention and intention is realized, and those nine probabilities are negated. As for the absence of a rational counterargument, it is known from the truthfulness of the speaker, because if the meaning is determined and intended by him, then if there were a rational counterargument, it would necessitate his lying. Yes, in their giving certainty in rational matters there is consideration, because it (i.e., their being productive of certainty) is based on whether by them alone (i.e., by transmitted proofs and consideration of them and the truthfulness of their speaker) certainty of the absence of a rational counterargument is obtained, and whether the indication (qarīna) that is observed or mass-transmitted has an entry in that (i.e., in certainty of the absence of a rational counterargument). And they are (i.e., the obtaining of that certainty...). His words: 'to such words', i.e., words whose usage in their understood meanings is known definitively. His words: 'observed or mass-transmitted indications indicating the negation of those probabilities.' His words: 'knowledge of the convention and intention is realized', i.e., convention of those words for those meanings and their intention from them with consideration of them, not their intention with respect to the speaker. His words: 'for if the meaning is determined' due to the word being used in it definitively. His words: 'and was intended by him', i.e., its being intended by the speaker by means of observed or mass-transmitted indications indicating the negation of the aforementioned probabilities, and its being shar‘ī, i.e., derived from the address of the Lawgiver, for if it were not intended by him with the negation of an indication denoting non-intention, that would be misguidance, not guidance. His words: 'because it is based on whether etc.', i.e., based on the answer to this question. For if the obtaining of certainty of the absence of a counterargument is by transmitted proofs alone and the truthfulness of their speaker without any entry of the indication in that, then they would be productive of certainty in rational matters as well, due to the sharing of the cause. But if the indication has an entry in obtaining certainty of the absence of a counterargument, then they would not be productive of certainty in rational matters because that indication is not realized in them, unlike shar‘ī matters. The gist of the objection is that this distinction is theoretical, because the pivot of that certainty is the truthfulness of the speaker: if it is certain, then certainty of the absence of a counterargument is obtained in both, otherwise not. The gist of the answer is the clarification of the distinction: that what is meant by shar‘ī matters is what cannot be grasped without it. So when a transmitted proof occurs concerning something that is shar‘ī and there is an observed or mass-transmitted indication negating those probabilities, then certainty is obtained that its meaning is intended by the speaker definitively, and certainty of the absence of a counterargument is obtained, because the ruling is shar‘ī and the intellect has no way to affirm or deny it. So if the truthful speaker reports one of them with speech that does not admit anything else, it is known definitively that the other is negated; otherwise, his lying would follow. Unlike a transmitted proof occurring concerning something that is rational, i.e., what the intellect has a way to affirm and deny, for it is possible that it is among the impossibilities. So observed and mass-transmitted indications that negate those probabilities do not yield certainty that its meaning is intended by the speaker, due to the possibility that the speaker relies for its non-intention on the indication of its being among the intellectual impossibilities, for he...
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents the correct view (al-ḥaqq) that transmitted proofs can yield certainty in shar‘ī (religious) matters by means of observed (mushāhada) or mass-transmitted (mutawātira) indications (qarā'in). The author first gives examples: we know definitively that words like 'earth' and 'sky' were used in their current meanings at the time of the Prophet; doubting that is sophistry. Similarly, grammatical forms like past tense, active participle, etc., are known. When such words are accompanied by observed or mass-transmitted indications, knowledge of the convention (wad‘) and intention (irāda) is realized, and the nine probabilities (iḥtimālāt) are negated. Then the discussion turns to the absence of a rational counterargument: that is known from the truthfulness of the speaker—if the meaning is determined and intended, then a contrary rational proof would imply lying. The author then notes that there is consideration (naẓar) in whether transmitted proofs give certainty in rational matters ('aqliyyāt). This depends on two issues: whether the transmitted proof alone (with truthfulness) can give certainty of the absence of a rational counterargument, and whether the indication (qarīna) plays a role in that certainty. The commentary explains: if certainty of no counterargument comes solely from the transmitted proof and truthfulness (without the indication), then it would work for both shar‘ī and rational matters. But if the indication is needed, then it works only for shar‘ī matters because such indications are not available in rational matters. An objection is raised: this distinction is theoretical because it all depends on the certainty of the speaker's truthfulness. The answer clarifies: shar‘ī matters are those that the intellect cannot independently grasp. So when the truthful speaker gives a clear report about a shar‘ī matter, the lack of a rational counterargument is certain because reason has no independent basis; otherwise, lying would follow. But in rational matters, the intellect can affirm or deny, so there remains the possibility that the reported meaning is impossible, and thus the indications do not yield certainty of intention.

الشرعيات
المعارض العقلي
قرائن
اليقين
صدق القائل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The distinction between shar'iyyat and aqliyyat is merely theoretical and has no real impact.
Correction: The answer shows that the distinction is real because the intellect's access differs, affecting whether transmitted proofs can yield certainty.

Checkpoint
Why do transmitted proofs not yield certainty in rational matters according to the answer?
A) Because rational matters are always uncertain
B) Because the intellect can judge them, so the possibility of the meaning being impossible prevents certainty
C) Because there are no indications in rational matters
D) Because the speaker is not truthful

Key Takeaway
Transmitted proofs yield certainty in shar‘ī matters because the intellect cannot independently judge, but they do not yield certainty in rational matters due to the possibility of rational impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a religious text that seems to conflict with reason, do you immediately try to rationalize it away or do you pause and consider that your intellect may not be the ultimate judge? This lesson shows that in shar‘ī matters, certainty is possible precisely because the intellect cannot judge. How willing are you to submit to revelation even when it doesn't align with your personal reasoning?
?
Think of a time when you dismissed a prophetic hadith because it seemed rationally impossible. Did you consider that the Prophet (peace be upon him) might have relied on the impossibility itself as a sign that he did not mean the literal interpretation? What would it mean for your faith if you took this lesson to heart?
?
The objection in this lesson comes from someone who thought the distinction was purely theoretical. Are there areas in your own understanding of Islam where you treat all transmitted texts the same way, ignoring the difference between shar‘ī and ‘aqlī matters? What change in your approach to learning this lesson demands of you today?

Certainty from Transmitted Proofs in Legal Matters
Author explains how transmitted proofs yield certainty in shar'i matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how transmitted proofs yield certainty in legal (shar'i) matters through known linguistic usage and contextual clues, and how the speaker's truthfulness ensures the absence of rational contradiction.

Core Idea
Transmitted proofs give certainty when the meaning is unambiguous and the speaker is truthful.

In this passage, the author explains that transmitted proofs (adillah naqliyya) can indeed produce certainty in matters of Islamic law (shar'iyyat). He argues that the meanings of words are known with certainty through their established usage, and when observed or widely transmitted contextual clues accompany the text, they eliminate alternative interpretations. Moreover, the absence of a rational contradiction is inferred from the truthfulness of the speaker. Let's read the original Arabic and unpack his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author builds his argument step by step.
Pay attention to the terms 'قرائن مشاهدة' and 'منقولة تواتراً'.
2
Identify the key components
The passage states that transmitted proofs yield certainty through: (1) known linguistic usage, (2) contextual clues (observed or transmitted), and (3) the speaker's truthfulness eliminating rational contradiction.
3
Understand the 'nine possibilities'
The author mentions that contextual clues negate nine possibilities. These are alternative interpretations like metaphor, abrogation, etc. The certainty comes from eliminating them.
This is a standard concept in usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الثامن : الدلائل النقلية هل تفيد اليقين واسم الفاعل وغيرها، فإنها معلومة الاستعمال في ذلك الزمان فيما يراد منها في زماننا وكذا رفع الفاعل ونصب المفعول وجر المضاف إليه مما علم معانيها قطعا ، فإذا انضم إلى مثل هذه الألفاظ قرائن مشاهدة أو منقولة تواتراً تحقق العلم بالوضع والإرادة، وانتفت تلك الاحتمالات التسعة وأما عدم المعارض العقلي، فيعلم من صدق القائل، فإنه إذا تعين المعنى وكان مراداً له فلو كان هناك معارض عقلي لزم كذبه ( نعم في إفادتها اليقين في العقليات نظر لأنه ) أي كونها مفيدة لليقين ( مبني على أنه هل يحصل بمجردها أي بمجرد الدلائل النقلية والنظر فيها وكون قائلها صادقاً ) ( الجزم بعدم المعارض العقلي و أنه (هل للقرينة) التي تشاهد أو تنقل تواتراً ( مدخل في ذلك ) أي الجزم بعدم المعارض العقلي ( وهما ) أي حصول ذلك الجزم قوله : ( إلى مثل هذه الألفاظ أي الألفاظ التي علم قطعاً استعمالها في معانيها المفهومة عنها من حيث جواهرها وهيئاتها . قوله : (قرائن مشاهدة أو منقولة تواتراً تدل على نفي تلك الاحتمالات . قوله : (تحقق العلم بالوضع) أي بوضع تلك الألفاظ لتلك المعاني وإرادتها منها بالنظر إليها لا إرادتها بالنسبة إلى المتكلم. قوله : ( فإنه إذا تعين المعنى بسبب كون اللفظ مستعملاً فيه قطعاً . قوله: (وكان مراداً له أي تعين كونه مراداً للمتكلم بواسطة القرائن المشاهدة، أو المتواترة الدالة على انتفاء الاحتمالات المذكورة، وكونه شرعياً أي مستفاداً من خطاب الشارع، إذ لو لم يكن مراداً له مع انتفاء قرينة دالة على عدم الإرادة كان ذلك إضلالاً لا إرشاداً. قوله : ( لأنه مبني على أنه هل إلخ أي مبني على جواب هذا الاستفهام فإن كان حصول الجزم بعدم المعارض بمجرد الدلائل النقلية وصدق قائلها من غير مدخلية للقرينة في ذلك كانت مفيدة لليقين في العقليات أيضاً للاشتراك في العلة وإن كان للقرينة مدخل في حصول الجزم بعدم المعارض لم تكن مفيدة لليقين في العقليات لعدم تحقق تلك القرينة فيها، بخلاف الشرعيات وحاصل الاعتراض أن هذا الفرق ،نظري لأن مدار الجزم المذكور على صدق القائل فإن كان مجزوماً به حصل الجزم بعدم المعارض فيهما وإلا فلا وحاصل الجواب بيان الفرق بأن المراد بالشرعيات ما لا يدرك بدونه فإذا ورد الدليل النقلي فيما هو شرعي وكان هناك قرينة مشاهدة أو متواترة تنفي تلك الاحتمالات حصل الجزم يكون معناه مراداً للمتكلم قطعاً، وحصل الجزم يعدم المعارض إذ الحكم شرعي ليس للعقل طريق إلى إثباته ونفيه فإذا أخبر القائل الصادق بأحدهما بكلام لا يحتمل غير ذلك علم قطعاً أن الآخر منتف، وإلا لزم كذبه بخلاف الدليل النقلي الوارد فيما هو عقلي أي ما يكون للعقل طريق إلى إثباته ونفيه فإنه يجوز أن يكون من الممتنعات فالقرائن المشاهدة والمتواترة الدالة على نفي تلك الاحتمالات لا تفيد الجزم بكون معناه مراداً للمتكلم، لاحتمال أن يعتمد المتكلم في عدم إرادته على قرينة كونه من الممتنعات العقلية فإنه فلا جرم بمجردها، ومدخلية القرينة فيه مما لا يمكن الجزم بأحد طرفيه ) أي النفي والإثبات كانت إفادتها اليقين في العقليات محل نظر ،وتأمل فإن قلت : إن كان صدق القائل مجزوماً به لزم منه الجزم بعدم المعارض في العقليات كما لزم منه ذلك في الشرعيات وإلا احتمل كلامه الكذب فيهما فلا فرق بينهما، قلت: المراد بالشرعيات أمور يجزم العقل بإمكانها ثبوتاً وانتفاء ولا طريق له إليها، والمراد بالعقليات ما ليس كذلك، وحينئذ جاز أن يكون من الممتنعات فلأجل هذا الاحتمال ربما لم يحصل الجزم بعدم المعارض العقلي للدليل النقلي في العقليات، وإن حصل الجزم به في الشرعيات وذلك بخلاف الأدلة العقلية في العقليات، فإنها بمجردها أقوى القرائن
The Sixth Observation - Eighth Intention: Transmitted proofs, do they yield certainty? And the active participle and others, for their usage in that time is known for what is intended from them in our time, and likewise the nominative of the subject and the accusative of the object and the genitive of the annexed, which their meanings are known with certainty. So when to such words are added observed contextual clues or those transmitted successively, knowledge of the linguistic meaning and intention is realized, and those nine possibilities are negated. As for the absence of rational contradiction, it is known from the truthfulness of the speaker, for if the meaning is determined and it is intended by him, then if there were a rational contradiction, his falsehood would follow. (Yes, in their yielding certainty in rational matters there is consideration, because it) i.e., that they yield certainty (is based on whether it is attained through them alone, i.e., through transmitted proofs themselves and reflection in them and the truthfulness of their speaker) (certainty of the absence of rational contradiction) and (whether the contextual clue) which is observed or transmitted successively (has an entry in that) i.e., certainty of the absence of rational contradiction (and these two) i.e., attainment of that certainty... (He said: 'to such words' i.e., the words whose usage in their understood meanings from the perspective of their essences and forms is known with certainty. He said: 'observed or transmitted successively contextual clues indicate the negation of those possibilities.' He said: 'knowledge of the linguistic meaning is realized' i.e., the linguistic meanings of those words and their intention with respect to them, not their intention with respect to the speaker. He said: 'for if the meaning is determined because the word is used in it with certainty.' He said: 'and it is intended by him' i.e., it is determined to be intended by the speaker by means of the observed or successively transmitted contextual clues indicating the negation of the mentioned possibilities, and it being legal i.e., derived from the address of the Lawgiver, since if it were not intended by him with the absence of a contextual clue indicating non-intention, that would be misguidance not guidance. He said: 'because it is based on whether etc.' i.e., based on the answer to this question, for if the attainment of certainty of the absence of contradiction through transmitted proofs alone and the truthfulness of their speaker without the contextual clue having an entry in that, then they would yield certainty in rational matters as well due to the commonality of the cause; but if the contextual clue has an entry in attaining certainty of the absence of contradiction, then they would not yield certainty in rational matters due to the non-existence of that contextual clue in them, unlike legal matters. And the summary of the objection is that this distinction is theoretical because the basis of the mentioned certainty is the speaker's truthfulness; if it is certain, then certainty of the absence of contradiction is attained in both, otherwise not. And the summary of the answer is the clarification of the distinction: that by legal matters is meant what is not grasped without it, so when the transmitted proof occurs in what is legal and there is an observed or successively transmitted contextual clue negating those possibilities, certainty is attained that its meaning is intended by the speaker with certainty, and certainty is attained of the absence of contradiction because the ruling is legal, the intellect has no way to affirm or negate it; so if the truthful speaker reports one of them with speech that does not admit otherwise, it is known with certainty that the other is negated, otherwise his falsehood would follow. Unlike the transmitted proof that occurs in what is rational, i.e., what the intellect has a way to affirm or negate, for it may be among the impossible things. So the observed and successively transmitted contextual clues indicating the negation of those possibilities do not yield certainty that its meaning is intended by the speaker, due to the possibility that the speaker relies in his non-intention on the contextual clue of its being among the rational impossibilities. Therefore, inevitably, through them alone and the entry of the contextual clue in that, it is not possible to be certain of one of the two sides (i.e., negation and affirmation). Their yielding certainty in rational matters is a matter of consideration and reflection. So if you say: If the truthfulness of the speaker is certain, then certainty of the absence of contradiction in rational matters follows from it, just as it follows in legal matters; otherwise, his speech may be false in both, so there is no difference between them. I say: By legal matters is meant matters that the intellect judges as possible in existence and non-existence, and it has no way to them; and by rational matters is meant what is not like that. Then it is possible that it is among the impossibilities, and due to this possibility, certainty of the absence of rational contradiction may not be attained for the transmitted proof in rational matters, although certainty is attained in legal matters. And that is unlike rational proofs in rational matters, for they by themselves are the strongest contextual clues.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument about whether transmitted (naqli) proofs can yield certainty (yaqin) in rational (aqli) matters. The author begins by stating the issue and then explains how in legal (shar'i) matters transmitted proofs do yield certainty because we know with certainty the meanings of words and their usage, and when observed or successively transmitted contextual clues (qara'in) are present, they negate the nine possibilities (such as metaphor, abrogation, etc.). Then, regarding the absence of rational contradiction, this is known from the truthfulness of the speaker: if the meaning is determined and intended, any rational contradiction would imply falsehood. The author then notes that the question of whether transmitted proofs yield certainty in rational matters depends on two sub-issues: (1) whether the certainty of no rational contradiction is attained solely through the transmitted proofs and the speaker's truthfulness, and (2) whether contextual clues play a role in that certainty. If the first is true, then transmitted proofs would yield certainty in aqli matters too; but if contextual clues are necessary, then they do not because such contextual clues are lacking in aqli matters. The author then presents an objection: since the certainty of no contradiction depends on the speaker's truthfulness, if that truthfulness is certain, then it should work equally in both types of matters. The answer is that legal matters are defined as matters that the intellect deems possible in themselves but has no way to determine them, so when a truthful speaker reports one possible option with unambiguous speech, the other option is known to be false. In contrast, rational matters are those the intellect can in principle affirm or negate, so there remains a possibility that the reported content is impossible; hence the contextual clues cannot guarantee intention. Therefore, transmitted proofs in aqli matters do not yield certainty, unlike rational proofs themselves which are the strongest contextual clues. The argument carefully distinguishes the epistemic role of possibility and the scope of reason.

Key Terms
الدلائل النقلية (transmitted proofs)
اليقين (certainty)
القرائن (contextual clues)
الاحتمالات التسعة (the nine possibilities)
المعارض العقلي (rational contradiction)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that transmitted proofs never yield certainty because they rely on human language and transmission.
Correction: The author shows that in legal matters, with known usage and proper contextual clues, transmitted proofs do yield certainty.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the role of contextual clues (qara'in) in transmitted proofs?
A) They create doubt about the meaning.
B) They negate the nine possibilities and confirm the intended meaning.
C) They are only needed in rational matters.
D) They are irrelevant if the speaker is truthful.

Key Takeaway
Transmitted proofs yield certainty in legal matters when contextual clues eliminate ambiguity and the speaker's truthfulness assures no rational contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse or hadith, do you actively seek out the contextual clues that determine its intended meaning, or do you rely on your initial impression? How can you train yourself to be more attentive to the qarā'in that eliminate ambiguity?
?
If you know that the truthfulness of Allah and His Messenger guarantees that no genuine rational contradiction exists in a clear text, how does that change your response when you encounter a text that seems to conflict with reason? Are you quick to reinterpret or to reconcile?
?
Reflect on a time when you misunderstood a shari'ah text because you overlooked one of the nine possibilities (e.g., thinking it was literal when it was actually a metaphor). How does this lesson call you to approach the study of the Qur'an and Sunnah with greater care and humility?

The Limitation of Transmitted Proofs in Rational Matters
Author explains why transmitted proofs yield certainty in shar'i matters but not in aqli matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why transmitted proofs do not yield certainty in rational (aqli) matters, due to the possibility that the reported content may be rationally impossible.

Core Idea
Rational matters allow for impossibility, so transmitted proofs cannot guarantee certainty unlike purely legal matters.

After establishing the certainty of transmitted proofs in legal matters, the author turns to rational matters (aqliyyat) and notes a problem. He presents an objection and its resolution, ultimately showing that transmitted proofs in rational matters do not yield certainty because the intellect can assess possibility. This lesson unpacks that critical distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the phrase 'نعم في إفادتها اليقين في العقليات نظر' which signals the shift in focus.
2
Identify the two sub-issues
The passage states that the matter rests on two points: whether certainty of no rational contradiction is attained through the proofs alone, and whether contextual clues have an entry in that.
The author uses 'مبني على أنه هل يحصل بمجردها ... وهل للقرينة مدخل'.
3
Understand the objection and answer
An objection says: if the speaker is truthful, why wouldn't the same certainty apply? The answer distinguishes legal matters (possible in themselves, intellect has no way) from rational matters (intellect can judge possibility), so in the latter, the possibility of impossibility prevents certainty.
The key is that in rational matters, the content could be impossible, so contextual clues cannot override that possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الثامن : الدلائل النقلية هل تفيد اليقين واسم الفاعل وغيرها، فإنها معلومة الاستعمال في ذلك الزمان فيما يراد منها في زماننا وكذا رفع الفاعل ونصب المفعول وجر المضاف إليه مما علم معانيها قطعا ، فإذا انضم إلى مثل هذه الألفاظ قرائن مشاهدة أو منقولة تواتراً تحقق العلم بالوضع والإرادة، وانتفت تلك الاحتمالات التسعة وأما عدم المعارض العقلي، فيعلم من صدق القائل، فإنه إذا تعين المعنى وكان مراداً له فلو كان هناك معارض عقلي لزم كذبه ( نعم في إفادتها اليقين في العقليات نظر لأنه ) أي كونها مفيدة لليقين ( مبني على أنه هل يحصل بمجردها أي بمجرد الدلائل النقلية والنظر فيها وكون قائلها صادقاً ) ( الجزم بعدم المعارض العقلي و أنه (هل للقرينة) التي تشاهد أو تنقل تواتراً ( مدخل في ذلك ) أي الجزم بعدم المعارض العقلي ( وهما ) أي حصول ذلك الجزم قوله : ( إلى مثل هذه الألفاظ أي الألفاظ التي علم قطعاً استعمالها في معانيها المفهومة عنها من حيث جواهرها وهيئاتها . قوله : (قرائن مشاهدة أو منقولة تواتراً تدل على نفي تلك الاحتمالات . قوله : (تحقق العلم بالوضع) أي بوضع تلك الألفاظ لتلك المعاني وإرادتها منها بالنظر إليها لا إرادتها بالنسبة إلى المتكلم. قوله : ( فإنه إذا تعين المعنى بسبب كون اللفظ مستعملاً فيه قطعاً . قوله: (وكان مراداً له أي تعين كونه مراداً للمتكلم بواسطة القرائن المشاهدة، أو المتواترة الدالة على انتفاء الاحتمالات المذكورة، وكونه شرعياً أي مستفاداً من خطاب الشارع، إذ لو لم يكن مراداً له مع انتفاء قرينة دالة على عدم الإرادة كان ذلك إضلالاً لا إرشاداً. قوله : ( لأنه مبني على أنه هل إلخ أي مبني على جواب هذا الاستفهام فإن كان حصول الجزم بعدم المعارض بمجرد الدلائل النقلية وصدق قائلها من غير مدخلية للقرينة في ذلك كانت مفيدة لليقين في العقليات أيضاً للاشتراك في العلة وإن كان للقرينة مدخل في حصول الجزم بعدم المعارض لم تكن مفيدة لليقين في العقليات لعدم تحقق تلك القرينة فيها، بخلاف الشرعيات وحاصل الاعتراض أن هذا الفرق ،نظري لأن مدار الجزم المذكور على صدق القائل فإن كان مجزوماً به حصل الجزم بعدم المعارض فيهما وإلا فلا وحاصل الجواب بيان الفرق بأن المراد بالشرعيات ما لا يدرك بدونه فإذا ورد الدليل النقلي فيما هو شرعي وكان هناك قرينة مشاهدة أو متواترة تنفي تلك الاحتمالات حصل الجزم يكون معناه مراداً للمتكلم قطعاً، وحصل الجزم يعدم المعارض إذ الحكم شرعي ليس للعقل طريق إلى إثباته ونفيه فإذا أخبر القائل الصادق بأحدهما بكلام لا يحتمل غير ذلك علم قطعاً أن الآخر منتف، وإلا لزم كذبه بخلاف الدليل النقلي الوارد فيما هو عقلي أي ما يكون للعقل طريق إلى إثباته ونفيه فإنه يجوز أن يكون من الممتنعات فالقرائن المشاهدة والمتواترة الدالة على نفي تلك الاحتمالات لا تفيد الجزم بكون معناه مراداً للمتكلم، لاحتمال أن يعتمد المتكلم في عدم إرادته على قرينة كونه من الممتنعات العقلية فإنه فلا جرم بمجردها، ومدخلية القرينة فيه مما لا يمكن الجزم بأحد طرفيه ) أي النفي والإثبات كانت إفادتها اليقين في العقليات محل نظر ،وتأمل فإن قلت : إن كان صدق القائل مجزوماً به لزم منه الجزم بعدم المعارض في العقليات كما لزم منه ذلك في الشرعيات وإلا احتمل كلامه الكذب فيهما فلا فرق بينهما، قلت: المراد بالشرعيات أمور يجزم العقل بإمكانها ثبوتاً وانتفاء ولا طريق له إليها، والمراد بالعقليات ما ليس كذلك، وحينئذ جاز أن يكون من الممتنعات فلأجل هذا الاحتمال ربما لم يحصل الجزم بعدم المعارض العقلي للدليل النقلي في العقليات، وإن حصل الجزم به في الشرعيات وذلك بخلاف الأدلة العقلية في العقليات، فإنها بمجردها أقوى القرائن
The Sixth Observation - Eighth Intention: Transmitted proofs, do they yield certainty? And the active participle and others, for their usage in that time is known for what is intended from them in our time, and likewise the nominative of the subject and the accusative of the object and the genitive of the annexed, which their meanings are known with certainty. So when to such words are added observed contextual clues or those transmitted successively, knowledge of the linguistic meaning and intention is realized, and those nine possibilities are negated. As for the absence of rational contradiction, it is known from the truthfulness of the speaker, for if the meaning is determined and it is intended by him, then if there were a rational contradiction, his falsehood would follow. (Yes, in their yielding certainty in rational matters there is consideration, because it) i.e., that they yield certainty (is based on whether it is attained through them alone, i.e., through transmitted proofs themselves and reflection in them and the truthfulness of their speaker) (certainty of the absence of rational contradiction) and (whether the contextual clue) which is observed or transmitted successively (has an entry in that) i.e., certainty of the absence of rational contradiction (and these two) i.e., attainment of that certainty... (He said: 'to such words' i.e., the words whose usage in their understood meanings from the perspective of their essences and forms is known with certainty. He said: 'observed or transmitted successively contextual clues indicate the negation of those possibilities.' He said: 'knowledge of the linguistic meaning is realized' i.e., the linguistic meanings of those words and their intention with respect to them, not their intention with respect to the speaker. He said: 'for if the meaning is determined because the word is used in it with certainty.' He said: 'and it is intended by him' i.e., it is determined to be intended by the speaker by means of the observed or successively transmitted contextual clues indicating the negation of the mentioned possibilities, and it being legal i.e., derived from the address of the Lawgiver, since if it were not intended by him with the absence of a contextual clue indicating non-intention, that would be misguidance not guidance. He said: 'because it is based on whether etc.' i.e., based on the answer to this question, for if the attainment of certainty of the absence of contradiction through transmitted proofs alone and the truthfulness of their speaker without the contextual clue having an entry in that, then they would yield certainty in rational matters as well due to the commonality of the cause; but if the contextual clue has an entry in attaining certainty of the absence of contradiction, then they would not yield certainty in rational matters due to the non-existence of that contextual clue in them, unlike legal matters. And the summary of the objection is that this distinction is theoretical because the basis of the mentioned certainty is the speaker's truthfulness; if it is certain, then certainty of the absence of contradiction is attained in both, otherwise not. And the summary of the answer is the clarification of the distinction: that by legal matters is meant what is not grasped without it, so when the transmitted proof occurs in what is legal and there is an observed or successively transmitted contextual clue negating those possibilities, certainty is attained that its meaning is intended by the speaker with certainty, and certainty is attained of the absence of contradiction because the ruling is legal, the intellect has no way to affirm or negate it; so if the truthful speaker reports one of them with speech that does not admit otherwise, it is known with certainty that the other is negated, otherwise his falsehood would follow. Unlike the transmitted proof that occurs in what is rational, i.e., what the intellect has a way to affirm or negate, for it may be among the impossible things. So the observed and successively transmitted contextual clues indicating the negation of those possibilities do not yield certainty that its meaning is intended by the speaker, due to the possibility that the speaker relies in his non-intention on the contextual clue of its being among the rational impossibilities. Therefore, inevitably, through them alone and the entry of the contextual clue in that, it is not possible to be certain of one of the two sides (i.e., negation and affirmation). Their yielding certainty in rational matters is a matter of consideration and reflection. So if you say: If the truthfulness of the speaker is certain, then certainty of the absence of contradiction in rational matters follows from it, just as it follows in legal matters; otherwise, his speech may be false in both, so there is no difference between them. I say: By legal matters is meant matters that the intellect judges as possible in existence and non-existence, and it has no way to them; and by rational matters is meant what is not like that. Then it is possible that it is among the impossibilities, and due to this possibility, certainty of the absence of rational contradiction may not be attained for the transmitted proof in rational matters, although certainty is attained in legal matters. And that is unlike rational proofs in rational matters, for they by themselves are the strongest contextual clues.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument about whether transmitted (naqli) proofs can yield certainty (yaqin) in rational (aqli) matters. The author begins by stating the issue and then explains how in legal (shar'i) matters transmitted proofs do yield certainty because we know with certainty the meanings of words and their usage, and when observed or successively transmitted contextual clues (qara'in) are present, they negate the nine possibilities (such as metaphor, abrogation, etc.). Then, regarding the absence of rational contradiction, this is known from the truthfulness of the speaker: if the meaning is determined and intended, any rational contradiction would imply falsehood. The author then notes that the question of whether transmitted proofs yield certainty in rational matters depends on two sub-issues: (1) whether the certainty of no rational contradiction is attained solely through the transmitted proofs and the speaker's truthfulness, and (2) whether contextual clues play a role in that certainty. If the first is true, then transmitted proofs would yield certainty in aqli matters too; but if contextual clues are necessary, then they do not because such contextual clues are lacking in aqli matters. The author then presents an objection: since the certainty of no contradiction depends on the speaker's truthfulness, if that truthfulness is certain, then it should work equally in both types of matters. The answer is that legal matters are defined as matters that the intellect deems possible in themselves but has no way to determine them, so when a truthful speaker reports one possible option with unambiguous speech, the other option is known to be false. In contrast, rational matters are those the intellect can in principle affirm or negate, so there remains a possibility that the reported content is impossible; hence the contextual clues cannot guarantee intention. Therefore, transmitted proofs in aqli matters do not yield certainty, unlike rational proofs themselves which are the strongest contextual clues. The argument carefully distinguishes the epistemic role of possibility and the scope of reason.

Key Terms
الدلائل النقلية (transmitted proofs)
اليقين (certainty)
القرائن (contextual clues)
المعارض العقلي (rational contradiction)
الشرعيات (legal matters)
العقليات (rational matters)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if the speaker is truthful, transmitted proofs should yield certainty in rational matters just as in legal matters.
Correction: The author demonstrates that the difference lies in the nature of the content: legal matters are beyond reason's determination, while rational matters are within reason's scope, allowing for the possibility of rational impossibility.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why do transmitted proofs fail to yield certainty in rational matters?
A) Because the speaker might be lying.
B) Because contextual clues are always absent.
C) Because the reported content could be rationally impossible, and contextual clues cannot override that possibility.
D) Because rational matters are always unclear.

Key Takeaway
Transmitted proofs do not yield certainty in rational matters because the content may be rationally impossible, preventing the contextual clues from guaranteeing the speaker's intention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a hadith that seems to conflict with a rational truth (e.g., anthropomorphic descriptions of Allah), do you instinctively prioritize the transmitted text or the rational proof? How does this lesson challenge your default approach? Will you now pause to ask: 'Is this a rational matter where reason can judge possibility?'
?
Have you ever felt confident in a theological stance simply because you found a verse or hadith for it, without considering whether the matter is rational or legal? Reflect on one example. What would it mean to you to seek rational proofs for rational matters as this lesson insists?
?
The passage warns that without contextual clues, ambiguous divine speech would be misleading (إضلال). When you read ambiguous verses about qadr or divine attributes, do you rely on the assumption that God intended the apparent meaning? How does knowing that contextual clues are needed change your humility before the text? What step can you take today to better understand those clues?
